CHAPTER II VARá¹†A

The number of works dealing with the origin and characteristics of the caste system in India is legion. Most of them, however, concern themselves with the detailed {{des cription|description}} of the bewildering variety of castes and subdivisions of castes in modern times and their present religious and social customs and usages. The origin of caste has given rise to great speculation and several schools of thought have arisen. Generally individual authors lay undue emphasis on one element or attach far too much importance to one point in tracing the origin of the caste system and its ramifications, such as race (Risley), tribe (Ibbetson), occupation (Nesfield). The study of the origin and development of caste in India is one of deep and absorbing interest to all students of sociology. A complete and critical examination of the several theories of caste advanced by distinguished authors and a detailed description of the hundreds of castes and {{suboastes|subcastes}} now found in India is far beyond the scope of the present work. For those who want to make a thorough study of the most important works on caste a modest list is given in the footnote below.1

The caste system has been highly eulogised and also most severely condemned by Western writers. Sidney Low in his ‘Vision of India’ (pp. 262-263, 2nd ed. of 1907) speaks of the beneficent aspect of the caste system in the following eloquent passage: ‘There is no doubt that it is the main cause of the fundamental stability and contentment by which Indian society has been braced up for centuries against the shocks of politics and the cataclysms of Nature. It provides every man with his place, his career, his {{ocoupation|occupation}}, his {{cirole|circle}} of friends. It makes him at the outset a member of a corporate body, it protects him through life from the canker of social jealousy and unfulfilled aspirations; it ensures him {{compa nionship|companionship}} and a sense of community with others in like case with himself. The caste organization is to the Hindu his club, his trade union, his benefit society, his philanthropic society. There are no work-houses in India and none are as yet needed.’ Abbe Dubois, who wrote about 130 years ago after being in close touch with Hindus of all castes for 15 years as a missionary, remarks (in his work on the character, manners and customs of the people of India, translated into English and published in London in 1817) ‘I consider the institution of castes among the Hindu nations as the happiest effort of their legislation; and I am well convinced that, if the people of India never sunk into a state of barbarism, and if, when almost all Europe was plunged in that dreary gulf, India kept up her head, preserved and extended the sciences, the arts and civilization, it is wholly to the distinction of castes that she is indebted for that high celebrity’ (p. 14) and he devotes several pages to the justification of this remark. Maine in his ‘Ancient [[21]] Law’ (new edition of 1930 p. 17) characterises it as ’the most disastrous and blighting of all human institutions.’ Sherring in ‘Hindu tribes and castes’ vol. III p. 293 says it is ’the most baneful, hard-hearted and cruel social system that could possibly be invented for damning the human race’. On the other hand Meredith Townsend in ‘Europe and Asia’ (edition of 1901 p. 72) wrote ‘I firmly believe caste to be a marvellous discovery, a form of socialism which through ages protected Hindu Society from anarchy and from the worst evils of industrial and competitive lifeâ€”it is an automatic poor law to begin with and the strongest form known of Trades Union’. There are others,2 though their number is small, that believe

[[22]] that the caste system was an invention, an artificial product, due to the machinations of crafty brÄhmaá¹‡as. Every great institution has its extremes of good or evil. This work will endeavour to steer clear of downright and hypercritical condemnation of the caste system due to relying on modern {{stand points|standpoints}} and conditions of society and unthinking adulation thereof. It will try to present and balance facts and though it cannot help passing judgments it will leave the reader free to judge for himself as far as possible. In the present work the discussion will be mostly confined to the evidence of Sanskrit literary monuments, ancient and medieval. An attempt will be made to trace the origin of caste from Vedic times, to exhibit theories of the DharmasÅ«trakÄras, other smá¹›tikÄras and {{oom mentators|commentators}} on the subject and to describe the peculiar ceremonies, privileges, duties and responsibilities of Hindu castes as gathered from these works in Sanskrit. To discuss the feasibility or desirability of totally destroying the caste system or the ways and means of attaining that end is deemed to be outside the legitimate limits of this volume. It may, however, be expressly stated as the author’s opinion, in order to avoid misunderstandings or fruitless speculations about his personal views, that he does not think that the caste system was an artificial product due to the intrigues, greed and cunning of brÄhmaá¹‡as, nor does he hold that it is feasible to destroy the whole edifice of the caste system in the near future. In the cities we may find some people taking their food together but the real India is in the villages, where in spite of the loud denunciations of reformers for a hundred years, the restrictions on taking food and inter-caste marriages are almost as rigid as they once were. Our efforts must be {{direoted|directed}} to wide and rapid spread of literacy among the village people, the diffusion of the idea of one people and one nationality and gradual fusion of small subcastes into larger similar units. We in India have no doubt reached a critical stage in our history when old ideals, institutions and habits are being shattered by the impact of new ideas and by the onrush of world forces. We have to decide whether we shall make or be able to make a clean sweep of all old ideals and institutions as so much debris and rubbish or whether while keeping the old ideals and some of the old institutions as foundations we shall build up a new social order and create and foster new habits of thought and action. It is beyond the scope of the present work to write more on this point.

A sort of caste system based on birth and occupation did prevail in many countries in ancient times as in Persia, Rome and Japan. But in all these countries it hardly ever made any near approach in rigour and complexity to the caste system that we have in India and instead of ramifying into divisions and subdivisions, it dwindled and disappeared in the course of time. {{Nounanimity|No unanimity}} seems possible as to the several causes and {{ciroum stanoes|circumstances}} which led in India alone to the evolution of the stupendous structure of caste. Not only is it impossible to hold that the origin of the modern complexity of the caste system is to be traced back to one single cause, but it is difficult to accept that even all the origins that have been postulated by the several authors can {{adoquately|adequately}} and satisfactorily explain the modern caste system.

In most of the works on the castes in India a few features are pointed out as the characteristics of the caste system and as common to all castes and {{sub-oestes|sub-castes}}. They are: (1) heredity i. e. in theory a man is assigned to a particular caste by birth in that caste; (2) endogamy and exogamy i. e. restriction as to marrying in the same caste and not marrying {{cortain|certain}} relatives or other persons, though of the same caste; (3) {{restrio tions|restrictions}} as to food (i. e. what food and water may be taken or not taken and from whom); (4) occupation (i. e. members of most castes follow certain occupations and no others); (5) {{grade tion|gradation}} of castes, some being at the top in the social scale and others being deemed to be so low that they are untouchable. Some authors3 like Senart add another characteristic, viz the caste council with its {{ohiof|chief}} having in meeting assembled among other matters the power to regulate the conduct of its members, to impose the penalties of fine or {{excommunioation|excommunication}} for lapses. It may be said at once that this last is a feature

[[23]] that is not found among most of the brÄhmaá¹‡a and ká¹£atriya castes even in modern times and is not dealt with by {{dharma sÄstra|dharmaÅ›Ästra}} works. Endogamy is now the most prominent characteristic of caste and so is the theory that it is by birth. The other three are more or less fluctuating from province to {{pro vince|province}} and age to age. In this work the first five characteristics of the caste system set out above will be subjected to a close critical examination on the basis of the Vedic and {{dharma sÄstra|dharmaÅ›Ästra}} material. We must also remember that the attributes of caste have not been the same throughout the ages. There is great difference between the popular conceptions of modern caste and the conceptions about it embodied in the ancient and medieval dharmaÅ›Ästra works. In the twentieth century caste in India is a matter of marriage and to a much lesser extent of food and drink. As to {{avoca tions|avocations}} any one can at present follow any profession without fear of loss of caste excepting a few believed to be very impure and very degrading ones (like those of sweepers, butchers, tanners &c.). It is also not possible even now for any one to be generally accepted as a priest, unless he is or claims to be a brÄhmaá¹‡a. The old barriers that separated one caste or subcaste from another have been greatly shaken by the influx of modern ideas and the exigencies of the times and one may hope that in a few decades more caste will remain as a purely social institution regulating marriages and to a lesser extent {{oommen. sality|commensality}} (and not a religious one). Western scholars, in spite of their most commendable patience and industry, often present, through ignorance or lack of first-hand knowledge, the number of castes as larger than what it actually is. For example, Sherring (vol. II. Introduction pp. XXII-XLVI) gives an {{alpbabetical|alphabetical}} list of brÄhmaá¹‡ical tribes and remarks (p. XLVII) ‘Hundreds of these tribes, if not at enmity with one another, cherish mutual distrust and antipathy to such a degree that they are socially separated from one another as far as it is possible for them to beâ€”as much as brÄhmaá¹‡as are from the lowest {{outoastes|outcastes}}â€”neither eating nor drinking together nor intermarrying’. The list he gives is most misleading. To take only a few examples, he enumerates Athavle, Achwal, {{Abhyan kara|Abhyankar}}, Apte, Agashe, Bhanu, Bivalkar, {{Badye|Badhe}}, Bhide, Bhagvat, Bhuskute, Bhat, Bodas as separate tribes; but it is well-known to people in Western India that these are the surnames (not subcastes) of the Konkanastha or {{Citpavana|CitpÄvana}} brÄhmaá¹‡as, who not only interdine, but also inter-marry among themselves, provided there is no bar on the ground of sameness of {{gotrn|gotra}} and pravara.

The word vará¹‡a means ‘colour’ or ’light’ in most passages of the {{Roveda|á¹šgveda}} (e. g. I. 73.7, II. 3.5, IX. 97.15, IX. 104.4, IX. 105.4, X. 124.7). But in some verses of the á¹šgveda4 the word vará¹‡a is associated with groups of people having a skin of a dark or fair colour. For example, we read in á¹šg. II. 12.4 ‘(Indra) who placed low the dÄsa colour in a cave (or darkness)’; in á¹šg. I. 179,6 ’the fierce sage (Agastya) cherished both vará¹‡as’; in á¹šg. IX. 71.2 ’like one (a fighter) who strikes the people he (Soma) who is powerful goes giving out frequent roars; he exposes the Asura colour’; in á¹šg. I. 130.8 ‘Indra helped in battles the Ä€rya sacrificer… Indra punished for the sake of Manu (the dÄsas) who do not observe the ordinances and subdued (or killed) the dark skin’; ‘Indra having killed the dasyus protected the Ärya vará¹‡a (á¹šg. III. 34.9); “You (Indra) subdued for RjiÅ›van, the son of Vidathin, Pipru and powerful Má¹›gaya; you mowed down fifty thousand dark (men), you shattered cities as old age does shatter good looks’ á¹šg. IV. 16.13; ‘Somas, which strike away the dark skin’ á¹šg. IX. 41.1. In á¹šg. I. 158.5 a certain dÄsa is called Traitana which name has a Persian ring about it; In á¹šg. I. 104.2 vará¹‡a seems to be placed in opposition to dÄsa. These passages make it clear that the Ä€ryas and dÄsas were two opposing camps and both were designated vará¹‡as on account of the colour of their skins. The {{Tai. Br.|TaittirÄ«ya BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} (Tai. Br.) I. 2. 6 (with reference to the MahÄvrata in which there was a mock fight between a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a Å›Å«dra) says ’that the brÄhmaá¹‡a is the divine vará¹‡a, and Å›Å«dra is the Äsurya vará¹‡a’.5 If we can interpret the á¹šgveda verse by the help of the Tai. Br., then in á¹šg. IX. 71.2, the words ‘Äsuryam vará¹‡am’ mean ‘Å›Å«dra tribe’. There is no doubt that the word asura when applied to gods like {{Varupa|Varuá¹‡a}} has another meaning also in the á¹šg. In numerous places in the á¹šgveda the antagonism [[25]]

between the Äryas and dÄsas or dasyus is emphasized and prayers are offered to Indra and other gods for having subdued or for subduing the dÄsa in favour of the Ä€rya. á¹šg. I. 51.8; I. 103.3; I. 117.21; II. 11.2, 4, 18, 19; III. 29.9; V. 70.3; VII. 5.6; IX. 88.4; VI. 18.3; VI. 25.2. In á¹šg. I. 51.8 Indra is requested to mark who are Äryas and who are dasyus. This does not mean that there was difference between the two in bodily appearance only; on the contrary the antithesis between the Ärya who is referred to as ‘{{barhismat|barhiá¹£mat}}’ and the dasyu who is styled ‘avrata’ clearly shows that the emphasis was rather on the difference of their cults. That dasyu and dÄsa are identical in meaning follows from the same epithets being applied to both and from the fact that dasyu and dÄsa occur in the same verses as applying to the same enemy.6 In á¹šg. X. 22.8 dasyu and dÄsa are used in the same verse as applicable to the same enemy. In á¹šg. X. 99.6 and 8 Indra is represented as killing both dÄsa and dasyu respectively. The dasyus are described as ‘avrata’ (not obeying the ordinances of the gods) in á¹šg. I. 51.8, I. 175.3, VI. 14.3, ‘akratu’ (who perform no sacrifice) in VII. 6.3,7 ‘{{má¹­dhravacaá¸¥|má¹›dhravÄcaá¸¥}}’ (whose speech is indistinct or soft) in VII. 6.3 and V. 29.10, ‘anÄsaá¸¥’ (snub-nosed or dumb) in á¹šg. V. 29.10. It appears that dÄsa and dasyu are synonyms and were sometimes styled asuras. For example, Åšambara is called dasyu and dÄsa in á¹šgveda VI. 31.4 and dÄsa in á¹šg. VI. 26.5 and is also associated with asuras like Pipru in VI. 18.8; Pipru is spoken of as a dÄsa in á¹šg. VIII. 32.2 and as an asura in X. 138.3. Varcin is styled dÄsa in á¹šg. IV. 30.15 and VI. 47.21 and asura in VII. 99.5. In {{Tai. S.|TaittirÄ«ya Saá¹ƒhitÄ}}8 (IV. 3.11.3) also it seems that they are held to be identical. The enmity between dÄsa and Ärya is breathed in such verses as the following: á¹šg. II. 11.4 ‘vanquish the tribe of dÄsas by the sun (i. e. by the help of a brilliant weapon); á¹šg. I. 174.7 ‘You made the earth a pillow for the dÄsa (i. e. you laid him low on the ground)’; á¹šg. III. 12.6 ‘Oh Indra and Agni, by one effort together you shook ninety [[26]]

cities that had {{dÄgas|dÄsas}} as overlords’. It is not possible to say that dÄsas or dasyus were some Aryan tribes that had fallen from the worship or culture of the Aryan singers of Vedic {{hyians|hymns}}. In many places the sage refers to the conquest for him by Indra and other gods of dÄsas as well as Aryan foes. For example, ‘Protectors of the good! you (two) killed Aryan foes and dÄsa foes’ á¹šg. VI. 60.6; ‘Oh Indra and Varuá¹‡a! you killed dÄsa foes and also Ä€rya foes and helped SudÄs with your protection’ á¹šg. VII.83.1. Vide also á¹šg. VI. 22.10, X. 69.6, X. 33.1, X. 102.3. This shows that though the Äryans had become divided and fought among themselves, they kept Äryas and dÄsas quite distinct. The foregoing shows that in the times of the á¹šgveda there were two antagonistic camps, of the Äryas and dÄsas or dasyus, they differed in the colour of their skins and also in worship, speech and bodily appearance. Therefore, in the earliest period we find the word vará¹‡a associated only with dÄsa and with Ärya. Though the words brÄhmaá¹‡a and ká¹£atriya occur frequently in the á¹šgveda, the word vará¹‡a is not used in connection with them. Even in the {{Puruá¹£asÄkta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} (á¹šgveda X. 90) where the words brÄhmaá¹‡a, rÄjanya, vaiÅ›ya and Å›Å«dra occur the word vará¹‡a is not used. Hence, one may reasonably say that the only water-tight groups that are positively or expressly vouchsafed by the á¹šgveda are Ärya and dÄsa or dasyu. It is often argued that as the word brÄhmaá¹‡a denotes a caste in later literature, in the á¹šgveda also it must be presumed to have the same meaning. But this begs the whole question. No one denies that brÄhmaá¹‡a denotes a caste in later literature. But whether it has the same sense in the á¹šgveda must be determined on the materials furnished by the á¹šgveda itself. Some rely on the word ‘{{bra hmaputra|brahmaputra}}’ in á¹šg. II. 43.2 as showing that a brÄhmaá¹‡a became so only by birth in the á¹šgveda. But the verse begins by saying that ‘you sing a SÄman like the UdgÄtá¹› priest’ and ‘brahmaputra’ must mean a á¹›tvij whose duty it was to recite Å›astras (the {{BrahmapÄochamsin|BrÄhmaá¹‡Äcchaá¹ƒsin}} as {{SÄyana|SÄyaá¹‡a}} explains). It is generally conceded that the {{Puruá¹£agÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} is a much later hymn than most of the hymns of the á¹šgveda. In the whole of the á¹šgveda the words vaiÅ›ya and Å›Å«dra do not occur except in the Puruá¹£asÅ«kta, though both of them occur in the {{Atharyaveda|Atharvaveda}} (V. 17.9 for vaiÅ›ya and IV. 20. 4 and 8 for Å›Å«dra and Ärya) and very frequently in the Tai. S. Besides we cannot forget that the final redaction of the á¹šgveda must be held to have been separated from the composition of the individual hymns by several hundred years (if not more) and that even if it be conceded [[27]] that at the time when the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} was composed, the four vará¹‡as had been constituted and had become castes, yet the same cannot be affirmed for the time of the original composition of the other {{byrns|hymns}}. The word brÄhmaá¹‡a occurs several times in the á¹šgveda:9 ‘Oh brÄhmaá¹‡as, Oh pitá¹›s fond of soma! May the sinless {{DyÄvÄprthivi|DyÄvÄpá¹›thivÄ«}} (Heaven and Earth) tend to our welfare’; ‘Like brÄhmaá¹‡as in the AtirÄtra where soma is to be drunk, uttering (words) round a lake full of water you have, Oh frogs, gathered together on that day of the year on which the rains begin’ (á¹šg. VII. 103.7);10 ‘The {{brahmanan|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, who drink soma, reciting prayers of the yearly sacrifice, have sent forth their speech’ (á¹šg. VII. 103. 8). In this verse brÄhmaá¹‡as are expressly said to be getting ‘brahma’ ready. ‘May {{Agoi|Agni}} who devours every thing make that (dead body) free from disease and (may) soma also (do the same) who entered into the brÄhmaá¹‡as’ (á¹šg. X. 16. 6). “When the brÄhmaá¹‡as worship together as friends in hymns (lit. speed of the mind) that are fabricated from their hearts’ (á¹šg. X. 71. 8). In á¹šg. VI. 75. 10 brÄhmaá¹‡as are invoked for welfare along with pitá¹›s. This shows that the brÄhmaá¹‡as were highly venerated. The other verses establish that they were the reciters of hymns (brahma) and drank soma. In á¹šg. VIII. 35. 16-18 we read ‘You ({{As vins|AÅ›vins}}) urge on (or inspire) brahma, you urge on our thoughts (or actions), you kill the evil spirits and subdue diseases; (17) you urge ká¹£atra (valour) and also men, you kill evil spirits (same as 16); (18) you urge on the cows and also the ViÅ› (the rest is same as 16).’ Here it seems that the groups of people (viz. those who think and make songs, those who show valour and lead men, and those common people who tend cattle) are clearly meant. These verses may be conceded as pointing to the existence of three groups (brÄhmaá¹‡as, ká¹£atriyas and {{vitaá¸¥|viÅ›aá¸¥}}) but there is nothing in them to show that these three had {{crystal. lised|crystallised}} into somewhat like the castes of later times. In á¹šg. VII. 33. 11 {{Vasiá¹£tha|Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} is addressed as brahman, but that does not mean that he was a brÄhmaá¹‡a (by birth), as he is said to have been born of {{UrvaÅ›i|UrvaÅ›Ä«}} from Mitra and Varuá¹‡a. Similarly, in [[28]]

á¹šg. IX. 96. 6 (BrahmÄ devÄnÄm) the word brahmÄ does not certainly mean ‘brÄhmaá¹‡a by birth’, nor does ‘viprÄá¹‡Äm’ mean “brÄhmanas by birth’. In that verse one who is super-eminent among a group is specified, just as the buffalo among animals, the hawk among carnivorous birds &c. In á¹šg. VIII. 33. 19 ’look down and not up: bring your feet close together; may thy {{kaÅ›aplakas|kaÅ›aplakas}} (legs?) be not seen, for though a brahmÄ, thou wert born a woman,’ it is impossible to hold that the last words mean ’thou wert a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman’. If it is only intended to refer to the fact that she is a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman, there is no reason why the Perfect tense (babhÅ«vitha) is used and not the present. Here ‘brahmÄ’ most probably means ‘a priest of that name’, as that is the meaning in á¹šg. II. 1. 2 (brahmÄ casi gá¹›hapatisca no dame). The word ‘brahmajÄyÄ’ in á¹šg. X. 109.2, 3, 6 and 7 does not mean the wife of a brÄhmaá¹‡a by birth but rather ‘wife of {{Bphaspati|Bá¹›haspati}}’. The whole hymn is obscure and more or less enigmatic or allegorical. In the Aitareya-brÄhmaá¹‡a11 35. 2-4 it is said that soma is the food of brÄhmaá¹‡as and that a ká¹£atriya was to press the tendrils of the Nyagrodha tree and the fruits of Udumbara, {{Asvattha|AÅ›vattha}} and {{Plakga|Plaká¹£a}} and drink the juice so pressed instead of soma. It appears, therefore, that the brÄhmaá¹‡as were a distinct group even in the earliest period of the á¹šgveda. Whether they were hereditary is certainly not clear; nor is there anything to show that there were restrictions as regards partaking of food from persons other than brÄhmaá¹‡as or as to marriage. That brÄhmaá¹‡as in the á¹šgveda were a class by themselves may be conceded, but whether they had become a caste by birth is a matter of opinion dependent on the connotation given to the word caste. Dr. Ghurye (‘Caste and race in India’ p. 42) thinks, probably following the Vedic Index (vol. I on Ká¹£atriya), that the reference in á¹šg. X. 71. 9 to a false claim for being regarded as a brÄhmaá¹‡a points to the {{nonolusion|conclusion}} that brÄhmaá¹‡as had become a caste. The verse literally translated means ’these (persons) who do not move below nor beyond, who are neither brÄhmaá¹‡as, nor engaged in pressing soma – they [[29]]

being ignorant and having resorted to speech in sinful (or coarse) language take to ploughshares and engage in {{agri. oultural|agricultural}} operations’. It is difficult to see how there is here any false claim to be regarded as a brÄhmaá¹‡a. This verse means apparently that those who are not composers of prayers or drinkers of soma (because they are ignorant) are men of low speech and have to turn to agriculture. Even in the days of the dharma-sÅ«tras restrictions as to food and marriage for brÄhmaá¹‡as were not at all as rigid as they became in medieval and modern times; but even when these restrictions were not rigid it was clearly laid down that a brÄhmaá¹‡a is so by birth alone. The word ‘brahma’ generally means in the á¹šgveda ‘prayer’ or ‘hymn’. Vide12 á¹šg. IV. 6.11, VI. 52.2, X. 105.8, X. 141.5 (‘Oh Agni, make our prayer and sacrifice prosper by your flames’). á¹šg. III. 53.12 is ’this brahma (prayer or spiritual power) of ViÅ›vÄmitra protects the Bharata people’. In the Atharvaveda II. 15.4 (as brahma and ká¹£atra entertain no fear, nor are they harmed) brahma seems to mean the class of ‘brÄhmaá¹‡as’. The transition of meaning from ‘brahma’ (prayer) to ‘brahma’ meaning the class of those who composed or recited prayers is natural and easy. In the á¹šgveda I. 157.2 both brahma and ká¹£atra occur13 in the same verse where they probably mean ‘prayer’ and ‘valour’ respectively. In the Atharvaveda III. 19.1 both words occur and probably mean the same thing as in á¹šg. I. 157.2. In some Vedic works brahma and ká¹£atra stand collectively for brÄhmaá¹‡as14 and ká¹£atriyas (e. g. {{Tai. Br.|TaittirÄ«ya BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} II. 7.18, {{Bá¹›. Up.|Bá¹›hadÄraá¹‡yaka Upaniá¹£ad}} I. 4.11, {{Kathopaniá¹£ad|Kaá¹­hopaniá¹£ad}} I. 2. 24). The word ká¹£atriya is very frequently applied as an epithet to several gods; e. g. á¹šg. VII. 64. 2 and VIII. 25. 8 (in both to Mitra and Varuá¹‡a), á¹šg. VIII. 67. 1 (to Ä€dityas), á¹šg. X. 66.8 (to gods in general). In some verses ká¹£atriya means ‘a king or a nobleman’; e.g. á¹šg. IV. 42.1 ’the kingdom on both sides (heaven and earth) belongs to me, who am a ká¹£atriya and who holds sway over all living persons, so that all the immortals (gods) are ours (on my side)’;15 á¹šg. X. 109.3 [[30]]

(= Atharva V. 17.3) ’the domain of the ká¹£atriya has been protected (from the sight of enemies)’. The word ‘rÄjanya’ occurs in the á¹šgveda only in the Puruá¹£asÅ«kta. It occurs in the sense of ká¹£atriya in the Atharvaveda V. 17.9. The same remark applies to the word ká¹£atriya as to brÄhmaá¹‡a. It is difficult to say whether ká¹£atriyas were so by birth in the times of the hymns of the á¹šg. or were only a class more or less fluid. We find that the á¹šg. speaks of DevÄpi as the purohita of ÅšÄntanu who became a king. The story is that both were sons of á¹šá¹£á¹­iá¹£ena and that ÅšÄntanu, though a younger brother, became king as DevÄpi was not willing to be a king.16 The result was a famine due to ÅšÄntanu’s transgression and so DevÄpi performed a sacrifice to induce rainfall. This shows that out of two brothers one became a king and the other a purohita. So kings and purohitas did not depend on birth. In á¹šg. IX. 112, 3 a poet exclaims ‘I am a reciter of hymns my father is a physician and my mother grinds (corn) with stones. We desire to obtain wealth in various actions’.17 In á¹šg. III. 44.5 the poet wistfully asks Indra ‘O, Indra, fond of soma, would you make me the protector of people, or would you make me a king, would you make me a sage, that has drunk of soma, would you impart to me endless wealth?’ This shows that the same man could be a á¹›á¹£i or a noble or a king. Dr. Ghurye (in ‘Caste and race in India’ p. 44) thinks that the ká¹£atriyas had become a compact body and he particularly relies on á¹šg. VII. 104. 13 (= Atharvaveda VIII. 4. 13) which is cited in the Vedic Index (vol. 1, p. 207) for the same purpose. That verse literally means ‘Soma does not urge on the crooked one, nor the ká¹£atriya who bears false (words). He strikes the raká¹£as, and strikes him who speaks falsely; both lie in the bonds of Indra’. The words ‘ká¹£atriyam mithuyÄ dhÄrayantam’ are explained by SÄyaá¹‡a as ‘Ká¹£atriya who bears false words’. The 2nd half is only an expansion of the first half and so ‘vá¹›jina’ corresponds to ‘raká¹£as’ and ‘asad vadantam’ is only a paraphrase of ‘mithuyÄ dhÄrayantam’. They may mean this that one who is a ká¹£atriya, but has no strength as a ká¹£atriya should have, is at [[31]]

the mercy of Indra. Unless we project our notions of the later state of society and the caste system when considering this verse, it is hardly possible to hold that this verse indicates that it refers to persons making a false claim for entrance into a compact body of ká¹£atriyas by birth. Dr. Ghurye also says (p. 44) ‘The 2nd order in society, the ká¹£atriya, is known in the earlier portions of the á¹šgveda as rÄjanya.’ I have not been able to find the word rÄjanya in the á¹šgveda any where except in the Puruá¹£a-sÅ«kta. In the Aitareya brÄhmaá¹‡a (chap. 34. 2) the word ‘rÄjanya’ stands for a member of the 2nd class in society, while ká¹£atriya means a king of whom land (for sacrifice to gods) is asked for by brÄhmaá¹‡a, rÄjanya or vaiÅ›ya.

Though the word vaiÅ›ya occurs in the á¹šgveda only in the Puruá¹£asÅ«kta, the word ‘viÅ›’ is very frequently employed in it. It generally means ‘people’ or ‘group of people’. In a large number of cases we have the words mÄnuá¹£Ä«r-viÅ›aá¸¥ or mÄnuá¹£iá¹£u viká¹£u or mÄnuá¹£iá¹‡Äm viÅ›Äm, e.g. á¹šg. In some places we have ‘dÄsir viÅ›aá¸¥’ (the dÄsa hordes) e. g. IV. 28-4, VI. 25.2. In18 á¹šg. In á¹šg. V. 32. 11 Indra is styled ‘pÄÃ±cajanya’ (favourable to the five people) and in IX. 66. 20 Agni has the appellation paÃ±cajanyaá¸¥ purohitaá¸¥ (the purohita of the five peoples). The word ‘dÄsa’ in later literature means ‘a serf or a slave.’ It follows that the dÄsa tribes that we see opposed to the Ä€ryas in the á¹šgveda were gradually vanquished and were then made to serve the Ä€ryas. In the Manusmá¹›ti (VIII. 413) the Å›Å«dra is said to have been created by God for service (dÄsya) of the brÄhmaá¹‡a.21 We find in the Tai. S., the Tai. Therefore it is reasonable to infer that the dÄsas or dasyus conquered by the Ä€ryas were gradually transformed into the Å›Å«dras. From being enemies they were brought into friendly relations and given a very subordinate position. Traces are visible even in the á¹šgveda that friendly relations had begun to be established between certain dÄsas and the priests. For example in á¹šg. In the very next verse the sun and the moon are said to have been born from the eye and mind of the Puruá¹£a. This shows that the composer of the hymn regarded the division of society into four classes to be very ancient and to be as natural and God-ordained as the sun and the moon.

Varna: brÄhmaá¹‡as

The position of the three vará¹‡as inter se (called collectively Ärya) now requires consideration. It is clear that the Saá¹ƒhitÄs other than the á¹šgveda and BrÄhmaá¹‡a works show that the three classes of brÄhmaá¹‡as, ká¹£atriyas and vaiÅ›yas had become differentiated and their privileges, duties and liabilities had become more or less fixed in those times. [[36]]

In á¹šg. IV. 50.8 we read that ‘king alone who places brahma first (i. e. honours him) dwells happy in his house, for him the earth always remains prosperous and to him all the people (or kingdoms) bow down of their own accord’. ‘BrÄhmaá¹‡as35 are gods that are directly seen’ (Tai. S. I. 7.3.1); ’there are two kinds of gods; for indeed the gods are gods and the brÄhmaá¹‡as who have studied and teach the sacred lore are the human gods’ Åšat. Br. (S. B. In the Atharvaveda V. 17. 19 there is an assertion of the pre-eminence of brÄhmaá¹‡as and the consequences of harming them or their cows. ‘Therefore the brÄhmaá¹‡a is the foremost’ (Tai. S. II. 6.2.5, V.2.7.1). ‘Therefore the brÄhmaá¹‡a shows his might by his mouth, since he was created from the mouth’ (TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br. XI. 1. 2).36 In the Ait. Br. (33. 4) Varuá¹‡a, when he was told that a brÄhmaá¹‡a boy would be offered in place of the son of the king HariÅ›candra, is made to say ‘a brÄhmaá¹‡a is indeed preferable to a ká¹£atriya’. The mere fact of birth as brÄhmaá¹‡a’s son is represented here as giving to the boy pre-eminence over a king’s son. On the other hand the Åšat. Br. it is said that playing on the vÄ«á¹‡Ä (in the AÅ›vamedha) is to be done by a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a rÄjanya (and not by two brÄhmaá¹‡as), since wealth does not find delight in the brÄhmaá¹‡a. The Åšatapatha BrÄhmaá¹‡a38 (XI. 5. 7.1) lays emphasis on the four peculiar attributes of brÄhmaá¹‡as viz. brÄhmaá¹‡ya (purity of parentage as a brÄhmaá¹‡a), pratirÅ«pacaryÄ (befitting deportment or conduct), yaÅ›as (glory) and lokapakti (the teaching or perfecting of people). “When the people are being perfected or taught by him, people endow him with four privileges viz. arcÄ (honour), dÄna (gifts), ajyeyatÄ (freedom [[38]]

from being harassed) and avadhyatÄ (freedom from being beaten). The Åšatapatha (V. 4. 6. 9) expressly mentions that brÄhmaá¹‡a, rÄjanya, vaiÅ›ya and Å›Å«dra are the four Vará¹‡as. Teaching had become so much associated with the brÄhmaá¹‡as that when the brÄhmaá¹‡a GÄrgya approached king AjÄtaÅ›atru for the knowledge of brahma, the latter replied “this is contrary to the natural order that a brÄhmaá¹‡a should approach a ká¹£atriya with the idea ‘he (ká¹£atriya) will propound to me brahma’”.39 In a separate section below all the privileges enjoyed by the brÄhmaá¹‡as will be set out at one place.

Varna: ká¹£atriyas

The position of ká¹£atriyas and their relation to the brÄhmaá¹‡as falls to be considered now. First of all with reference to the king several important passages may be cited. In some cases ‘rÄjan’ means only a ’noble’ or ‘chief’, as in á¹šg. X. 42. 10, X. 97. 6. In many passages ‘rÄjan’ means ‘king’. The government often seems to have been tribal, such tribes as Yadus, TurvaÅ›as, Druhyus, Anus and PÅ«rus (á¹šg. I. 108. 8) being frequently mentioned (vide á¹šg. The king was regarded as keeping the people within bounds.40 When a king was crowned, it was thought that ‘a ká¹£atriya was produced, a lord of all beings, the defender of brÄhmaá¹‡as (or of holy texts) and of dharma.’41 The Åšat. Br. says ‘for these two (Å›rotriya and king) are the upholders of the sacred law among men’ (S. B. ká¹£atriya came to occupy a very high position. The Åšatapatha (S. B. Even the gods required a purohita, as the Tai. S. II. 5. 1. 1. says ‘ViÅ›varÅ«pa son of Tvaá¹£á¹­á¹› was the purohita of gods.’44 Åšaá¹‡á¸a and Amarka were the purohitas of the Asuras (KÄá¹­haka S. (chap. 34.8) says45 ’the purohita is half the soul of the ká¹£atriya’ and contains an eulogy of purohita in the following words (40.1) ‘for indeed the gods do not eat the food of a king who has no purohita; therefore a king when about to offer a sacrifice should have a brÄhmaá¹‡a as his purohita with the idea ‘may the gods eat my food.’” The combination of the brÄhmaá¹‡a and the rÄjanya is said to be most desirable and that it is conducive to the eminence of both is declared in the Tai. (V. 1. 10. 3)46 ‘Therefore a brÄhmaá¹‡a who is supported by a rÄjanya is superior to another brÄhmaá¹‡a (not so supported), hence a rÄjanya who has a brÄhmaá¹‡a (to help him) is superior to another rÄjanya’. The Åšatapatha (V. 4. 4. 15) declares that ‘king indeed who is not powerful to the brÄhmaá¹‡as (i. e. who is humble before the brÄhmaá¹‡as) becomes more powerful than his foes.’47 It is not to be supposed that this attainment of the supreme position by brÄhmaá¹‡as was an affair of plain sailing. Sometimes ká¹£atriyas claimed higher position and also paid scant respect to brÄhmaá¹‡as. In the Åšatapatha we read ‘whence the brÄhmaá¹‡a is an object of respect after the king’ (S. B. therefore the brÄhmaá¹‡a sits down below the ká¹£atriya in the RÄjasÅ«ya’ (Åšatapatha 14. 4. 1. 23 = Bá¹›. Up. I. 4. 11). In the Atharvaveda several verses occur which declare the harm that results from disrespecting or injuring a brÄhmaá¹‡a or from robbing him of his cow. “He who regards brÄhmaá¹‡a as food drinks of the poison of TaimÄta; he who injures a brÄhmaá¹‡a, the relative of the gods, does not attain to the world of pitá¹›s (Atharvaveda V. 18. 4 and 13). The stories of king KÄrtavÄ«rya and ViÅ›vÄmitra who respectively carried off the cows of Jamadagni and Vasiá¹£á¹­ha narrated in the MahÄbhÄrata (ÅšÄnti 49 for KÄrtavÄ«rya; Ä€di. 175 for ViÅ›vÄmitra) and the PurÄá¹‡as show how several kings were high-handed and treated brÄhmaá¹‡as with no respect whatever. The Tai. said to have been born from á¹›k verses, they declare the Yajurveda as the origin of the ká¹£atriya, the SÄmaveda is indeed the source of brÄhmaá¹‡as.’52 The same BrÄhmaá¹‡a further says ’the viÅ› go away from (reside separately from) the brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas’. The TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br. says53 ‘Hence the vaiÅ›ya, though being eaten (i. e. subsisted upon) by others is not exhausted, since he was created from the prajanana (from the sexual parts of PrajÄpati); therefore he has numerous cattle, he has all the gods (as his patrons) and was produced with the JagatÄ« metre, his season is the rains, therefore he is to be eaten by the brÄhmaá¹‡a and the rÄjanya, since he was created as lower (than those two classes). The Åšat. Br. (S. B. These passages show that vaiÅ›yas were entitled to sacrifice, reared cattle, were far more numerous than the other two classes, they had to bear the brunt of taxation, they lived apart from brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas and were obedient to them.

The system of the four vará¹‡as had taken such deep roots in the period when the BrÄhmaá¹‡a works were composed, that we often meet with Vedic passages saying that it extended even to the gods, Agni and Bá¹›haspati being the brÄhmaá¹‡as among gods; Indra, Varuá¹‡a, Soma, Yama being the ká¹£atriyas; Vasus, Rudras, the ViÅ›ve Devas and Maruts being the viÅ›, and PÅ«á¹£an being the Å›Å«dra.55 Similarly it is said ’the brÄhmaá¹‡a is really spring, the ká¹£atriya summer, and the viÅ› are the rainy season.’ [[42]]

With reference to an iá¹£á¹­i offered to Rudra a Vedic text says ‘one should make a niá¹£Ädasthapati perform this iá¹£á¹­i.’ The PÅ«rvamÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ-sÅ«tra64 (VI. 1. 51-52) discusses the question whether this authorises a niá¹£Äda who is himself a chieftain or a chieftain (who is a member of the three higher vará¹‡as) of niá¹£Ädas. The Aitareya BrÄhmaá¹‡a68 narrates that ViÅ›vÄmitra cursed his senior fifty sons, when they did not agree to his proposal to treat Åšunaá¸¥Å›epa (DevarÄta) as his son, that they would associate with the lowest castes and that they became the Andhras, Puá¹‡á¸ras, Åšabaras, Pulindas and MÅ«tibas who are among the lowest of society and are mostly composed of dasyus. Manu further adds that the various castes that are outside the (influence of the) four vará¹‡as are all known as dasyus whether they speak the language of Mlecchas or of Ä€ryas.

One very important question is whether the theory of the four vará¹‡as with their peculiar privileges and duties described in the dharmasÅ«tras and other smá¹›tis was merely a theory even in the most ancient times. When the Puruá¹£asÅ«kta of the á¹šgveda speaks of BrÄhmaá¹‡a, RÄjanya, VaiÅ›ya and ÅšÅ«dra or when the Åšatapatha BrÄhmaá¹‡a expressly says that they are four Vará¹‡as, it appears to me that they speak of facts existing in their times and not merely of a theory which was to be striven for as an ideal. Smá¹›ti writers try to place all their dicta in the frame-work of the vará¹‡as because the four vará¹‡as and their duties and privileges had been more or less clearly defined in the times of the Vedas and BrÄhmaá¹‡as, which according to the authors of the smá¹›tis were Å›ruti, eternal and infallible. They tried to approximate the state of society existing in their times to the vará¹‡as which they held were of hoary antiquity. But there is nothing to show that the same difficulties were felt by the authors of the Vedic hymns and the BrÄhmaá¹‡as. The manner in which they refer to the privileges and disabilities of the several vará¹‡as have such a deep ring of actuality that one must concede that the vará¹‡as spoken of in them represented the [[48]]

real divisions of society at least to a very great extent, if not cent percent.

The preceding discussion renders the following propositions most probable:

(1) that in the earliest times about which we have literary records there were only two vará¹‡as, the Äryas and their opponents the dasyus or dÄsas; that the difference between the two was based on difference of colour and culture and was thus more or less racial and cultural;

(2) that centuries before the saá¹ƒhitÄ period closed the dasyus had been conquered and were given a position subservient to the Äryas;

(3) that the Å›Å«dras were the dasyus so subjugated and made subservient;

(4) that the spirit of exclusiveness and pride of superiority existing among the Ä€ryas with reference to dasyus soon extended to groups among the Äryas themselves;

(5) that by the time of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a Literature, brÄhmaá¹‡as (men supposed to be devoted to learning and priesthood), ká¹£atriyas (kings, noblemen and some warriors) and vaiÅ›yas (the artisans and common people) had become separated into groups more or less dependent on birth and that the brÄhmaá¹‡a70 had come to be regarded as superior to the ká¹£atriya by the fact of birth;

(6) that even such low castes as cÄá¹‡á¸Älas and paulkasas had been evolved long before the end of the Vedic period;

(7) that owing to cultural advance, division of labour arose and numerous arts and crafts had been developed and they were in process of contributing to the complexity of the system by creating numerous sub-castes based upon occupations;

(8) that besides the four vará¹‡as intermediate castes like the rathakÄra had been evolved;

(9) that there were certain non-Ä€ryan tribes which were supposed to have been originally ká¹£atriyas but fallen later on. [[49]]

The close of the Vedic period is here taken as being not later than about 1000 B. C. How much earlier it may be placed it is not possible to say.

They are arranged in the alphabetical order of Sanskrit (though transliterated). The meanings of most of them have been given above. Where the meaning is doubtful a question mark has been made.

Several centuries before the Christian era there were several castes. This follows not only from the dharmasÅ«tras but also from the ancient Buddhist works and from the meagre existing fragments of the work of Megasthenes on India. Though Megasthenes was confused in his statements about the caste system as prevalent in his day, some propositions are clearly deducible therefrom. He states (pp. 40 ff) that the whole population of India was divided into seven castes, (1) philosophers, (2) husbandmen, (3) neatherds and shepherds, (4) artisans, (5) military, (6) overseers, (7) councillors and assessors. Out of these 1 and 5 correspond to brÄhmaá¹‡as and ká¹£atriyas, 2-3 to vaiÅ›yas, 4 to Å›Å«dras; 6th and 7th correspond to adhyaká¹£as and amÄtyas (as in Kauá¹­ilya’s ArthaÅ›Ästra) and are not really castes but occupations. He probably regards them as castes because the offices of the adhyaká¹£as and amÄtyas were generally hereditary or at least he gathered that they were so. Megasthenes’ further statement (pp. 43-44) that ’no one is allowed to marry out of his own caste or to exercise any calling or art except his own’ shows that the prevailing tendency was that caste had become exclusive and mostly occupational in his day, though there must have been exceptions of which he was not informed.

Varna and castes derived therefrom

The ancient writers on dharmaÅ›Ästra strive very hard to account for the bewildering ramifications of the caste system from the four vará¹‡as that were spoken of in the Å›ruti (revelation). There is unanimity on the theory that the numerous castes actually found in the country arose from the unions of males of different vará¹‡as with women belonging to vará¹‡as differing from their own. The divergences (and they are [[51]] many) among the several smá¹›tikÄras relate only to details. The smá¹›ti writers had before them the hoary theory of four Vará¹‡as vouched for by infallible Vedic authority, but they were at the same time quite cognizant of the realities in society viz. the separate existence of numerous castes and sub-castes that had varying claims as regards social status and that were based in popular estimation on birth alone. Therefore, when one criticizes the smá¹›ti view of the derivation of numerous castes from the mixture or confusion (saá¹…kara) of the four vará¹‡as as purely hypothetical and imaginary, the criticism is true only partially. The smá¹›tis were composed in different parts of India at different times and they were meant to supply a popular want, to guide the people and to reflect the prevailing state of society and popular feeling. Therefore, it must be admitted that the numerous castes mentioned in the smá¹›tis did exist at the time of the smá¹›tis, that the social status of the several castes might have varied from country to country or from epoch to epoch, that the peculiar avocations and means of livelihood assigned to the various castes reflected the real state of things. The element of hypothesis and speculation lies only in the theory of a particular sub-caste having sprung from the union of two persons belonging to two particular vará¹‡as or castes.71 This description of the origin of the several castes was only indicative of the author’s view or of the accepted view about the social status of those castes in particular localities. There is here, as a matter of fact, great divergence of views among the several authors.

In the first place all writers on dharmaÅ›Ästra start with the propositions viz. (1) that the four vará¹‡as, brÄhmaá¹‡a, ká¹£atriya, vaiÅ›ya and Å›Å«dra, are arranged in a descending scale of social status; and (2) that marriage is or was permissible between a male of a higher vará¹‡a with a woman of a lower vará¹‡a, but the union of a woman of a higher vará¹‡a with a male of vará¹‡a lower than her own is reprehensible and not permitted. There is a third proposition advanced by many writers that a man belongs to a particular vará¹‡a or jÄti by birth only i. e. if born in lawful wedlock of parents both of whom belong to that vará¹‡a [[52]]

or jÄti.72 This is the view held by all medieval writers and digests and it is expressly said that a man belongs to a caste by birth and no actions of his can alter that fact, that several castes are like the species of animals and that caste attaches to the body and not to the soul (vide SÅ«tasaá¹ƒhitÄ). When a male of a higher vará¹‡a marries a woman of a lower vará¹‡a, the marriage is said to be anuloma (lit. with the hair, in the natural order) and the offspring is said to be anuloma; when there is a union of a woman of a higher vará¹‡a with a male of a lower vará¹‡a, it is said to be pratiloma (against the hair, i. e. against the natural or proper order) and the children of the union are said to be pratiloma. These two words anuloma and pratiloma (as applied to marriage or progeny) hardly ever occur in the vedic literature. In the Bá¹›. Up. (II. 1. 15) and Kauá¹£Ä«taki Br. Up. IV. 18 (quoted in note 89 above) the word ‘pratiloma’ is applied to the procedure adopted by a brÄhmaá¹‡a of going to a ká¹£atriya for knowledge about brahman. From this it may possibly be inferred that pratiloma and anuloma might have been employed with reference to marriages also (in the days of the Upaniá¹£ad). PÄá¹‡ini (IV. 4. 28) teaches the formation of words from anuloma and pratiloma. They occur in Gautama (IV. 14-15), Baud. Dh. S. (I. 8. 8), Vasiá¹£á¹­ha (18.7), Manu (X. 13), YÄj. (I. 95) and other smá¹›ti works.

One important question is whether the theory of anuloma or pratiloma castes presupposes a marriage or only a union (outside marriage) of a man and a woman. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 6. 13. 1, 3-4) lays down73 that a man must marry a virgin of his own vará¹‡a with the rites prescribed by the Å›Ästras and only the son born of such a marriage is entitled to the privileges and occupation of his father, that sexual intercourse with a woman who has been married by another or on whom the proper ceremonies of marriage are not performed or who [[53]]

Varna: anuloma and pratiloma castes

belongs to another caste is condemned and that the son (and not the daughter) born of such an union is condemned (through the sin of the parents). So Ä€pastamba looked with disfavour even on marriages called anuloma. He is entirely silent about anuloma and pratiloma castes. It is no doubt true that most ancient writers like Gautama (IV. 1), Vasiá¹£á¹­ha (I. 24), Manu (III. 12-13), YÄj. (I. 55 and 57) prescribe that a person should by preference marry a girl of his own vará¹‡a but also allow the marriage of a person with a girl of another vará¹‡a lower than his own. YÄj. (I. 92) expressly says that the six anuloma castes, mÅ«rdhÄvasikta, ambaá¹£á¹­ha, niá¹£Äda, mÄhiá¹£ya, ugra, and karaá¹‡a are so called only when they are the offspring of women married by men of higher vará¹‡as. Manu (X. 41) says that the six anuloma castes are entitled to the rites (saá¹ƒskÄras like upanayana) performed for dvijas, but that the pratiloma castes are like Å›Å«dras (i. e. even when a pratiloma caste springs from a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman and a ká¹£atriya or vaiÅ›ya male they cannot have upanayana and other rites of dvijas performed for them, though both parents are dvijas). Kauá¹­ilya74 (III. 7) also says that all pratilomas except cÄá¹‡á¸Älas are like Å›Å«dras; Viá¹£á¹‡u says that they are condemned by all Ä€ryas. Devala’s (quoted by ParÄÅ›aramÄdhavÄ«ya I. 2. p. 122) says that pratilomas are outside the pale of the system of vará¹‡as and are patita. The Smá¹›tyarthasÄra p. 13 says that anuloma sons and those born of the mÅ«rdhÄvasikta and other anuloma castes are dvijÄtis and are entitled to saá¹ƒskÄras as dvijÄtis. Commentators like KullÅ«ka (on Manu X. 11) say that, as no marriage is legally possible between a woman of a higher vará¹‡a and a male of a lower vará¹‡a, all the pratilomas are born outside lawful wedlock. Gautama (IV. 20) says that all pratilomas are dharmahÄ«na, which is interpreted by the Mit. on YÄj. III. 262 as meaning that they cannot have upanayana and similar saá¹ƒskÄras of dvijas performed for them, though they are amenable to the rules of morality and entitled to perform vratas and prÄyaÅ›cittas. Vasiá¹£á¹­ha, BaudhÄyana and several others do not make it clear whether, when they speak of pratilomas, they contemplate offspring of legal [[54]]

marriages or only of illegitimate and adulterous unions. But it will be seen from the list appended below that UÅ›anas and VaikhÄnasa almost always make a distinction between the caste assigned to the offspring of the union of parents of different vará¹‡as, according as there is a marriage between the two or it is only a clandestine, illegitimate or adulterous union. For example, UÅ›anas says75 that when there is a marriage between a ká¹£atriya male and a brÄhmaá¹‡a female, the offspring is called sÅ«ta, while the offspring of a clandestine union between a brÄhmaá¹‡a woman and a ká¹£atriya male was called rathakÄra. So these two authors held that there could be a legal marriage when a woman of a higher vará¹‡a married a male of a lower vará¹‡a.76 There were several other works like the SÅ«tasaá¹ƒhitÄ (ÅšivamÄhÄtmyakhaá¹‡á¸a chap. 12. 12-48) where a similar distinction is made between the children of marriages and clandestine unions. The Mit. on YÄj. I. 90 says that such progeny as kuá¹‡á¸a and golaka (Manu III. 174), kÄnÄ«na, sahoá¸haja (who are all not due to intercourse in wedlock) are different from savará¹‡a, anuloma and pratiloma and are to be treated as Å›Å«dras and that the ká¹£etraja son is to be treated as in a different category (since niyoga is allowed by the smá¹›tis and by the usage of Å›iá¹£á¹­as) and belongs to the caste of his mother. AparÄrka (on YÄj. These several kinds of secondary sons will be treated under inheritance.

Varna and jÄti

A few words must be said about the word jÄti. The idea of vará¹‡a was as we have seen based originally on race, culture, character and profession. It takes account mainly of the moral and intellectual worth of man and is a system of classes which appears more or less natural. The ideal of vará¹‡a even in the smá¹›tis lays far more emphasis on duties, on a high standard of effort for the community or society rather than on the rights and privileges of birth. The system of jÄtis (castes) lays all emphasis on [[55]]

birth and heredity and tends to create the mentality of clinging to privileges without trying to fulfil the obligations corresponding to such privileges. The word “jÄti’ in the sense of caste hardly ever occurs in the vedic literature. In the Nirukta77 (XII. 13) it is said ‘after agnicayana (the building of the fire altar), a man should not approach a rÄmÄ (for sexual intercourse); rÄmÄ is so called because she is approached only for pleasure and not for (accumulation of) merit; she is of a dark caste’. Here the word ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ajÄtÄ«yÄ occurs with reference to a woman of the Å›Å«dra caste. Almost these very words occur in Vasiá¹£á¹­ha (18. 17-18)78 where for ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ajÄtÄ«yÄ the word ká¹›á¹£á¹‡avará¹‡Ä is substituted. ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. I. 56 (yad-ucyate etc.)79 quotes these words as a brÄhmaá¹‡a text, but there the word is ká¹›á¹£á¹‡avará¹‡Ä«. Therefore, the use of the word jÄti in the sense of caste can be traced back at least to the times of the Nirukta. PÄá¹‡ini (V. 4. 9)80 teaches the formation of words like ‘brÄhmaá¹‡ajÄtÄ«ya’ derived from words ending in ‘jÄti’ (in the sense of caste). The expression ‘jÄtidharma’ (rules of castes) occurs in Gautama XI. 20, Vasiá¹£á¹­ha I. 17 and XIX. 7, Manu I. 118 and VIII, 41 and the word jÄti (caste) occurs also in Ä€p. Dh. S. II. 3. 6.1, II. 1.2.3, Manu (IV. 141, X, 11, 18, 40, 97), YÄj. (I. 95, 361, II. 69, 206, III. 213), NÄrada (á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna 288) and in the vÄrtika on PÄá¹‡ini IV. 1. 137. Vará¹‡a and jÄti are sometimes clearly distinguished as in YÄj. II. 69 and 206. But very often they are confounded. In Manu X. 27, 31 the word vará¹‡a is used in the sense of mixed castes (jÄtis). Conversely the word jÄti often appears to be used to indicate ‘vará¹‡a’. Vide Manu III. 15, VIII. 177, IX. 86 and 335, X. 41 and YÄj. I. 89 (in which latter sajÄti appears to mean ‘savará¹‡a’).

Even when anuloma marriages were allowed there is no unanimity among the sages and the smá¹›tis as to the status of the progeny of such unions. Three different views are found. The first view is that if a male of one vará¹‡a married a female of the vará¹‡a immediately after it, the progeny belonged to the vará¹‡a [[56]]

of the father; e. g. Baud. Dh. S.81 (I. 8.6 and I. 9. 3) says that sons born to a person of a savará¹‡a wife or a wife of the vará¹‡a next to his own are savará¹‡a i. e. the son of a brÄhmaá¹‡a from a wife of the ká¹£atriya vará¹‡a is a brÄhmaá¹‡a. The AnuÅ›Äsana-parva 48. 4, NÄrada (strÄ«puá¹ƒsa 106) and Kauá¹­ilya (III. 7) say the same. Gautama IV. 15 as interpreted by Haradatta appears to say that the offspring of a brÄhmaá¹‡a from a ká¹£atriya wife is called savará¹‡a, but not the offspring of a ká¹£atriya male from a vaiÅ›ya wife or of a vaiÅ›ya male from a Å›Å«dra wife. Fick (pp. 54-57) shows that even according to the BhaddasÄla JÄtaka ’the family of the mother does not matter; the family of the father alone is important’. The second view is that the progeny of anuloma unions is in status lower than the father, but higher than the mother; e. g. Manu X. 6 ‘sages declare the sons begotten by dvijas on wives of vará¹‡as immediately next to theirs as similar (to the fathers, but not of the same vará¹‡a with the fathers) but tainted by the inferiority of their mothers.’ The third view (and this is the common view) is that the progeny of anuloma marriages is of the same vará¹‡a as regards its privileges and obligations as the mother’s; e. g. vide Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 16. 282 and Åšaá¹…kha (prose) quoted by the Mit. on YÄj. I. 91 and AparÄrka (p. 118). A classical echo of this view is found in the ÅšakuntalÄ of KÄlidÄsa where king Duá¹£yanta exclaims aside to himself ‘would that this girl were born of the sage from a wife who was not savará¹‡a’.83 MedhÄtithi on Manu X. 6 says that PÄá¹‡á¸u, Dhá¹›tarÄá¹£á¹­ra and Vidura being ká¹£etraja sons took the caste of their mothers. The Mit. explains the words of Åšaá¹…kha by saying that the offspring of anuloma marriages such as mÅ«rdhÄvasikta are not really different from ká¹£atriyas, that they have the same saá¹ƒskÄras but they do not become ká¹£atriyas etc. and are dubbed by these names to indicate their mixed origin and come to form separate sub-castes. The pratilomas, as said above, are lower in status than any of the two parents. [[57]]

Varna and the enumeration of castes

The ancient dharmasÅ«tras mention only a few mixed castes. Ä€p. Dh. S. mentions only cÄá¹‡á¸Äla, paulkasa and vaiá¹‡a. Gautama names five anuloma castes, six pratiloma, one and eight others according to the view of some. BaudhÄyana adds to those mentioned by Gautama a few more viz rathakÄra, Å›vapaka, vaiá¹‡a and kukkuá¹­a. Vasiá¹£á¹­ha names even a smaller number than Gautama and BaudhÄyana. It is Manu (X) and Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. (XVI) that for the first time dilate upon the avocations of the mixed castes. Manu refers to 6 anuloma, 6 pratiloma and 20 doubly mixed castes and states the avocations of about 23; YÄj. names only 13 castes (other than the four Vará¹‡as). UÅ›anas names about 40 and gives their peculiar avocations. All the smá¹›tis taken together hardly mention more than about one hundred castes.

The number of primary anulomas is only six (vide Manu X. 10, YÄj. I. 91-92), but Manu names only three of them, viz. ambaá¹£á¹­ha, niá¹£Äda and ugra. The primary pratilomas also are six (vide Manu X. 11â€“12 and 16-17 and YÄj. I. 93â€“94) viz sÅ«ta, vaidehaka, cÄá¹‡á¸Äla, mÄgadha, ká¹£attá¹›, and Äyogava. Further sub-castes are said to arise from the unions of the anulomas and pratilomas with the four vará¹‡as and of the male of one anuloma and the female of another, from the union of pratilomas among themselves and from the union of a male or female of an anuloma caste and the female or male of a pratiloma caste. For example, YÄj. I. 95 defines rathakÄra as the offspring of a mÄhiá¹£ya male and a karaá¹‡a female, i. e. it is a further mixture between two anuloma castes. The Mit. on YÄj. I. 95 says that upanayana and other saá¹ƒskÄras are performed for the offspring of anuloma persons marrying among themselves. The Smá¹›tyarthasÄra (p. 13) says the same.84 Similarly Manu X. 15 says that Ävá¹›ta and ÄbhÄ«ra spring from the union of a brÄhmaá¹‡a with an ugra girl and an ambaá¹£á¹­ha girl respectively (i. e. from the union of a brÄhmaá¹‡a with anuloma caste girls). Manu X. 19 says that Å›vapÄka is the offspring of a ká¹£attá¹› male (a pratiloma) with an ugra female (an anuloma). Manu X. 33 defines maitreyaka as the offspring of a vaidehaka male and an Äyogava female (i.e. from parents who are both pratiloma). A [[58]]

smá¹›ti verse quoted85 by ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. I. 95 says that there are six anulomas, 24 doubly mixed castes (due to the union of the six anulomas with the four vará¹‡as), 6 pratilomas and 24 doubly mixed castes (due to the unions of 6 pratilomas with four vará¹‡as) i. e. in all 60 and further mixtures of these among themselves give rise to innumerable sub-castes. Similarly Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 16.786 says that the further mixed castes arising from the unions of mixed castes are numberless. This shows that before the time of the Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra (i. e. at least about 2000 years ago) numberless castes and sub-castes had been formed and the writers on dharmaÅ›Ästra practically gave up in despair the task of deriving them, even though mediately, from the primary vará¹‡as. The same state of things was continued and was rather aggravated by the time of the nibandhakÄras. MedhÄtithi on Manu X. 31 speaks of 60 mixed castes along with the four vará¹‡as and adds that by the intermingling of these endless sub-castes are formed. The MitÄká¹£arÄ87 on YÄj. I. 95 observes that since the castes springing from the double intermingling of vará¹‡as are innumerable, it is impossible to describe or enumerate them. Similarly the Ká¹›tyakalpataru in its prakÄ«rá¹‡aka topic of vyavahÄra section, when dealing with castes, merely quotes several passages from the smá¹›tis and does not add a word of its own about the castes, their names or avocations in its own day (first half of 12th century). The Mit. on YÄj. I. 9488 expressly says that the avocations of the pratilomas (about which YÄj. is silent) should be understood from the smá¹›tis of UÅ›anas and Manu. Medieval writers on dharmaÅ›Ästra usually ignore the treatment in detail of the numerous sub-castes and content themselves with dilating upon the duties of the four vará¹‡as.

Varna and sub-castes

There was great diversity of opinion among the smá¹›tikÄras about the derivation and status of the several sub-castes. We find that the same sub-caste is known under five or six different names even so early as the present Manusmá¹›ti. [[59]]

Manu X. 22 gives seven names for the same caste and Manu X. 23 gives five for another. ViÅ›varÅ«pa on YÄj. I. 92 explains that these different names are due to difference of locality.89 There is another difficulty. The same name given to a caste is differently derived by different writers (vide under niá¹£Äda and pÄraÅ›ava below). For the same sub-caste different names are given in different smá¹›tis (vide under ká¹›ta and rÄmaka). It became difficult to assign any peculiar derivation for groups of people and so Manu (X. 40), Vasiá¹£á¹­ha90 (18.7) and AnuÅ›Äsana-parva 148. 29 laid down that men’s sub-caste was to be known from their actions and occupations. This shows that according to most writers castes in the times of the smá¹›tis were predominantly occupational.

One word that frequently occurs in connection with castes and sub-castes is vará¹‡asaá¹…kara (or only saá¹…kara). In Manu X. 12 and 24 the word vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara is used in the plural in the sense of ‘mixed castes’, while in Manu X. 40 (and in V. 89) the word saá¹…kara seems to be used in the sense of ‘mixture or intermingling of Vará¹‡as’. Gautama (VIII. 3) employs the word saá¹ƒkara and says ‘on the two (the brÄhmaá¹‡a and the king) depends the prosperity of men, protection, the prevention of mixture (of vará¹‡as) and the (accumulation of) merit (or the observance of dharma).91 NÄrada (strÄ«puá¹ƒsa 102)92 says ’to be born from a union in the inverse order of Vará¹‡as amounts to vará¹‡asaá¹…kara;’ while Bá¹›haspati quoted in the Ká¹›tyakalpataru appears to apply the word vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara to both anuloma and pratiloma castes. Baud.93 Dh. S. (I. 9. 16) says that those who are born of [[60]]

Vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara are called vrÄtyas. The Mit. on YÄj. I. 96 applies the word vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara to both anuloma and pratiloma progeny. MedhÄtithi94 on Manu V. 88 says that the word ‘saá¹ƒkarajÄta’ indicates pratilomas like Äyogava and that even though as regards anulomas there is intermingling (of vará¹‡as), yet as they have the privileges of the caste of their mothers, even Manu himself does not apply the word saá¹ƒkÄ«rá¹‡ayoni to them as seen above (Manu X. 25). Yama95 quoted in the Ká¹›tyakalpataru says ‘Vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara arises by the violation of the restrictions (about marriage); if the proper order of vará¹‡as (i. e. male of higher vará¹‡a marrying a woman of a lower vará¹‡a) is followed, (the offspring) is entitled to be regarded as belonging to the system of vará¹‡as, but if the reverse order is followed it is sin.’ Manu (X. 24) says ‘mixed castes arise by members of one vará¹‡a having sexual intercourse with women of another vará¹‡a, by marrying women who ought not to have been married (such as a sagotra girl) and by neglect of the duties peculiar to one’s vará¹‡a’. The AnuÅ›Äsanaparva 48. 196 remarks that ‘vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara arises from wealth, greed, desires, uncertainty about the vará¹‡a (of a person) or ignorance about vará¹‡a’. Even in such a philosophical treatise as the Bhagavad-gÄ«tÄ (I. 41-43), it is said ‘when women become corrupt (or demoralised), intermingling of vará¹‡as arises; saá¹ƒkara necessarily leads the whole family and the destroyer of the family to hell. By reason of these transgressions of the destroyers of families which bring about vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara, the ancient caste observances and family observances are subverted.’

Varna and vará¹‡asaá¹…kara

On account of the great emphasis laid on the prevention of vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara the smá¹›tis ordain that it is one of the principal duties of the king to punish people if they transgress the rules prescribed for vará¹‡as and to punish men and women if guilty of vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara. from them;’ Vasiá¹£á¹­ha (19. 7-8) ’the king paying attention to all these viz. the observances of countries, castes and families should make the four vará¹‡as conform to their duties and should punish them when they go astray’; Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. It is on account of this that so early as in the first centuries of the Christian era we see that king VÄsiá¹­hÄ«puta Siri PulumÄyi is extolled as having prevented the mixture of the four vará¹‡as (E. That Vará¹‡asaá¹ƒkara had gone too far in the opinion of the author of the MahÄbhÄrata (Vanaparva 180. 31-33) follows from the following words which are despairingly put in the mouth of Yudhiá¹£á¹­hira:98 “It appears to me that it is very difficult to ascertain the caste of human beings on account of the confusion of all vará¹‡as; all sorts of men are always begetting offspring from all sorts of women; speech, sexual intercourse, being born and deathâ€”these are common to all human beings; and there is scriptural authority (for this view) in the words ‘We, who ever we are, offer the sacrifice’. Varna and jÄtyutkará¹£a

Gautama (IV. 18-19), Manu (X. 64-65) and YÄj. (I. 96) speak of a peculiar doctrine called JÄtyutkará¹£a and JÄtyapakará¹£a. These passages have given rise to some divergence of interpretation, [[62]]

but their general sense is clear. Gautama (IV. 18)100 says that according to the ÄcÄryas the anulomas when they marry in such a way that the bridegroom in each stage is higher or lower than the bride they rise to a higher or go down to a lower vará¹‡a in the 7th or 5th generation (respectively). This is explained by Haradatta as follows: When a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a ká¹£atriya woman the daughter born of this marriage is called savará¹‡Ä; if this latter is married to a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a daughter is born and this latter is again married by a brÄhmaá¹‡a and if this continues in the same way for seven generations, then when the 7th girl in descent marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a, whatever child is born of that union belongs to the brÄhmaá¹‡a vará¹‡a (though in the preceding generations only the fathers were brÄhmaá¹‡as and the mothers were all not strictly brÄhmaá¹‡as, but only savará¹‡Äs if at all). This is called jÄtyutkará¹£a (rise in status as a caste). On the other hand, when a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a ká¹£atriya girl and a son is born who is called savará¹‡a, then that son marries a ká¹£atriya girl and has a son and this is continued for five generations, then when the fifth son (in descent) marries a ká¹£atriya girl, the child born belongs to the ká¹£atriya vará¹‡a (though in all the preceding generations the father was higher than ká¹£atriya and the mother only was a ká¹£atriya). This is jÄtyapakará¹£a (fall in status as a caste). The same rule holds good as regards a ká¹£atriya marrying a vaiÅ›ya female and a vaiÅ›ya marrying a Å›Å«dra female. The same rule applies among anulomas also e. g. if a savará¹‡a (as defined by Gautama) marries an ambaá¹£á¹­ha girl and a daughter is born who again marries a savará¹‡a and this continues for seven generations, then the child of the 7th girl (in descent) from a savará¹‡a male becomes a savará¹‡a (rises in status); on the other hand if a savará¹‡a (offspring of brÄhmaá¹‡a male and ká¹£atriya female) marries an ambaá¹£á¹­ha girl and a son is born and that son marries an ambaá¹£á¹­ha girl and their son marries an ambaá¹£á¹­ha girl and this goes on for seven generations, then the child of [[63]]

the 7th male (in descent) born of an ambaá¹£á¹­ha wife becomes an ambaá¹£á¹­ha (i. e. there is jÄtyapakará¹£a as to anulomas).

According to Manu101 X. 64 when a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a Å›Å«dra woman, the daughter born is pÄraÅ›ava, and if this pÄraÅ›ava daughter marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a and the daughter of this latter union marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a and this continues for seven generations, then the seventh generation will be a brÄhmaá¹‡a (i. e. there will be jÄtyutkará¹£a). Conversely, if a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a Å›Å«dra woman and a son is born, he is a pÄraÅ›ava and that son marries a Å›Å«dra woman and their son again marries a Å›Å«dra woman and this goes on for 7 generations, the 7th generation becomes a mere Å›Å«dra (there is jÄtyapakará¹£a). It will be seen that this differs from Gautama in several respects. In the first place for both jÄtyutkará¹£a and jÄtyapakará¹£a seven generations are prescribed, while in Gautama they are 7 and 5 respectively (according to Haradatta). In the second place according to Gautama the 8th in descent from the first anuloma marriage secures jÄtyutkará¹£a, while according to Manu, the 7th secures it. Further Manu is silent about jÄtyutkará¹£a when the original parents are anulomas. Besides, the commentators of Manu shorten the period for rise or fall in jÄti as stated below. Manu X. 65 extends the same rules to the offspring of the marriage of a ká¹£atriya with a vaiÅ›ya woman and of a vaiÅ›ya with a Å›Å«dra woman. MedhÄtithi and KullÅ«ka extend these express words of Manu further by explaining that if a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a vaiÅ›ya female and a daughter is born and she again marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a then in the fifth generation there will be jÄtyutkará¹£a; conversely if a son is born from a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a vaiÅ›ya wife, and he marries [[64]]

a vaiÅ›ya female and this goes on, then in the fifth generation the son will be a vaiÅ›ya (i. e. there will be jÄtyapakará¹£a). Similarly if a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a ká¹£atriya woman then there is jÄtyutkará¹£a or jÄtyapakará¹£a in three generations.

YajÃ±avalkya (I. 96)102 speaks of two kinds of jÄtyutkará¹£a or jÄtyapakará¹£a viz. one due to marriage (as in Manu and Gautama) and another due to the avocation followed. It should be understood that there is rise in caste in the 7th or even in the 5th generation; if there is inversion as to the avocations, then there is corresponding similarity (of vará¹‡a in the 7th or even 5th generation). This is elaborated by the MitÄká¹£arÄ as follows: If a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a Å›Å«dra woman and a daughter is born she is a niá¹£ÄdÄ«; if this latter marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a daughter is born and she in turn marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a and this goes on for six generations, then the child of the 6th girl (in descent) becomes a brÄhmaá¹‡a (he being 7th in descent); similarly if a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a vaiÅ›ya woman and a girl is born, she is an ambaá¹£á¹­hÄ; if the latter marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a daughter is born and this goes on in the same way, then the fifth girl in descent has a child (from a brÄhmaá¹‡a husband) which is the 6th in descent from the original anuloma marriage and which then becomes a brÄhmaá¹‡a. If a brÄhmaá¹‡a marries a ká¹£atriya woman and a daughter is born who is called mÅ«rdhÄvasiktÄ (YÄj. I. 91) and she marries a brÄhmaá¹‡a, then the fourth in descent marrying a brÄhmaá¹‡a has a child (5th in descent), then that child becomes a brÄhmaá¹‡a. Similarly, if a ká¹£atriya married a Å›Å«dra female and a daughter was born, she was called ugrÄ, then jÄtyutkará¹£a by marrying a higher male took place in the 6th generation. If a ká¹£atriya married a vaiÅ›ya woman, the daughter born was a mÄhiá¹£yÄ and jÄtyutkará¹£a took place in the 5th generation. If a vaiÅ›ya married a Å›Å«dra woman, the daughter of the marriage is a karaá¹‡Ä« and if she marries a vaiÅ›ya, then in the fifth generation there would be jÄtyutkará¹£a. Certain peculiar avocations and activities are prescribed for the four vará¹‡as. Each vará¹‡a may in times of difficulty follow the occupation peculiar to the caste immediately below it, but should not follow the avocations peculiar to the higher vará¹‡as;103 he must however revert to his proper avocation when the difficulty is [[65]]

Varna and jÄtyapakará¹£a

over (vide Vasiá¹£á¹­ha II. 13-23, Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. II. 15, YÄj. I. 118-120, Gautama X. 1-7 &c.). If a brÄhmaá¹‡a begins to follow the avocations peculiar to a Å›Å«dra and has a son, who does the same and this goes on continuously for seven generations, the 7th becomes a Å›Å«dra (by caste). If a brÄhmaá¹‡a begins to follow the avocations peculiar to a vaiÅ›ya or a ká¹£atriya then in the 5th or 6th generation respectively there is fall in caste (i.e. the 5th or 6th becomes respectively a vaiÅ›ya or ká¹£atriya). Similarly if a ká¹£atriya follows the avocation of a vaiÅ›ya or Å›Å«dra and this goes on continuously, then the 5th or 6th (respectively) becomes a Å›Å«dra or vaiÅ›ya. Similarly if a vaiÅ›ya takes to the work peculiar to Å›Å«dras, then the fifth generation becomes Å›Å«dra.

Baud.104 Dh. S. (I. 8. 13-14) gives another illustration of jÄtyutkará¹£a. He says “if a niá¹£Äda (the son of a brÄhmaá¹‡a from a Å›Å«dra wife) marries a niá¹£ÄdÄ« (and this goes on continuously), then the fifth generation becomes free of the taint of a Å›Å«dra status, he can have upanayana performed for him and his son (6th from the original niá¹£Äda pair) can have a vedic sacrifice performed for him.’

These provisions would considerably lessen the rigour of the caste system based purely on birth. But one feels grave doubts whether such a method of jÄtyutkará¹£a or jÄtyapakará¹£a (particularly the one based on occupation) was or could be ever enforced in actual life. It would have been impossible to remember descent in a particular way for five or seven generations. The want of unanimity among the original smá¹›tikÄras and the commentators also points in the direction that the method advocated, though it might have originally some slight basis in fact, was only a hypothesis and an ideal. Hardly any examples of jÄtyutkará¹£a in the way set out by Manu or YÄj. occur in the literature on dharma-Å›Ästra or in inscriptions. In the inscriptions we have authentic cases of intercaste marriages only, but hardly anything further.105 From the TÄlaguá¹‡á¸a pillar inscription of king KÄkusthavarman of the Kadamba family we learn that the Kadambas were originally of brÄhmaá¹‡a lineage, that the founder of the family was a brÄhmaá¹‡a [[66]]

MayÅ«raÅ›arman, who became exasperated with the Pallavas of KÄÃ±cÄ«pura and took up the sword to conquer the earth, that his descendants applied the affix ‘Varman’ to their names (as if they were ká¹£atriyas, according to Manu II. 32) and KÄkustha Varman (4th in descent from MayÅ«raÅ›arman) married his daughters to Gupta and other kings106. This shows that an originally brÄhmaá¹‡a family came to look upon itself as ká¹£atriya by virtue of pursuing the profession of arms and governing the people. In the MahÄbhÄrata we meet with stories of kings who became brÄhmaá¹‡as. For example, AnuÅ›Äsana 30 speaks of Vitahavya, a king, as having become a brÄhmaá¹‡a; similarly Åšalya (39. 36-37) speaks of Ä€rá¹£á¹­iá¹£eá¹‡a, SindhudvÄ«pa, DevÄpi and ViÅ›vÄmitra as having become brÄhmaá¹‡as at a sacred spot on the SarasvatÄ«. In the PurÄá¹‡as also there are stories107 of kings like ViÅ›vÄmitra, MÄndhÄtÄ, Saá¹ƒká¹›ti, Kapi, VadhryaÅ›va, Purukutsa, Ará¹£á¹­iá¹£eá¹‡a, AjamÄ«á¸ha and others as having risen to the status of brÄhmaá¹‡as. These are mythical sages and their rise is not stated to have been due to the principle of jÄtyutkará¹£a108. Ibbetson (Report on the census of the Punjab 1881, pp. 174-176) notes that brÄhmaá¹‡as following certain professions became degraded and that the caste was changed.

Varna: caste and other guilds

That the professional castes were wealthy and well organized follows from the dharmaÅ›Ästra works and epigraphic records. In this connection the words Å›reá¹‡i, pÅ«ga, gaá¹‡a, vrÄta and saá¹…gha deserve to be carefully studied. All these were called samÅ«ha (group) or varga according to KÄtyÄyana.109 These [[67]]

words occur in the Vedic literature, but the sense is generally ‘a group’ and there is no special meaning attached. ‘Åšreá¹‡i’ occurs in the á¹šg. I. 163. 10110 (like flamingoes the horses press forward in rows or groups); both vrÄta and gaá¹‡a occur in á¹šg. III. 26. 6, V. 53. 11 and in numerous other places. The Kauá¹£Ä«taki Br. 16.7111 speaks of Rudra as pÅ«ga (as he is the head of the band of Maruts). Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 1. 3. 26112 quotes a BrÄhmaá¹‡a passage about a group (saá¹…gha) of brahmacÄrins going about for alms. PÄá¹‡ini teaches113 the formation of derivatives from pÅ«ga, gaá¹‡a, saá¹…gha (V. 2. 52), from vrÄta (V. 2. 21). In his time it appears the words had acquired specific meanings. (vide my notes to the translation of KÄtyÄyana verses 678-682 of that reconstructed smá¹›ti). KÄyastha.125 Heated controversies have raged in medieval and modern times about the origin and status of kÄyasthas and the bitterness is reflected in the decisions of the Indian courts also. In Bholanath v. Emperor126 the Calcutta High Court held that the kÄyasthas of Bengal were Å›Å«dras and went so far as to hold that a kÄyastha could marry a Dom female. But in Asita Mohan v. Nirode Mohani127 the Privy Council left open the question whether the kÄyasthas of Bengal were Å›Å«dras. On the other hand in Tulsi Ram v. Bihari Lal128 and in Ishwari Prasad v. Rai Hari Prasad129 the Allahabad and Patna High Courts respectively held that the kÄyasthas were dvijas and not Å›Å«dras. In Subrao v. Radha 52 Bom. 497 at p. 504-506 this conflict of decisions is referred to. The word kÄyastha does not occur in the ancient dharmasÅ«tras of Gautama, Ä€pastamba, BaudhÄyana, or Vasiá¹£á¹­ha nor [[76]]

in the Manusmá¹›ti. The Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. VII. 3 defines a public document (rÄjasÄká¹£ika) as one written in the royal court or office by a kÄyastha appointed by the king and attested by the hand of the superintendent of the office.[^181] These words suggest that the kÄyastha was an officer and that there is nothing about a caste here. YÄj. I. 322 calls upon the king to protect the subjects from the harassment of cÄá¹­as (rogues), thieves, bad characters, desperadoes and the like and particularly of kÄyasthas. The Mit. explains that kÄyasthas are accountants and scribes, are favourites of the king and very cunning. UÅ›anas[^182] (35) holds the kÄyasthas to be a caste and gives an uncomplimentary derivation of the name by saying that it is compounded of the first letters of kÄka (crow), Yama and sthapati to convey the three attributes of greed, cruelty and the spoliation (or paring), characteristic of the three. The Veda-VyÄsa-smá¹›ti[^183] (I. 10-11) includes the kÄyastha among Å›Å«dras along with barbers, potters and others. Sumantu quoted in the Par. M. II part 1.

The number of works dealing with the origin and characteristics of the caste system in India is legion. Most of them, however, concern themselves with the detailed {{des cription|description}} of the bewildering variety of castes and subdivisions of castes in modern times and their present religious and social customs and usages. The origin of caste has given rise to great speculation and several schools of thought have arisen. Generally individual authors lay undue emphasis on one element or attach far too much importance to one point in tracing the origin of the caste system and its ramifications, such as race (Risley), tribe (Ibbetson), occupation (Nesfield). The study of the origin and development of caste in India is one of deep and absorbing interest to all students of sociology. A complete and critical examination of the several theories of caste advanced by distinguished authors and a detailed description of the hundreds of castes and {{suboastes|sub-castes}} now found in India is far beyond the scope of the present work. For those who want to make a thorough study of the most important works on caste a modest list is given in the footnote below.1

The {{oaste|caste}} system has been highly eulogised and also most severely condemned by Western writers.
