Sidney Low in his Vision of India (pp. 262-263, 2nd ed. of 1907) speaks of the beneficent aspect of the caste system in the following eloquent passage: ‘There is no doubt that it is the main cause of the fundamental stability and contentment by which Indian society has been braced up for centuries against the shocks of politics and the cataclysms of Nature. It provides every man with his place, his career, his {{ocoupation|occupation}}, {{bie|his}} {{cirole|circle}} of friends. It makes him at the outset a member of a corporate body; it protects him through life from the canker of social jealousy and unfulfilled aspirations; it ensures him {{compa nionship|companionship}} and a sense of community with others in like case with himself. The caste organization is to the Hindu his club, {{bis|his}} trade union, his benefit society, his philanthropic society. There are no work-houses in India and none are as yet needed.’ AbbÃ© Dubois, who wrote about 130 years ago after being in close touch with Hindus of all castes for 15 years as {{&|a}} missionary, remarks (in his work on the character, manners and customs of the people of India, translated into English and published in London in 1817) ‘I consider the institution of castes among the Hindu nations as the happiest effort of their legislation; and I am well convinced that, if the people of India never sunk into a state of barbarism, and if, when almost all Europe was plunged in that dreary gulf, India kept up her head, preserved and {{ex tended|extended}} the sciences, the arts and civilization, it is wholly to the distinction of castes that she is indebted for that high celebrity’ (p. 14) and he devotes several pages to the justification of this remark. Maine in his Ancient Law (new edition of 1930 p. 17) characterises it as ’the most disastrous and blighting of all human institutions.’ Sherring in Hindu tribes and castes vol. III p. 293 says ‘it is the most baneful, hard-hearted and cruel social system that could possibly be invented for damning the human race’. On the other hand Meredith Townsend in Europe and Asia (edition of 1901 p. 72) wrote ‘I firmly believe caste to be a marvellous discovery, a form of socialism which through ages protected Hindu {{Sooiety|society}} from anarchy and from the worst evils of industrial and competitive lifeâ€”it is an automatic poor law to begin with and the strongest form known of {{Trades Union|Trade Union}}’. There are others,2 though their number is small, that believe that the caste system was an invention, an artificial product, due to the machinations of crafty {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. Every great institution has its extremes of good or evil. This work will endeavour to steer clear of downright and hypercritical {{con demnation|condemnation}} of the caste system due to relying on modern {{stand points|standpoints}} and conditions of society and unthinking adulation thereof. It will try to present and balance facts and though it cannot help passing judgments it will leave the reader free to judge for himself as far as possible. In the present work the discussion will be mostly confined to the evidence of Sanskrit literary monuments, ancient and medieval. An attempt will be made to trace the origin of caste from Vedic times, to exhibit theories of the {{DharmasÅ«trakÄras|DharmasÅ«trakÄras}}, other {{smá¹›tikÄras|smá¹›tikÄras}} and {{oom mentators|commentators}} on the subject and to describe the peculiar ceremonies, privileges, duties and responsibilities of Hindu castes {{88|as}} gathered from these works in Sanskrit. To discuss the feasibility or desirability of totally destroying the caste system or the ways and means of attaining that end is deemed to be outside the legitimate limits of this volume. It may, however, be expressly stated as the author’s opinion, in order to avoid {{misunderstand ings|misunderstandings}} or fruitless speculations about his personal views, that he does not think that the caste system was an artificial product due to the intrigues, greed and cunning of {{brÄbmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, nor does he hold that it is feasible to destroy the whole edifice of the caste system in the near future. In the cities we may find some people taking their food together but the real India is in the villages, where in spite of the loud denunciations of reformers for a hundred years, the restrictions on taking food and inter-caste marriages are almost as rigid as they once were. Our efforts must be {{direoted|directed}} to wide and rapid spread of literacy among the village people, the diffusion of the idea of one people and one nationality and gradual fusion of small subcastes into larger similar units. We in India have no doubt reached {{&|a}} critical stage in our history when old ideals, institutions and habits are being shattered by the impact of new ideas and by the onrush of world forces. We {{bave|have}} to decide whether we shall make or be able to make a clean sweep of all old ideals and institutions as so much debris and rubbish or whether while keeping the old ideals and some of the old institutions as {{founda tions|foundations}} we shall build up a new social order and create and foster new {{babits|habits}} of thought and action. It is beyond the scope of the present work to write more on this point. [[21]]

A sort of caste system based on birth and occupation did prevail in many countries in ancient times as in Persia, Rome and Japan. But in all these countries it hardly ever made any near approach in rigour and complexity to the {{oaste|caste}} system that we have in India and instead of ramifying into divisions and subdivisions, it dwindled and disappeared in the course of time. {{Nounanimity|No unanimity}} seems possible as to the several causes and {{ciroum stanoes|circumstances}} which led in India alone to the evolution of the stupendous structure of caste. Not only is it impossible to hold that the origin of the modern complexity of the {{casto|caste}} system is to be traced back to one single cause, but it is difficult to accept that even all the origins that have been postulated by the several authors can {{adoquately|adequately}} and satisfactorily explain the modern caste system.

In most of the works on the castes in India a {{fow|few}} features are pointed out as the characteristics of the caste system and as common to all castes and {{sub-oestes|sub-castes}}. They are: (1) heredity i. e. in theory a man is assigned to a particular caste by birth in that {{oaste|caste}}; (2) endogamy and exogamy i. e. restriction as to marrying in the same caste and not marrying {{cortain rela. tives|certain relatives}} or other persons, though of the same caste; (3) {{restrio tions|restrictions}} as to food (i. e. what food and water may be taken or not taken and from whom); (4) occupation (i. e. members of most castes follow certain occupations and no others); (5) {{grade tion|gradation}} of castes, some being at the top in the social scale and others being deemed to be so low that they are untouchable. Some authors3 like Senart add another characteristic, viz. the {{oaste|caste}} council with its {{ohiof|chief}} having in meeting assembled among other matters the power to regulate the conduct of its members, to impose the penalties of fine or excommunication for lapses. It may be said at once that this last is a feature that is not found among most of the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and {{ká¹£atriya}} castes even in modern times and is not dealt with by {{dharma sÄstra|dharmaÅ›Ästra}} works. Endogamy is now the most prominent {{characte ristio|characteristic}} of caste and so is the theory that it is by birth. The other three are more or less fluctuating from province to {{prom vince|province}} and age to age. In this work the first five characteristics of the {{oaste|caste}} system set out above will be subjected to a close critical examination on the basis of the Vedic and {{dharma sÄstra|dharmaÅ›Ästra}} material. We must also remember that the attributes of caste have not been the same throughout the ages. There is great difference between the popular conceptions of modern caste and the conceptions about it embodied in the ancient and medieval {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} works. In the twentieth century caste in India is a matter of marriage and to a much lesser extent of food and drink. As to {{avoca tions|avocations}} any one can at present follow any profession without fear of loss of caste excepting a few believed to be very impure and very degrading ones (like those of sweepers, butchers, tanners &c.). It is also not possible even now for any one to be generally accepted as a priest, unless he is or claims to be a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. The old barriers that separated one caste or subcaste from another have been greatly shaken by the influx of modern ideas and the exigencies of the times and one may hope that in a few decades more caste will remain as a purely social institution regulating marriages and to a lesser extent {{commen. sality|commensality}} (and not a religious one). Western scholars, in spite of their most commendable patience and industry, often present, through ignorance or lack of first-hand knowledge, the number of castes as larger than what it actually is. For example, Sherring (vol. II. Introduction pp. XXII-XLVI) gives an {{alpbabetical|alphabetical}} list of {{brahmanical|brÄhmanical}} tribes and remarks (XLVII) ‘Hundreds of these tribes, if not at enmity with one another, cherish mutual distrust and antipathy to such a degree that they are socially separated from one another as far as it is possible for them to beâ€”as much as {{brahmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} are from the lowest {{outoastes|outcastes}}â€”neither eating nor drinking together nor intermarrying’. The list he gives is most misleading. To take only a few examples, he enumerates Athavle, Achwal, Abhyankara, Apte, Agashe, Bhanu, Bivalkar, Badye, Bhide, Bhagvat, Bhuskute, Bhat, Bodas as separate tribes; but it is well-known to people in Western India that these are the surnames (not subcastes) of the Konkanastha or Citpavana {{brabmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, who not only interdine, but also inter-marry among themselves, provided there is no bar on the ground of sameness of {{gotrn|gotra}} and pravara.

The word {{vará¹‡a}} means ‘colour’ or ’light’ in most passages of the {{Roveda|á¹šgveda}} (e. g. I. 73.7, II. 3.5, IX. 97.15, IX. 104.4, IX. 105.4, X. 124.7). But in some verses of the {{á¹šgveda}}4 the word {{vará¹‡a}} is associated with groups of people having a skin of a dark or fair colour. For example, we read in á¹šg. II. 12.4 ‘(Indra) who placed low the {{dÄsa}} colour in a cave (or darkness)’; in á¹šg. I. 179.6 ’the fierce sage (Agastya) cherished both {{vará¹‡as}}’; in á¹šg. IX. 71.2 ’like one (a fighter) who strikes the people he (Soma) who is powerful goes giving out frequent roars; he exposes the Asura colour’; in á¹šg. I. 130.8 ‘Indra helped in battles the {{Ä€rya}} sacrificer… Indra punished for the sake of Manu (the {{dÄsas}}) who do not observe the ordinances and subdued (or killed) the dark skin’; ‘Indra having killed the {{dasyus}} protected the {{Ärya vará¹‡a}} (á¹šg. III. 34.9); ‘You (Indra) subdued for {{á¹šjiÅ›van}}, the son of {{Vidathin}}, Pipru and powerful {{Má¹›gaya}}; you mowed down fifty thousand dark (men), you shattered cities as old age does shatter good looks’ á¹šg. IV. 16.13; ‘Somas, which strike away the dark skin’ á¹šg. IX. 41.1. In á¹šg. I. 158.5 a certain {{dÄsa}} is called Traitana which name has a Persian ring about it; In á¹šg. I. 104.2 {{vará¹‡a}} seems to be placed in opposition to {{dÄsa}}. These passages make it clear that the {{Ä€ryas}} and {{dÄsas}} were two opposing camps and both were designated {{vará¹‡as}} on account of the colour of their skins. The {{Tai. Br.|Tait. Br.}} I. 2. 6 (with reference to the {{MahÄvrata}} in which there was a mock fight between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{Å›Å«dra}}) says ’that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is the divine {{vará¹‡a}}, and {{Å›Å«dra}} is the {{asurya|Äsurya vará¹‡a}}’.5 If we can interpret the á¹šgveda verse by the help of the {{Tai. Br.|Tait. Br.}}, then in á¹šg. IX, 71.2, the words ‘{{asuryam vará¹‡am}}’ mean ‘{{Å›Å«dra}} tribe’. There is no doubt that the word asura when applied to gods like {{Varupa|Varuá¹‡a}} has another meaning also in the á¹šg. In numerous places in the á¹šgveda the antagonism between the {{Äryas}} and {{dÄsas}} or {{dasyus}} is emphasized and prayers are offered to Indra and other gods for having subdued or for subduing the {{dÄsa}} in favour of the {{Ä€rya}}. á¹šg. I. 51.8; I. 103.3; I. 117.21; II. 11.2, 4, 18, 19; III. 29.9; V. 70.3; VII. 5.6; IX. 88.4; VI. 18.3; VI. 25.2. In á¹šg. I. 51.8 Indra is requested to mark who are {{Äryas}} and who are {{dasyus}}. This does not mean that there was difference between the two in bodily appearance only; on the contrary the antithesis between the {{Ärya}} who is referred to as ‘{{barhiá¹£mat}}’ and the {{dasyu}} who is styled ‘{{avrata}}’ clearly shows that the emphasis was rather on the difference of their cults. That {{dasyu}} and {{dÄsa}} are identical in meaning follows from the same epithets being applied to both and from the fact that {{dasyu}} and {{dÄsa}} occur in the same verses as applying to the same enemy.6 In á¹šg. X. 22.8 {{dasyu}} and {{dÄsa}} are used in the same verse as applicable to the same enemy. In á¹šg. X. 99.6 and 8 Indra is represented as killing both {{dÄsa}} and {{dasyu}} respectively. The {{dasyus}} are described as ‘{{avrata}}’ (not obeying the ordinances of the gods) in á¹šg. I. 51.8, I. 175.3, VI. 14.3, ‘{{akratu}}’ (who perform no sacrifice) in VII. 6.3,7 ‘{{má¹›dhravÄcaá¸¥}}’ (whose speech is indistinct or soft) in VII. 6.3 and V. 29.10, ‘{{anÄsaá¸¥}}’ (snub-nosed or dumb) in á¹šg. V. 29.10. It appears that {{dÄsa}} and {{dasyu}} are synonyms and were sometimes styled asuras. For example, {{Åšambara}} is called {{dasyu}} and {{dÄsa}} in á¹šgveda VI. 31.4 and {{dÄsa}} in á¹šg. VI. 26.5 and is also associated with asuras like Pipru in VI. 18.8; Pipru is spoken of as a {{dÄsa}} in á¹šg. VIII. 32.2 and as an asura in X. 138.3. {{Varcin}} is styled {{dÄsa}} in á¹šg. IV. 30.15 and VI. 47.21 and asura in VII. 99.5. In {{Tai. S.|Tait. S.}}8 (IV. 3.11.3) also it seems that they are held to be identical. The enmity between {{dÄsa}} and {{Ärya}} is breathed in such verses as the following: á¹šg. II. 11.4 ‘vanquish the tribe of {{dÄsas}} by the sun (i. e. by the help of a brilliant weapon); á¹šg. I. 174.7 ‘You made the earth a pillow for the {{dÄsa}} (i. e. you laid him low on the ground); á¹šg. III. 12.6 ‘Oh Indra and Agni, by one effort together you shook ninety cities that had {{dÄsas}} as overlords’. It is not possible to say that {{dÄsas}} or {{dasyus}} were some Aryan tribes that had fallen from the worship or culture of the Aryan singers of Vedic {{hyians|hymns}}. In many places the sage refers to the conquest for him by Indra and other gods of {{dÄsas}} as well as Aryan foes. For example, ‘Protectors of the good! you (two) killed Aryan foes and {{dÄsa}} foes’ á¹šg. VI. 60.6; ‘Oh Indra and {{Varuá¹‡a}}! you killed {{dÄsa}} foes and also {{Ä€rya}} foes and helped {{SudÄs}} with your protection’ á¹šg. VII.83.1. Vide also á¹šg. VI. 22.10, X. 69.6, X. 33.1, X. 102.3. This shows that though the {{Äryans|Ä€ryas}} had become divided and fought among themselves, they kept {{Äryas}} and {{dÄsas}} quite distinct. The foregoing shows that in the times of the á¹šgveda there were two antagonistic camps, of the {{dryas|Ä€ryas}} and {{dÄsas}} or {{dasyus}}, {{tbey|they}} differed in the colour of their skins and also in worship, speech and bodily appearance. Therefore, in the earliest period we find the word {{vará¹‡a}} associated only with {{dÄsa}} and with {{drya|Ärya}}. Though the words {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and {{ká¹£atriya}} occur frequently in the á¹šgveda, the word {{vará¹‡a}} is not used in connection with them. Even in the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} (á¹šgveda X. 90) where the words {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{rÄjanya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya}} and {{Å›Å«dra}} occur the word {{vará¹‡a}} is not used. Hence, one may reasonably say that the only water-tight groups that are positively or expressly vouchsafed by the á¹šgveda are {{Ärya}} and {{dÄsa}} or {{dasyu}}. It is often argued that as the word {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} denotes a caste in later literature, in the á¹šgveda also it must be presumed to have the same meaning. But this begs the whole question. No one denies that {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} denotes a caste in later literature. But whether it has the same sense in the á¹šgveda must be determined on the materials furnished by the á¹šgveda itself. Some rely on the word ‘{{bra hmaputra|brahmaputra}}’ in á¹šg. II. 43.2 as showing that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} became so only by birth in the á¹šgveda. But the verse begins by saying that ‘you sing a {{SÄma}} like the {{UdgÄtá¹›}} priest’ and so ‘{{brahma putra|brahmaputra}}’ must mean a {{ftvij|á¹›tvij}} whose duty it was to recite {{Å›astras}} (the {{BrahmapÄochamsin|BrahmÄá¹‡Äcchaá¹sin}} as {{SÄyana}} explains). It is generally conceded that the {{Puruá¹£agÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} is a much later hymn than most of the hymns of the á¹šgveda. In the whole of the á¹šgveda the words {{vaiÅ›ya}} and {{Å›Å«dra}} do not {{oocur|occur}} except in the {{Puruá¹£a sÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}}, though both of them occur in the {{Atharyaveda|Atharvaveda}} (V. 17.9 for {{vaiÅ›ya}} and IV. 20. 4 and 8 for {{Å›Å«dra}} and {{Ärya}}) and very frequently in the {{Tai. S|Tait. S}}. Besides we cannot forget that the final redaction of the á¹šgveda must be held to have been separated from the composition of the individual hymns by several hundred years (if not more) and that even if it be {{oonooded|conceded}} that at the time when the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} was composed, the four {{vará¹‡as}} had been constituted and had become castes, yet the same cannot be affirmed for the time of the original composition of the other {{bymns|hymns}}. The word {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} occurs several times in the á¹šgveda:9 ‘Oh {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, Oh {{pitá¹›s}} fond of soma! May the sinless {{DyÄvÄpá¹›thivi|DyÄvÄpá¹›thivÄ«}} (Heaven and Earth) tend to our welfare’; ‘Like {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} in the AtirÄtra where soma is to be drunk, uttering (words) round a lake full of water you have, Oh frogs, gathered together on that day of the year on which the rains begin’ (á¹šg. VII. 103.7); ‘The {{brÄhmanan|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, who drink soma, reciting prayers of the yearly sacrifice, have sent forth their speech’ (á¹šg. VII. 103. 8). In this verse {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} are expressly said to be getting ‘{{brahma}}’ ready. ‘May {{Agoi|Agni}} who devours every thing make that (dead body) free from disease and (may) soma also (do the same) who entered into the {{brÄhmanas|brÄhmaá¹‡as}}’ (á¹šg. X. 16. 6). ‘When the {{brÄbmapas|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} worship together as friends in hymns (lit. speed of the mind) that are fabricated from their hearts’ (á¹šg. X. 71. 8). In {{RÃ¸.|á¹šg.}} VI. 75. 10 {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} are invoked for welfare along with {{pitá¹›s}}. This shows that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were highly venerated. The other verses establish that they were the reciters of hymns ({{brahma}}) and drank soma. In á¹šg. VIII. 35. 16-18 we read ‘You ({{As vins|AÅ›vins}}) urge on (or inspire) {{brabma|brahma}}, you urge on our thoughts (or actions), you kill the evil spirits and subdue diseases; (17) you urge {{ká¹£atra}} (valour) and also men, you kill evil spirits (same as 16); (18) you urge on the cows and also the {{ViÅ›}} (the rest is same as 16).’ Here it seems that the groups of people (viz. those who think and make songs, those who show valour and lead men, and those common people who tend cattle) are clearly meant. These verses may be conceded as pointing to the existence of three groups ({{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, {{ká¹£atriyas}} and {{viÅ›aá¸¥}}) but there is nothing in them to show that these three had crystallised into something like the castes of later times. In á¹šg. VII. 33. 11 Vasiá¹£á¹­ha is addressed as {{brahman}}, but that does not mean that he was a {{brÄbmaá¹‡a}} (by birth), as he is said to have been born of {{UrvaÅ›i}} from Mitra and Varuá¹‡a. Similarly, in á¹šg. IX. 96. 6 ({{BrahmÄ devÄnÄm}}) the word {{brahma|brahmÄ}}130 does not certainly mean ‘{{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} by birth’, nor does ‘{{viprÄá¹‡Äm}}’ mean ‘{{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} by birth’. In that verse one who is super-eminent among a group is specified, just as the buffalo among animals, the hawk among carnivorous birds &c. In á¹šg. VIII. 33. 19 ’look down and not up: bring your feet close together; may thy {{kaÅ›aplakas|kÄÅ›aplakas}} (legs?) be not seen, for though a {{brahmÄ}}, thou wert born a woman,’ it is impossible to hold that the last words mean ’thou wert a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman’. If it is only intended to refer to the fact that she is a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman, there is no reason why the Perfect tense ({{babhÅ«vitha}}) is used and not the present. Here ‘{{brahmÄ}}’ most probably means ‘a priest of that name’, as that is the meaning in á¹šg. II. 1. 2 ({{brahma casi gá¹›bapatis-ca no dame|brahmÄ cÄsi gá¹›hapatiÅ›ca no dame}}). The word ‘{{brahmajÄyÄ}}’ in á¹šg. X. 109.2, 3, 6 and 7 does not mean the wife of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} by birth but rather ‘wife of {{Bá¹›haspati}}’. The whole hymn is obscure and more or less enigmatic or allegorical. In the Aitareya-brÄhmaá¹‡a11 35. 2-4 it is said that soma is the food of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and that a {{ká¹£atriya}} was to press the tendrils of the Nyagrodha tree and the fruits of Udumbara, {{AÅ›vattha}} and {{Plaká¹£a}} and drink the juice so pressed instead of soma. It appears, therefore, that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were a distinct group even in the earliest period of the á¹šgveda. Whether they were hereditary is certainly not clear; nor is there anything to show that there were restrictions as regards partaking of food from persons other than {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} or as to marriage. That {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} in the á¹šgveda were a class by themselves may be conceded, but whether they had become a caste by birth is a matter of opinion dependent on the connotation given to the word caste. Dr. Ghurye (Caste and race in India p. 42) thinks, probably following the Vedic Index (vol. I on {{Ká¹£atriya}}), that the reference in á¹šg. X. 71. 9 to a false claim for being regarded as a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} points to the {{nonolusion|conclusion}} that {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} had become a caste. The {{verge|verse}} literally translated means ’these (persons) who do not move below nor beyond, who are neither {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, nor engaged in pressing soma – they being ignorant and having reported to speech in sinful (or coarse) language take to ploughshares and engage in (agricultural) operations’. It is difficult to see how there is here any false claim to be regarded as a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. This verse means apparently that those who are not composers of prayers or drinkers of soma (because they are ignorant) are men of low speech and have to turn to agriculture. Even in the days of the {{dharma-sÅ«tras}} restrictions as to food and marriage for {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were not at all as rigid as they became in medieval and modern times; but even when these restrictions were not rigid it was clearly laid down that a {{brÄbmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is so by birth alone. The word ‘{{brahma}}’ generally means in the á¹šgveda ‘prayer’ or ‘hymn’. Vide12 á¹šg. IV. 6.11, VI. 52.2, X. 105.8, X. 141.5 (‘Oh Agni, make our prayer and sacrifice prosper by your flames’). á¹šg. III. 53.12 is ’this {{brahma}} (prayer or spiritual power) of {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}} protects the Bharata people’. In the Atharvaveda II. 15.4 (as {{brahma}} and {{ká¹£atra}} entertain no fear, nor are they harmed) {{brahma}} seems to mean ’the class of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}’. The transition of meaning from ‘{{brahma}}’ (prayer) to ‘{{brahma}}’ meaning the class of those who composed or recited prayers is natural and easy. In the á¹šgveda I. 157.2 both {{brahina|brahma}} and {{kpatra|ká¹£atra}} occur13 in the same verse where they probably mean ‘prayer’ and ‘valour’ respectively. In the Atharvaveda III. 19.1 both words occur and probably mean the same thing as in á¹šg. I. 157.2. In some Vedic works {{brahma}} and {{ká¹£atra}} stand collectively for {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}14 and {{ká¹£atriyas}} (e. g. {{Tait. Br.|Tait. Br.}} II. 7.18, {{Bá¹›. Up.|Bá¹›. Up.}} I. 4.11, {{Kathopaniá¹£ad|Kaá¹­hopaniá¹£ad}} I. 2. 24). The word {{ká¹£atriya}} is very frequently applied as an epithet to several gods; e. g. á¹šg. VII. 64. 2 and VIII. 25. 8 (in both to Mitra and Varuá¹‡a), á¹šg. VIII. 67. 1 (to {{Ä€dityas}}), á¹šg. X. 66.8 (to gods in general). In some verses {{ká¹£atriya}} means ‘a king or a nobleman’; e. g. á¹šg. IV. 42.1 ’the kingdom on both sides (heaven and earth) belongs to me, who am a {{ká¹£atriya}} and who holds sway over all living persons, so that all the immortals (gods) are ours (on my side)’;15 á¹šg. X. 109.3 (= Atharva V. 17.3) ’the domain of the {{ká¹£atriya}} has been protected (from the sight of enemies)’. The word ‘{{rÄjanya}}’ occurs in the á¹šgveda only in the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}}. It occurs in the sense of {{ká¹£atriya}} in the Atharvaveda V. 17.9. The same remark applies to the word {{ká¹£atriya}} as to {{brÄhmang|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. It is difficult to say whether {{ká¹£atriyas}} were so by birth in the times of the hymns of the á¹šg. or were only a class more or less fluid. We find that the á¹šg. speaks of {{DevÄpi}} as the {{purohita}} of Åšantanu who became a king. The story is16 that both were sons of {{á¹šá¹£á¹­iá¹£ena}} and that Åšantanu, though a younger brother, became king as {{DevÄpi}} was not willing to be a king. The result was a famine due to Åšantanu’s transgression and so {{DevÄpi}} performed a sacrifice to induce rainfall. This shows that out of two brothers one became a king and the other a {{purohita}}. So kings and {{purohitas}} did not depend on birth. In á¹šg. IX. 112. 3 a poet exclaims ‘I am a reciter of hymns my father is a physician and my mother grinds (corn) with stones. We desire to obtain wealth in various actions’.17 In á¹šg. III. 44.5 the poet wistfully asks Indra ‘O, Indra, fond of soma, would you make me the protector of people, or would you make me a king, would you make me a sage, that has drunk of soma, would you impart to me endless wealth?’ This shows that the same man could be a {{á¹›á¹£i}} or a noble or a king. Dr. Ghurye (in ‘Caste and race in India’ p. 44) thinks that the {{ká¹£atriyas}} had become a compact body and he particularly relies on á¹šg. VII. 104. 13 (= Atharvaveda VIII. 4. 13) which is cited in the Vedic Index (vol. I, p. 207) for the same purpose. That verse literally means ‘Soma does not urge on the crooked one, nor the {{ká¹£atriya}} who bears {{false|false.}} He strikes the {{raká¹£as}}, and strikes him who speaks falsely; both lie in the bonds of Indra’. The words ‘{{ká¹£atriyam mithuyÄ dhÄrayantam}}’ are explained by {{SÄyaá¹‡a}} as ‘{{Ká¹£atriya}} who bears false words’. The 2nd half is only an expansion of the first half and so ‘{{vá¹›jina}}’ corresponds to ‘{{raká¹£as}}’ and ‘{{asad vadantan|asad vadantam}}’ is only a paraphrase of ‘{{mithuyÄ dbÄrayantam|mithuyÄ dhÄrayantam}}’. They may mean this that one who is a {{ká¹£atriya}}, but has no strength as a {{ká¹£atriya}} should have, is at the mercy of Indra. Unless we project our notions of the later state of society and the caste system when considering this verse, it is hardly possible to hold that this verse indicates that it refers to persons making a false claim for entrance into a compact body of {{ká¹£atriyas}} by birth. Dr. Ghurye also says (p. 44) ‘The 2nd order in society, the {{ká¹£atriya}}, is known in the earlier portions of the á¹šgveda as {{rÄjanya}}.’ I have not been able to find the word {{rÄjanya}} in the á¹šgveda any where except in the {{Puruá¹£a-sÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}}. In the {{Aitareya brÄhmaá¹‡a|Aitareya-brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (chap. 34. 2) the word ‘{{rÄjanya}}’ stands for a member of the 2nd class in society, while {{ká¹£atriya}} means a king of whom land (for sacrifice to gods) is asked for by a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{rÄjanya}} or {{vaiÅ›ya}}. [[27]] [[28]] [[29]] [[30]] [[31]]

Though the word {{vaiÅ›ya}} occurs in the á¹šgveda only in the {{PurugasÅ«kta|Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}}, the word ‘{{viÅ›}}’ is very frequently employed in it. It generally means ‘people’ or ‘group of people’. In a large number of cases we have the words {{mÄnuá¹£Ä«r-viÅ›aá¸¥}} or {{mÄnuá¹£iá¹‡u viká¹£u|mÄnuá¹£Ä«á¹£u viká¹£u}} or {{mÄnuá¹£iá¹‡Äm viÅ›Äm|mÄnuá¹£Ä«á¹‡Äá¹ viÅ›Äm}}, e.g. á¹šg. III.5.3, III.6.3, III. 11.5 (invincible Agni goes in front of human groups), IV. 6. 7 and 8, IV.9.2, V. 1. 9, V. 8.3, VI. 48. 8, VI. 47. 16 ({{viÅ›o mÄnuá¹£yÄn}}), X. I. 4, X. 69. 9. In some places we have ‘{{dÄsir viÅ›aá¸¥}}’ (the {{dÄsa}} hordes) e. g. IV. 28.4, VI. 25.2. In18 á¹šg. III. 34.2 we have ‘Oh Indra, you are the leader of human hordes as well as of divine hordes’ (where we have {{daivÄ«nÄá¹ viÅ›Äm}}). á¹šg.19 VIII. 63.7 (when loud invocations were sent towards Indra by the people consisting of five groups) shows that ‘{{viÅ›}}’ means all the Aryan people. In á¹šg. V. 32. 11 Indra is styled ‘{{pÄÃ±cajanya}}’ (favourable to the five people) and in IX. 66. 20 Agni has the appellation {{paÃ±cajanyaá¸¥ purohitab|paÃ±cajanyaá¸¥ purohitaá¸¥}} (the {{purohita}} of the five peoples). Sometimes {{jana}} and {{viÅ›}} seem to be contradistinguished as in á¹šg. II. 26. 3 ({{sa ij-janena sa viÅ›Ä sa janmanÄ sa putrair-vÄjam bharate dhana ná¹›bhiá¸¥}}). But the very fact that {{viÅ›}} is qualified also as {{pÄÃ±cajanya}} shows that there was hardly any distinction between {{jana}} and {{viÅ›}}. In the á¹šgveda frequent reference is made to five people ({{paÃ±ca janaá¸¥}}); vide á¹šg. III. 37. 9, III. 59.7, VI. 11. 4, VIII. 32. 22, X. 65.23, X. 45.6. In the same way the words {{ká¹›á¹£á¹­i}} (II. 2. 10, IV.38.10), {{ká¹£iti}} (V. 35. 2, VI. 48. 7, VII. 75. 4), {{cará¹£aá¹‡i}} (V.86.2, VII. 15.2) are employed along with {{paÃ±ca}}. We have also ‘{{pÄÃ±cajanyÄsu ká¹›á¹£á¹­iá¹£u}}’ in á¹šg. III. 53.16. So it is clear that {{viÅ›}} cannot in almost all hymns of the á¹šg. mean ‘{{vaiÅ›ya}}’ but means ’the people’ or ‘{{Ärgan|Ä€ryan}} people’ when no epithet like ‘{{dÄsiá¸¥}}’ or ‘{{daiviá¸¥}}’ is prefixed. The {{Ait. Br.|Ait. Br.}} (1. 26) says that ‘{{viÅ›aá¸¥}}’ means ‘{{rÄá¹£á¹­rÄá¹‡i}}’ (countries) when explaining á¹šg. IV. 50.8 ({{tasmai viÅ›aá¸¥ svayam-evÄnamante}}).

The word ‘{{dÄsa}}’ in later literature means ‘a serf or a slave.’ It follows that the {{dÄsa}} tribes that we see opposed to the {{Ä€ryas}} in the á¹šgveda were gradually {{vonquished|vanquished}} and were then made to serve the {{Ä€ryas}}. In the {{Manusmá¹£ti|Manusmá¹›ti}} (VIII. 413) the {{Å›Å«dra}} is said to have been created by God for service ({{dÄsya}}) of the {{brÄhmapa|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}.21 We find in the {{Tai. S.|Tait. S.}}, the {{Tai. Br.|Tait. Br.}} and other BrÄhmaá¹‡a works that the {{Å›Å«dra}} occupies the same position that he does in the {{smÅ›tis|smá¹›tis}}. Therefore it is reasonable to infer that the {{dÄsa}} or {{dasyus}} conquered by the {{Ä€ryas}} were gradually transformed into the {{Å›Å«dras}}. From being enemies they were brought into friendly relations and given a very subordinate position. Traces are visible even in the á¹šgveda that friendly relations had begun to be established between certain {{dÄsas}} and the priests. For example in á¹šg. VIII. 46. 32 we read ’the singer took a hundred (cows or other gifts) from the {{dÄsa BalbÅ«tha}} and from Taruká¹£a.’ In the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} (X. 90.12) the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya}} and {{Å›Å«dra}} are said to have sprung from the mouth, arms, thighs and feet of the supreme {{Puruá¹£a}}. In the very next verse the sun and the moon are said to have been born from the eye and mind of the {{Puruá¹£a}}. This shows that the composer of the hymn regarded the division of society into four classes to be very ancient and to be as natural and God-ordained as the sun and the moon.

We shall now see what position was assigned to the {{Å›Å«dra}} in the Vedic Saá¹hitÄs and {{BrÄbmapas|BrÄhmaá¹‡as}}. In the á¹šgveda the {{Ä€rya}} is contradistinguished from the men of dark skin. In the dharmasÅ«tras we find the {{ÅšÅ«dras}} spoken of as ‘dark {{vará¹‡a}}’. Vide Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 9. 27. 11 which is the same as Baudh. Dh. S. II. 1. 59.22 ‘The {{Å›Å«dra}} among men and the horse among beasts. Therefore those two, the horse and the {{Å›Å«dra}}, are the conveyances of beings; therefore the {{Å›Å«dra}} is not fit (or ordained) for sacrifice’ ({{Tai. S.|Tait. S.}} VII. 1. 1. 6).23 This shows that the {{Å›Å«dra}} could not perform the Vedic sacrifices and that he was employed for carrying persons in a palanquin or otherwise. In {{Tai. S.|Tait. S.}} V. 7. 6. 3-4 we have ‘put light (glory) in our {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, put it in our chiefs (or kings), (put) light in {{vaiÅ›yas}} and {{Å›Å«dras}}, put light in me by your light.’24 This is a sure indication that the {{Å›Å«dra}} who took the place of the {{dÄsa}} is here placed on the same level with the other three classes in the matter of the receipt of light from God and that far from being looked upon as an enemy, he had come to be looked upon as a member of the society (though the lowest in the scale). ‘The {{Å›Å«dra}} is a moving burial ground; therefore one should not study the Veda in the vicinity of a {{Å›Å«dra}}.’25 ‘He created the {{brÄh inana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with GÄyatrÄ«, the {{rÄjanya}} with Triá¹£á¹­ubh and the {{vaiÅ›ya}} with JagatÄ«; but he did not create the {{Å›Å«dra}} with any metre.’26 The {{TÄá¹‡á¸yamahÄbrÄhmaá¹‡a}} says27 ‘Therefore a {{Å›Å«dra}}, though he may have many beasts, is not entitled to perform sacrifice, he is godless as no deity was created after him (as in the case of the other {{vará¹‡as}}); therefore he does not go beyond washing the feet (of the three other {{vará¹‡as}}), since he was created from the feet’ (this last being an allusion to the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} X. 90.12 ‘{{padbhyÄá¹ Å›Å«dro ajÄyata}}’). This shows that the {{Å›Å«dra}}, however rich in cattle he might be, had to perform the menial duty of washing the feet of {{dvijas}}. The {{Åšatapatha Br.}} says that the {{Å›Å«dra}} is ‘untruth’ (S. B. E. vol. 44. p. 446), that the {{Å›Å«dra}} is ’toil’ (S. B. E. vol. 44 p. 410), and that a {{dÄ«ká¹£ita}} (one who was initiated for a Vedic sacrifice) was not to speak with a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} (S. B. E. vol. 26 p. 4). The Aitareya BrÄhmaá¹‡a remarks that ’the {{Å›Å«dra}}28 is at the beck and call of others (the three {{vará¹‡as}}), he can be made to rise at will, he can be beaten at will.’ When the father of {{Åšunaá¸¥Å›epa}}29 (who had sold {{Åšunaá¸¥Å›epa}} for 100 coins and had shown himself ready to kill him as a {{paÅ›u}} for another hundred coins) urged his son to come back to him after the boy had been taken into favour by Varuá¹‡a and ViÅ›vÄmitra, he contemptuously discards his father’s proposal with the words ‘one who commits an evil deed once may commit another sinful deed thereafter; you did not leave aside the {{Å›Å«dra}}’s line of conduct; you did what leaves no door for reconciliation.’ These passages show that the {{Å›Å«dra}}, though he had ceased to be an enemy of the {{Ärya}} and had been allowed to be within the pale of society, was looked down upon, was assigned a very low position, had to perform work of toil as a menial and was not allowed to perform Vedic sacrifices. A clear line of demarcation was kept between the {{Ä€rya}} and the {{ÅšÅ«dra}} in the times of the BrÄhmaá¹‡a works and even in the dharmasÅ«tras. The {{TÄá¹‡á¸ya BrÄhmaá¹‡a}}30 speaks of a mock fight ’the {{Å›Å«dra}} and {{Ärya}} fight on a hide, out of the two they so arrange that the {{Ärya}} colour becomes the victor’. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 1.3.40-41) says31 that a {{brahmacÄrin}}, if he cannot himself eat all the food he has brought by begging, may keep it near an {{Ärya}} (for his use) or he may give it to a {{Å›Å«dra}} who is a {{dÄsa}} (of his teacher). The same DharmasÅ«tra32 (II. 2.3. 1 and 4) says ‘{{Ä€ryas}} who are pure (by bath) should prepare the food for VaiÅ›vadeva; … or {{Å›Å«dras}} supervised by {{Ä€ryas}} should prepare it’. Similarly Gautama X. 69 uses the word ‘{{anÄrya}}’ for {{Å›Å«dra}} and Gautama 12.3 prescribes heavy punishment for a {{Å›Å«dra}} having sexual intercourse with an {{Ärya}} woman. Jaimini in his {{PÅ«rvamÄ«mÄá¹ƒsÄ-sÅ«tra}} (VI. 1.25-38) establishes after elaborate discussion that the {{Å›Å«dra}} has no {{adhikÄra}} for the performance of Agnihotra and Vedic sacrifices. It is, however, somewhat gratifying to find that at least one {{ÄcÄrya}}, BÄdari, espoused the cause of the {{Å›Å«dra}} and propounded the view that all (including {{Å›Å«dras}}) were entitled to perform Vedic sacrifices (VI. 1.27). In the VedÄnta-sÅ«tras also (I. 3.34-38) it is established that the {{Å›Å«dra}} has no {{adhikÄra}} for {{brahmavidyÄ}} based on Veda study, though some {{Å›Å«dras}} like Vidura might have been endowed with the knowledge of {{brahma}} owing to {{saá¹skÄras}} of former births. In the {{smá¹›ti}} literature, however, a few passages are found allowing marriages of {{Äryas}} with {{Å›Å«dra}} women (which will be discussed later on). Similarly sexual relations (illicit) between a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and a man of higher {{vará¹‡a}} are alluded to even in the {{Saá¹hitÄs}} e. g. {{Tai. S.|Tait. S.}}33 VII. 4.19.3 ‘when a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman has an {{Ärya}} as her paramour she does not seek wealth for the prosperity (of her relations)’. In Ait. Br. (8. 1) there is the story of {{Kavaá¹£a AilÅ«á¹£a}}, who was driven out from the sacrifice on the SarasvatÄ« with the words ‘Oh, son of a female slave, you are a rogue and not a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}; how did you take the {{dÄ«ká¹£Ä}} (initiation) as one of us’ and they carried him off to a sandy desert with the idea that he might die of thirst there. He when tormented by thirst ‘saw’ the hymn á¹šg. X. 30 and SarasvatÄ« came rushing to him.34 Further discussion about the {{Å›Å«dra}} and his disabilities will follow in another section later. [[32]] [[33]] [[34]] [[35]]

The position of the three {{vará¹‡as}} inter se (called collectively {{Ärya}}) now requires consideration. It is clear that the {{Saá¹hitÄs}} other than the {{á¹šgveda}} and {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} works show that the three classes of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, {{ká¹£atriyas}} and {{vaiÅ›yas}} had {{bocome|become}} {{differ entiated|differentiated}} and their privileges, duties and liabilities had become more or less fixed in those times.

Varna: brÄhmaá¹‡as

[[36]] In {{á¹šg.}} IV. 50.8 we read that king alone who places {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} first (i. e. honours him) dwells happy in his house, for him the earth always remains prosperous and to him all the people (or kingdoms) bow down of their own accord’. ‘{{BrÄhmaá¹‡as}}35 are gods that are directly seen’ ({{Tait. S.}} I. 7.3.1); ’there are two kinds of gods; for indeed the gods are gods and the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} who have studied and teach the sacred lore are the human gods’ {{Åšat. Br.}} (S. B. E. vol. 12 p. 309, vol. 26 p. 341). In the Atharvaveda V. 17.19 there is an assertion of the pre-eminence of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and the consequences of harming them or their cows. Therefore the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is the foremost’ ({{Tait. S.}} II. 6.2.5, V.2.7.1). Therefore the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} shows his might by his mouth, since he was created from the mouth’ ({{TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br.}} XI. 1. 2).36 In the {{Ait. Br.}} (33. 4) Varuá¹‡a, when he was told that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} boy would be offered in place of the son of the king {{HariÅ›candra}}, is made to say ‘a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is indeed preferable to a {{ká¹£atriya}}’. The mere fact of birth as {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}’s son is represented here as giving to the boy pre-eminence over a king’s son. On the other hand the {{Åšat. Br.}} says (V. 1. 1. 12) ‘a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}37 is not adequate to (competent to manage) a kingdom’. In the {{Tait. Br.}} it is said that playing on the {{vÄ«á¹‡Ä}} (in the {{AÅ›vamedha}}) is to be done by a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{rÄjanya}} (and not by two {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}), since wealth does not find delight in the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. The {{Åšatapatha BrÄhmaá¹‡a}}38 (XI. 5. 7.1) lays emphasis on the four peculiar attributes of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} viz. {{brÄhmaá¹‡ya}} (purity of parentage as a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}), {{pratirÅ«pacaryÄ}} (befitting deportment or conduct), {{yaÅ›as}} (glory) and {{lokapakti}} (the teaching or perfecting of people). “When the people are being perfected or taught by him, people endow him with four privileges viz. {{arcÄ}} (honour), {{dÄna}} (gifts), {{ajyeyatÄ}} (freedom from being harassed) and {{avadhyatÄ}} (freedom from being beaten). The {{Åšatapatha}} (V. 4. 6. 9) expressly mentions that {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{rÄjanya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya}} and {{Å›Å«dra}} are the four {{Vará¹‡as}}. Teaching had become so much associated with the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} that when the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a GÄrgya}} approached king {{AjÄtaÅ›atru}} for the knowledge of {{brahma}}, the latter replied â€œthis is {{oontrary|contrary}} to the natural order that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} should approach a {{ká¹£atriya}} with the idea ‘he ({{ká¹£atriya}}) will propound to me {{brahma}}…’” In a separate section below all the privileges enjoyed by the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} will be set out at one place.39 [[38]]

Varna: ká¹£atriyas

[[39]] The position of {{ká¹£atriyas}} and their relation to the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} falls to be considered now. First of all with reference to the king several important passages may be cited. In some cases ‘{{rÄjan}}’ means only a ’noble’ or ‘chief’, as in {{á¹šg.}} X. 42. 10, X. 97. 6. In many passages ‘{{rÄjan}}’ means ‘king’. The government often seems to have been tribal, such tribes as {{Yadus}}, {{Turvabas|TurvaÅ›as}}, {{Druhyus}}, Anus and {{PÅ«rus}} ({{á¹šg.}} I. 108. 8) being frequently mentioned (vide {{á¹šg.}} VII. 18. 6 for {{Bhá¹›gus}} and {{Druhyus}} and VII. 18. 7 for {{Tá¹›tsus}}, VIII. 6. 46 for {{Yadvas|Yadus}}). Whether kingship was by election it is not necessary here to discuss. The king was regarded as keeping the people within bounds. When a king was crowned, it was thought that ‘a {{ká¹£atriya}} was produced, a lord of all beings, the defender of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} (or of holy texts) and of {{dharma}}’.41 The {{Åšat. Br.}} says ‘for these two ({{Å›rotriya}} and king) are the upholders of the sacred law among men (S. B. E. vol. 41 p. 106).42 That the co-operation between {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and {{ká¹£atriya}} results in glory and success is frequently emphasized e. g. *therefore a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} must certainly be approached by a {{ká¹£atriya}} who is about to perform some act, for indeed that act of a {{ká¹£atriya}} which is sped on by {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} succeeds ({{Åšatapatha}}, S. B. E. vol. 26 pp. 270-271). The {{purohita}} of a {{ká¹£atriya}} came to occupy a very high position. The {{Åšatapatha}} (S. B. E. vol. 26 p. 270) lays emphasis on the importance of the {{purohita}} and cautions a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} against being the {{purohita}} of any king he meets with and adds that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} may remain without a king, but a king should not be without a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. Even the gods required a {{purohita}}, as the {{Tait. S.}} II. 5. 1. 1. says ‘{{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}} son of {{Tvaá¹£á¹­á¹›}} was the {{purobita|purohita}} of gods’.44 {{Åšaá¹‡á¸a}} and {{Marka}} were the {{purohitas}} of the Asuras ({{KÄá¹­haka S.}} IV. 4). Agni is often called {{purohita}} ({{á¹šg.}} I. 1. 1., I. 44. 10, III. 2. 8). “May we, {{purohitas}}, be awake in the kingdom (for its welfare)’ says the {{Tait. S.}} (I. 7. 10.1). The {{Ait. Br.}} (chap. 34.8) says45 ’the {{purohita}} is half the soul of the {{ká¹£atriya}}’ and contains an eulogy of {{purohita}} in the following words (40.1) “for indeed the gods do not eat the food of a king who has no {{purohita}}; therefore a king when about to offer a sacrifice should have a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} as his {{purohita}} with the idea ‘may the gods eat my food.’” The combination of the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and the {{rÄjanya}} is said to be most desirable and that it is conducive to the eminence of both is declared in the {{Tait. S.}} (V. 1. 10. 3)46. Therefore a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} who is supported by a {{rÄjanya}} is superior to another {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (not so supported), hence a {{rÄjanya}} who has a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (to help him) is superior to another {{rÄjanya}}’. The {{Åšatapatha}} (V. 4. 4. 15) declares ’that king indeed who is not powerful to the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} (i. e. who is humble before the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}) becomes more powerful than his foes.’47 It is not to be supposed that this attainment of the supreme position by {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} was an affair of plain sailing. Sometimes {{ká¹£atriyas}} claimed higher position and also paid scant respect to {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. In the {{Åšatapatha}} we read ‘whence the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is an object of respect after the king’ (S. B. E. vol. 41, p. 96); ‘hence the people here serve, from a lower position, the {{ká¹£atriya}} seated above them’ {{Åšatapatha}} (S. B. E. vol. 12 p. 94); ‘hence when a nobleman approaches, all these people, the subjects crouch down by him on the ground’ {{Åšatapatha}} (S. B. E. vol. 26 p. 228); ’therefore there is nothing higher than the {{ká¹£atra}}; therefore the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} sits down below the {{ká¹£atriya}} in the {{rÄjasÅ«ya}}’ ({{Åšatapatha}} 14. 4. 1. 23 = {{Bá¹›. Up.}} I. 4. 11). In the Atharvaveda several {{verges|verses}} occur which declare the harm that results from disrespecting or injuring a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} or from robbing him of his cow. “He who regards {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} as food drinks of the poison of TaimÄta; he who injures a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, the relative of the gods, does not attain to the world of {{pitá¹›s}} (Atharvaveda V. 18. 4 and 13). Those who spat on a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} or those who sent to him mucous remain biting the hair in the midst of a stream by their mouth; that kingdom sinks as water {{sank|sinks}} a shattered vessel, where they injure a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}; that wicked act strikes that kingdom’ (Atharvaveda V. 19. 3 and 8).48

The stories of king {{KÄrtavÄ«rya}} and {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}} who respectively carried off the cows of {{Jamadagni}} and {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} narrated in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} ({{ÅšÄnti}} 49 for {{KÄrtavÄ«rya}}; {{Ä€di.}} 175 for {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}}) and the {{PurÄá¹‡as}} show how several kings were high-handed and treated {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} with no respect whatever. It appears that even the wives of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} were not quite safe at the hands of kings. ‘The chamberlain ({{ká¹£attá¹›}}) of that king in whose kingdom the wife of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is thoughtlessly put under restraint does not march in front of cooking vessels with a golden ornament on his {{neok|neck}}’49 (Atharva V. 17. 14). The somewhat mystical hymn ({{á¹šg.}} X. 109) where ‘{{brahmajÄyÄ}}’ figures prominently probably hints at the same fact (viz. the kidnapping of the wives of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}).

The {{Tait. S.}} says that ’the {{vaiÅ›ya}} indeed sacrifices, being desirous of cattle’ and that the gods having been defeated were reduced to the condition of being the {{vaiÅ›yas}} or ‘{{viÅ›}}’ of asuras’;50 ’the {{vaiÅ›ya}} among men, cows among beasts, therefore they are to be enjoyed (to be eaten, to be subsisted upon) by others; they were produced from the receptacle of food; therefore they exceed others in numbers’.51 The {{Tait. Br.}} says ’the {{vaiÅ›ya}} class is said to have been born from {{pk|á¹›k}} verses, they declare the {{Yajurveda}} as the origin of the {{ká¹£atriya}}, the {{SÄmaveda}} is indeed the source of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}’.52 The same {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} further says ’the {{viÅ›}} go away from (reside separately from) the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and {{ká¹£atriyas}}’. The {{TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br.}} says53 ‘Hence the {{vaiÅ›ya}}, though being eaten (i. e. subsisted upon) by others is not exhausted, since he was created from the {{prajanana}} (from the sexual parts of {{PrajÄpati}}); therefore he has numerous cattle, he has all the gods (as his patrons) and was produced with the {{JagatÄ«}} metre, his season is the rains, therefore he is to be eaten by the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and the {{rÄjanya}}, since he was created as lower (than those two classes)’. The {{Åšat. Br.}} (S. B. E. vol. 26 p. 335) says ‘He thus assigns to the Maruts a share therein after Indra, whereby he makes the people ({{viÅ›}}) subservient and obedient to the nobility’.54 According to the {{Ait. Br.}} 35, 3 the {{vaiÅ›ya}} is one who is the food of others, who pays taxes to others. These passages show that {{vaiÅ›yas}} were entitled to sacrifice, reared cattle, were far more numerous than the other two classes, they had to bear the brunt of taxation, they lived apart from {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and {{ká¹£atriyas}} and were obedient to them.

The system of the four {{vará¹‡as}} had taken such deep roots in the period when the {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} works were composed, that we often meet with Vedic passages saying that it extended even to the gods, Agni and {{Bá¹›haspati}} being the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} among gods; Indra, Varuá¹‡a, Soma, Yama being the {{ká¹£atriyas}}; Vasus, {{Rudras}}, the {{ViÅ›ve Devas}} and Maruts being the {{viÅ›}}, and {{PÅ«á¹£an}} being the {{Å›Å«dra}}.55 Similarly it is said ’the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is really spring, the {{ká¹£atriya}} summer, and the {{viÅ›}} are the rainy season.’

Various crafts in the Vedas

That there were other professions and crafts with specific names (which in later times at least became castes) even in the times of the {{Saá¹hitÄs}} is quite clear. The {{á¹šg.}} speaks of {{vaptÄ}} (barber) in X. 142.4, {{taá¹£á¹­Ä}} (a carpenter or maker of chariots) in {{á¹šg.}} I. 61.4, VII. 32.20, IX. 112.1, {{X119.5|X. 119. 5}}; {{tvaá¹£á¹­á¹›}} (a carpenter) in VIII. 102.8; {{bhiá¹£ak}} (medicine man) in IX. 112.1 and 3, {{karmÄra}} or {{kÄrmÄra}} (iron-smith) in X. 72.2 and IX. 112.2, {{carmamna}} (tanner) in {{á¹šg.}} VIII. 5.38.

The Atharvaveda mentions {{rathakÄra}} (III. 5.6), {{karmÄra}} (III. 5.6) and {{sÅ«ta}} (III. 5.7). In the {{Tait. S.}} (IV. 5.4.2)56 mention is made of {{ká¹£attá¹›}} (royal chamberlain or doorkeeper), {{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}} (treasurer), {{taká¹£an}} (carpenter) and {{rathakÄra}} (maker of chariots), {{kulÄla}} (potter), {{karmÄra}}, {{puÃ±jiá¹£á¹­a}} (fowler), {{niá¹£Äda}}, {{iá¹£uká¹›t}} (maker of arrows), {{dhanvaká¹›t}} (maker of bows), {{má¹›gayu}} (hunter) and {{Å›vanin}} (those who lead packs of hounds). These also occur in the {{VÄjasaneyi-saá¹hitÄ}} 16.26-28 and these and a few more in {{VÄj. S.}} 30.5-13 and in {{KÄá¹­haka Saá¹hitÄ}} 17.13. In the {{Tait. Br.}} (III. 4.1 and the following {{anuvÄkas}} that deal with {{Puruá¹£amedha}}) we have {{dyogÅ«}}, {{mÄgadha}} (bard), {{sÅ«ta}}, {{Å›ailÅ«á¹£a}} (actor), {{rebha}}, {{bhasmala}}, {{rathakÄra}}, {{taká¹£an}}, {{kaulÄla}}, {{karmÄra}}, {{maá¹‡ikÄra}}, {{vapa}} (sower or barber), {{iá¹£ukÄra}}, {{dhanvakÄra}}, {{jyÄkÄra}} (maker of bow-string), {{rajjusarga}}, {{má¹›gayu}}, {{Å›vanin}}, {{SurÄkÄra}} (vintner), {{ayastÄpa}} (heater of iron or copper), {{kitava}} (gambler), {{bidalakÄra}} (worker in wicker-work), {{kaá¹‡á¹­akakÄra}}. The {{Åšatapatha Br.}} XI. 8.1. speaks of {{kaulÄla-cakra}} (the potter’s wheel). The {{Tait. Br.}} III. 8.5, mentions also {{rÄjaputra ugra}}. The {{Åšatapatha}} (S. B. E. Vol. 44 p. 397) speaks of {{Marutta Ä€viká¹£ita}} as an {{Äyogava}} king. This latter is a {{pratiloma}} caste according to the {{dharmaÅ›Ästras}} (vide below). Whether in the {{Åšatapatha}} that word has the same sense is doubtful. The {{Tait. S.}} I. 8.9.1-2 mentions among the ‘{{ratnas}}’ (the jewels), the {{sÅ«ta}}, {{grÄmaá¹‡i}}, {{ká¹£attá¹›}}, {{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}}, {{bhÄgadugha}} (collector of taxes), {{aká¹£ÄvÄpa}} (superintendent of gambling). Vide also {{Tait. Br.}} I. 7.3. In the {{TÄá¹‡á¸ya BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} (19.1.4) it is said that ’eight brave persons hold up the kingdom viz. the king’s brother, the king’s son, the {{purohita}}, the crowned queen, the {{sÅ«ta}}, the {{grÄmaá¹‡Ä«}}, {{ká¹£attá¹›}} and {{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}}’. Therefore it looks likely that {{ká¹£attá¹›}} and {{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}} were high officers of state and not castes. In the {{Åšat. Br.}} XIII. 4. 1. 5 among the guards of the horse let loose in the {{AÅ›vamedha}} there were a hundred sons of {{ká¹£Ättá¹›-saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›s}} carrying clubs’. As the word ‘{{ká¹£Ättra}}’ is prefixed to ‘{{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}}’ it follows that ‘{{saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›s}}’ were officers who may have belonged to any {{vará¹‡a}}. In the same passage mention is made of a hundred guardians who were the sons of {{sÅ«tagrÄmaá¹‡is}}. In another passage of the same {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (XIII. 2.2.18) the {{sÅ«tas}} and {{grÄmaá¹‡is}} are said to be no kings and yet are {{rÄjaká¹›t}} (i. e. king-makers).57 This means probably that they are the principal persons on whose support the king depends. The {{Åšat. Br.}} V. 4. 4.15-19 arranged {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, king, king’s brother, {{sÅ«ta}} or {{sthapati}}, {{grÄmaá¹‡i}}, {{sajÄta}} in a descending scale of powerfulness. So the {{sÅ«ta}} appears to have been originally an important officer. It is hardly possible to say with assurance that all these had become petrified into castes in the modern sense, particularly when several persons associated with these in the {{VÄj. S.}} and elsewhere were not castes such as the thief ({{taskara}}), the impotent ({{klÄ«ba}}), humpbacked ({{kubja}}), dwarf ({{vÄmana}}); but most of the avocations and crafts referred to above have corresponding castes and subcastes for hundreds of years. It is therefore possible to say that in the times of the {{Saá¹hitÄs}} and {{BrÄhmaá¹‡as}} these were groups founded on occupations that had become castes or were in process of developing into castes. The {{TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br.}} speaks of {{KirÄtas}} (who were and are non-Aryan and were aboriginal tribes).58 The {{VÄj. S.}} (30. 17) speaks of {{Paulkasa}} in connection with {{bÄ«bhatsÄ}} (nauseating filth) and of {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} (in 30.21) in connection with {{vÄyu}} (wind). The {{paulkasa}} and {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} occur in {{Tait. Br.}} (III. 4. 14 and III. 4. 17 respectively). In the {{ChÄndogya Up.}} (V. 10.7)59 the {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} is ranked with the dog and the boar. “Therefore even if one knowing thus were to give the leavings of his food to a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} that would in his case be an offering made into the Self as Fire’-{{ChÄndogya}} V. 24. 4. This shows that the {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} was the lowest in the social scale. ‘Just as a {{dÄsya}} or {{vaideha}}, or the son of an {{ugra}}, after having made his bow strung &c.’ –{{Bá¹›. Up.}} III. 8.2. In the {{Bá¹›. Up.}} IV. 3.22 mention is made of both {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} and {{paulkasa}} and in IV. 3.37 it is said60 that ‘just as when a king pays a visit, the {{ugras}}, {{pratyenasas}} (thief catchers), the {{sÅ«tas}} and headmen of the village make arrangements for him with food and drink and with pavilions.” Here {{ugras}} seem to be a group of nobles subordinate to the king. In later literature {{ugra}} is the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman ({{YÄj.}} I. 91). In the {{á¹šg.}} X. 97, 12 the word {{ugra}} occurs “You destroy disease just as an {{ugra}} who is a mediator or arbitrator (removes dispute).” What {{ugra}} means here cannot be said with certainty. It may only mean a ‘formidable chief or king.’

The {{rathakÄra}} and {{niá¹£Äda}} deserve a passing notice. The {{Tait. Br.}} I. 1. 4 after stating that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} should consecrate sacred fires in the spring, the {{ká¹£atriya}} in summer, the {{vaiÅ›ya}} in autumn, ordains that the {{rathakÄra}} should consecrate sacred fires in the rainy season. The question arises whether the {{rathakÄra}} is a member of the three higher castes who has taken in economic distress to the profession of making chariots or is a person belonging to a caste other than the three higher {{vará¹‡as}}. Jaimini in his {{PÅ«rvamÄ«mÄá¹sÄ-sÅ«tra}} (VI. 1. 44-50) discusses this question and establishes61 that the {{rathakÄra}} is a member of a caste other than the three higher {{vará¹‡as}}, that he has on account of the express words in the {{Å›ruti}} the privilege to consecrate sacred fires with vedic mantras, that the mantra for the consecration of {{rathakÄras}} is ‘{{á¹›bhÅ«á¹‡Äá¹ tvÄ}}’ ({{Tait. Br.}} I. 1. 4) and that the {{rathakÄras}} are the caste called {{Saudhanvana}} which is neither {{Å›Å«dra}} nor one of the three higher ones, but is slightly inferior to the three higher {{vará¹‡as}}. {{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}}62 (on {{YÄj.}} I. 10) notices that in some {{smá¹›ti}} the {{rathakÄra}} though not belonging to the three higher {{vará¹‡as}}, was allowed the privilege of {{upanayana}}, but adds that this dictum of the {{smá¹›ti}} is due to mistake, it being misled by the fact that he is allowed the privilege of {{ÄdhÄna}}63 (consecration of sacred fires). In modern times the members of the carpenter caste in certain parts of the Deccan at least are in the habit of performing the {{upanayana}} and wearing the sacred thread.

With reference to an {{iá¹£á¹­i}} offered to Rudra a Vedic text says ‘one should make a {{niá¹£Ädasthapati}} perform this {{iá¹£á¹­i}}.’ The {{PÅ«rvamÄ«mÄá¹sÄ sÅ«tra}}64 (VI. 1, 51-52) discusses the question whether this authorises a {{niá¹£Äda}} who is himself a chieftain or a chieftain (who is a member of the three higher {{vará¹‡as}}) of {{niá¹£Ädas}}. The established conclusion is that the {{iá¹£á¹­i}} is to be performed by a {{niá¹£Äda}} who is a chieftain though he be beyond the pale of the three {{vará¹‡as}}, as the {{KarmadhÄraya}} compound is the proper way of dissolving the compound and not the genitive {{Tatpuruá¹£a}}. The {{Ait. Br.}} (37.7) says65 ‘just as the {{niá¹£Ädas}}, or {{selagas}} (thieves) or evil-doers seize a wealthy man in a forest and throwing him in a well run away with his wealth.’ The {{SÄá¹…khÄyana Br.}} (25. 15) allows one who had performed the {{ViÅ›vajit}} sacrifice (in which everything is given away) to stay in a settlement of {{niá¹£Ädas}} whose food is the lowest that he is allowed to take. The {{KÄtyÄyana-Å›rauta-sÅ«tra}} (I. 1. 12-14) says66 that the chieftain who is a {{niá¹£Äda}} can offer a {{caru}} of {{Gavedhuka}} corn to Rudra, but this offering is to be made in ordinary fire (and not in the fires consecrated with Vedic mantras) i. e. the permission to offer {{RaudrÄ« iá¹£á¹­i}} does not entitle him to perform Vedic consecration of fire ({{ÄdhÄna}}). But according to {{SatyÄá¹£Äá¸ha}}67 {{kalpa}} III. 1 both the {{niá¹£Äda}} and the {{rathakÄra}} are entitled to perform Agnihotra and {{DarÅ›a-pÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa}}.

The {{Aitareya BrÄhmaá¹‡a}}68 narrates that {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}} cursed his senior fifty sons, when they did not agree to his proposal to treat {{Åšunaá¸¥Å›epa}} ({{DevarÄta}}) as his son, that they would associate with the lowest castes and that they became the {{Andhras}}, {{Puá¹‡á¸ras}}, {{Åšabaras}}, {{Pulindas}} and {{MÅ«tibas}} who are among the lowest of society and are mostly composed of {{dasyus}}. It is probably owing to this legend that the {{Manusmá¹›ti}}69 (X. 43-45) is prepared to regard the {{Pauá¹‡á¸rakas}}, the {{Oá¸ras}}, {{Draviá¸as}}, {{KÄmbojas}}, {{Yavanas}}, {{Åšakas}}, {{PÄradas}}, {{Pahlavas}}, {{CÄ«nas}}, {{KirÄtas}}, {{Daradas}} and {{KhaÅ›as}} as being originally {{ká¹£atriya}} castes, but later on reduced to the position of {{Å›Å«dras}} by the non-performance of Vedic {{saá¹skÄras}} (like {{upanayana}}) and by the absence of contact with {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. Manu further adds that the various castes that are outside the (influence of the) four {{vará¹‡as}} are all known as {{dasyus}} whether they speak the language of {{Mlecchas}} or of {{Ä€ryas}}.

One very important question is whether the theory of the four {{vará¹‡as}} with their peculiar privileges and duties {{desoribed|described}} in the {{dharmaÅ›Ästras}} and other {{smá¹›tis}} was merely a theory even in the most ancient times. When the {{Puruá¹£asÅ«kta}} of the {{á¹šgveda}} speaks of {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{RÄjanya}}, {{VaiÅ›ya}} and {{ÅšÅ«dra}} or when the {{Åšatapatha BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} expressly says that they are four {{Vará¹‡as}}, it appears to me that they speak of facts existing in their times and not merely of a theory which was to be striven for as an ideal. {{Smá¹›ti}} writers try to place all their {{diota|dicta}} in the frame-work of the {{vará¹‡as}} because the four {{vará¹‡as}} and their duties and privileges had been more or less clearly defined in the times of the Vedas and {{BrÄhmaá¹‡as}}, which according to the authors of the {{smá¹›tis}} were {{Å›ruti}}, eternal and infallible. They tried to approximate the state of society existing in their times to the {{vará¹‡as}} which they held were of {{hoery|hoary}} antiquity. But there is nothing to show that the same difficulties were felt by the authors of the Vedic hymns and the {{BrÄhmaá¹‡as}}. The manner in which they refer to the privileges and disabilities of the several {{vará¹‡as}} have such a deep ring of actuality that one must concede that the {{vará¹‡as}} spoken of in them represented the real divisions of society at least to a very great extent, if not cent percent.

The preceding discussion renders the following propositions most probable;

(1) that in the earliest times about which we have literary records there were only two {{vará¹‡as}}, the {{Äryas}} and their opponents the {{dasyus}} or {{dÄsas}}; that the difference between the two was based on difference of colour and culture and was thus more or less racial and cultural; (2) that centuries before the {{saá¹hitÄ}} period closed the {{dasyus}} had been conquered and were given a position subservient to the {{Äryas}}; (3) that the {{Å›Å«dras}} were the {{dasyus}} so subjugated and made subservient; (4) that the spirit of exclusiveness and pride of superiority existing among the {{Ä€ryas}} with reference to {{dasyus}} soon extended to groups among the {{Äryas}} themselves; (5) that by the time of the {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} Literature, {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} (men supposed to be devoted to learning and priesthood), {{ká¹£atriyas}} (kings, noblemen and some warriors) and {{vaiÅ›yas}} (the artisans and common people) had become separated into groups more or less dependent on birth and that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}70 had come to be regarded as superior to the {{ká¹£atriya}} by the fact of birth; (6) that even such low castes as {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and {{paulkasas}} had been evolved long before the end of the Vedic period; (7) that owing to cultural advance, division of labour arose and numerous arts and crafts had been developed and they were in process of contributing to the complexity of the system by creating numerous sub-castes based upon occupations; (8) that besides the four {{vará¹‡as}} intermediate castes like the {{rathakÄra}} had been evolved; (9) that there were certain non-Aryan tribes which were supposed to have been originally {{ká¹£atriyas}} but fallen later on.

The close of the Vedic period is here taken as being not later than about 1000 B. C. How much earlier it may be placed it is not possible to say. [[48]]

List of professions and crafts from Vedic texts

The following is a list of persons engaged in professions and crafts, which had probably become castes or were in the process of becoming castes, before the close of the Vedic period, as seen from the {{VÄj. S.}}, {{Tait. S.}} and {{Tait. Br.}}, {{KÄá¹­haka Saá¹.}} (17. 13), the {{Atharva-veda}}, the {{TÄá¹‡á¸ya Br.}} (III. 4.), the {{Ait. Br.}}, the {{ChÄndogya}} and the {{Bá¹›. Up.}} The meaning of some of the words is not quite clear and it is possible that some in the list were not at all castes or professions. They are arranged in the alphabetical order of Sanskrit (though transliterated). The meanings of most of them have been given above. Where the meaning is doubtful a question mark has been made. [[49]]

{{AjÄpala}} (goatherd) {{DÄÅ›a}} {{Andhra}} {{Dhanuá¹£kÄra}} {{AyastÄpa}} or {{Ä€yogÅ«}} or {{Ä€gogÅ«}} {{DhanvakÄra}} {{AvipÄla}} (keeper of ewes) or {{Dhanvaká¹›t}} {{Iá¹£ukÄra}} {{Dhaivara}} {{Ugra}} {{Niá¹£Äda}} {{Kaá¹‡á¹­akakÄra}} or {{Kaá¹‡á¹­akÄ«}} {{PumÅ›calÅ«}} {{KarmÄra}} {{PuÃ±jiá¹£á¹­a}} {{KÄri}} (dancer?) or {{KirÄta}} {{Kitava}} {{PauÃ±jiá¹£á¹­ha}} {{KinÄÅ›a}} (cultivator?) {{Puá¹‡á¸ra}} {{KulÄla}} or {{KaulÄla}} {{Pulinda}} {{Keparta}} {{Paulkasa}} {{KoÅ›akÄrÄ«}} (blower of bellows) {{Bainda}} (catching fish in nets) {{Ká¹£attá¹›}} {{Bhiá¹£ak}} {{GopÄla}} {{Bhimala}} (timid?) {{Carmamna}} {{Maá¹‡ikÄra}} {{CÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} {{MÄgadha}} {{Jambhaka}} (?) {{MÄrgÄra}} {{JyakÄra}} {{MÅ«tiba}} {{Taká¹£an}} {{Má¹›gayu}} {{Mainala}} (catcher of fish?) {{VidalakÄrÄ«}} or {{Bidala}} {{Rajayitri}} (dyer) {{VrÄtya}} {{Rajusarga}} or-{{sarja}} {{Åšabara}} {{RathakÄra}} {{ÅšÄbalya}} (?) {{RÄjaputra}} {{ÅšailÅ«á¹£a}} {{Rebha}} (?) {{Åšvanin}} (or {{Åšvanita}}) {{Vaá¹Å›anartin}} {{Saá¹grahÄ«tá¹›}} {{Vapa}} (barber) {{SurÄkÄra}} {{Vaá¹‡ija}} {{SÅ«ta}} {{VÄsaá¸¥-palpÅ«lÄ«}} (a washer-woman) {{Selaga}} {{Hiraá¹‡yakÄra}}

Several centuries before the Christian era there were several castes. This follows not only from the {{dharmaÅ›Ästras}} but also from the ancient Buddhist works and from the meagre existing fragments of the work of Megasthenes on India. Though Megasthenes was confused in his statements about the caste system as prevalent in his day, some propositions are clearly deducible therefrom. He states (pp. 40 ff) that the whole population of India was divided into seven castes, (1) philosophers, (2) husbandmen, (3) neatherds and shepherds, (4) artisans, (5) military, (6) overseers, (7) councillors and assessors. Out of these 1 and 5 correspond to {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and {{ká¹£atriyas}}, 2-3 to {{vaiÅ›yas}}, 4 to {{Å›Å«dras}}; 6th and 7th correspond to {{adhyaká¹£as}} and {{amÄtyas}} (as in {{Kauá¹­ilya}}’s {{ArthaÅ›Ästra}}) and are not really castes but occupations. He probably regards them as castes because the offices of the {{adhyaká¹£as}} and {{amÄtyas}} were generally hereditary or at least he gathered that they were so. Megasthenes’ further statement (pp. 43-44) that no one is allowed to marry out of his own caste or to {{exeroise|exercise}} any calling or art except his own’ shows that the prevailing tendency was that caste had become exclusive and mostly occupational in his day, though there must have been exceptions of which he was not informed.

Varna and castes derived therefrom

The ancient writers on {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} strive very hard to account for the bewildering ramifications of the caste system from the four {{vará¹‡as}} that were spoken of in the {{Å›ruti}} (revelation). There is unanimity on the theory that the numerous castes actually found in the country arose from the unions of males of different {{vará¹‡as}} with women belonging to {{vará¹‡as}} differing from their own. The divergences (and they are many) among the several {{smá¹›tikÄras}} relate only to details. The {{smá¹›ti}} writers had before them the hoary theory of four {{Vará¹‡as}} vouched for by infallible Vedic authority, but they were at the same time quite cognizant of the realities in society viz. the separate existence of numerous castes and sub-castes that had varying claims as regards social status and that were based in popular estimation on birth alone. Therefore, when one criticizes the {{smá¹›ti}} view of the derivation of numerous castes from the mixture or confusion ({{saá¹…kara}}) of the four {{vará¹‡as}} as purely hypothetical and imaginary, the criticism is true only partially. The {{smá¹›tis}} were composed in different parts of India at different times and they were meant to supply a popular want, to guide the people and to reflect the prevailing state of society and popular feeling. Therefore, it must be admitted that the numerous castes mentioned in the {{smá¹›tis}} did exist at the time of the {{smá¹›tis}}, that the social status of the several castes might have varied from country to country or from epoch to epoch, that the peculiar avocations and means of livelihood assigned to the various castes reflected the real state of things. The element of hypothesis and speculation lies only in the theory of a particular sub-caste having sprung from the union of two persons belonging to two particular {{vará¹‡as}} or castes.71 This description of the origin of the several castes was only indicative of the author’s view or of the accepted view about the social status of those castes in particular localities. There is here, as a matter of fact, great divergence of views among the several authors.

In the first place all writers on {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} start with the propositions viz. (1) that the four {{vará¹‡as}}, {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{ká¹£atriya}}, {{vaiÅ›ya}} and {{Å›Å«dra}}, are arranged in a descending scale of social status; and (2) that marriage is or was permissible between a male of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} with a woman of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}, but the union of a woman of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} with a male of {{vará¹‡a}} lower than her own is reprehensible and not permitted. There is a third proposition advanced by many writers that a man belongs to a particular {{vará¹‡a}} or {{jÄti}} by birth only i. e. if born in lawful wedlock of parents both of whom belong to that {{vará¹‡a}} or {{jÄti}}.72 This is the view held by all medieval writers and digests and it is expressly said that a man belongs to a caste by birth and no actions of his can alter that fact, that several castes are like the species of animals and that caste attaches to the body and not to the soul (vide {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}}). When a male of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} marries a woman of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}, the marriage is said to be {{anuloma}} (lit. with the hair, in the natural order) and the offspring is said to be {{anuloma}}; when there is a union of a woman of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} with a male of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}, it is said to be {{pratiloma}} (against the hair, i. e. against the natural or proper order) and the children of the union are said to be {{pratiloma}}. These two words {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}} (as applied to marriage or progeny) hardly ever occur in the vedic literature. In the {{Bá¹›. Up.}} (II. 1. 15) and {{Kauá¹£Ä«takÄ« Br. Up.}} IV. 18 (quoted in note 89 above) the word ‘{{pratiloma}}’ is applied to the procedure adopted by a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} of going to a {{ká¹£atriya}} for knowledge about {{brahman}}. From this it may possibly be inferred that {{pratiloma}} and {{anuloma}} might have been employed with reference to marriages also (in the days of the {{Upaniá¹£ad}}). {{PÄá¹‡ini}} (IV. 4. 28) teaches the formation of words from {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}}. They occur in Gautama (IV. 14-15), {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 8. 8), {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} (18.7), Manu (X. 13), {{YÄj.}} (I. 95) and other {{smá¹›ti}} works.

One important question is whether the theory of {{anuloma}} or {{pratiloma}} castes presupposes a marriage or only a union (outside marriage) of a man and a woman. The {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} (II. 6. 13. 1, 3-4) lays down73 that a man must marry a virgin of his own {{vará¹‡a}} with the rites prescribed by the {{Å›Ästras}} and only the son born of such a marriage is entitled to the privileges and occupation of his father, that sexual intercourse with a woman who has been married by another or on whom the proper ceremonies of marriage are not performed or who belongs to another caste is condemned and that the son (and not the daughter) born of such an union is condemned (through the sin of the parents). So {{Ä€pastamba}} looked with disfavour even on marriages called {{anuloma}}. He is entirely silent about {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}} castes. It is no doubt true that most ancient writers like Gautama (IV. 1), {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} (I. 24), Manu (III. 12-13), {{YÄj.}} (I. 55 and 57) prescribe that a person should by preference marry a girl of his own {{vará¹‡a}} but also allow the marriage of a person with a girl of another {{vará¹‡a}} lower than his own. {{YÄj.}} (I.92) expressly says that the six {{anuloma}} castes, {{mÅ«rdhÄvasikta}}, {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}, {{niá¹£Äda}}, {{mÄhiá¹£ya}}, {{ugra}}, and {{karaá¹‡a}} are so called only when they are the offspring of women married by men of higher {{vará¹‡as}}. Manu (X. 41) says that the six {{anuloma}} castes are entitled to the rites ({{saá¹skÄras}} like {{upanayana}}) performed for {{dvijas}}, but that the {{pratiloma}} castes are like {{Å›Å«dras}} (i. e. even when a {{pratiloma}} caste springs from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman and a {{ká¹£atriya}} or {{vaiÅ›ya}} male they cannot have {{upanayana}} and other rites of {{dvijas}} performed for them, though both parents are {{dvijas}}). {{Kauá¹­ilya}}74 (III. 7) also says that all {{pratilomas}} except {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} are like {{Å›Å«dras}}; {{Viá¹£á¹‡u}} says that they are condemned by all {{Ä€ryas}}. {{Devala}}’s (quoted by {{ParÄÅ›aramÄdhavÄ«ya}} I. 2. p. 122) says that {{pratilomas}} are outside the pale of the system of {{vará¹‡as}} and are {{patita}}. The {{Smá¹›tyarthasÄra}} p. 13 says that {{anuloma}} sons and those born of the {{mÅ«rdhÄvasikta}} and other {{anuloma}} castes are {{dvijÄtis}} and are entitled to {{saá¹skÄras}} as {{dvijÄtis}}. Commentators like {{KullÅ«ka}} (on Manu X. 11) say that, as no marriage is legally possible between a woman of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} and a male of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}, all the {{pratilomas}} are born outside lawful wedlock. Gautama (IV. 20) says that all {{pratilomas}} are {{dharmahÄ«na}}, which is interpreted by the {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} III. 262 as meaning that they cannot have {{upanayana}} and similar {{saá¹skÄras}} of {{dvijas}} performed for them, though they are amenable to the rules of morality and entitled to perform {{vratas}} and {{prÄyaÅ›cittas}}. {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}}, {{BaudhÄyana}} and several others do not make it clear whether, when they speak of {{pratilomas}}, they contemplate offspring of legal marriages or only of illegitimate and adulterous unions. But it will be seen from the list appended below that {{UÅ›anas}} and {{VaikhÄnasa}} almost always make a distinction between the caste assigned to the offspring of the union of parents of different {{vará¹‡as}}, according as there is a marriage between the two or it is only a clandestine, illegitimate or adulterous union. For example, {{UÅ›anas}} says75 that when there is a marriage between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female, the offspring is called {{sÅ«ta}}, while the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman and a {{ká¹£atriya}} male was called {{rathakÄra}}. So these two authors held that there could be a legal marriage when a woman of a higher {{vará¹‡a}} married a male of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}.76 There were several other works like the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} ({{ÅšivamÄhÄtmyakhaá¹‡á¸a}} chap. 12. 12-48) where a similar distinction is made between the children of marriages and clandestine unions. The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 90 says that such progeny as {{kuá¹‡á¸a}} and {{golaka}} (Manu III. 174), {{kÄnÄ«na}}, {{sahodhaja}} (who are all not due to intercourse in wedlock) are different from {{savará¹‡a}}, {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}} and are to be treated as {{Å›Å«dras}} and that the {{ká¹£etraja}} son is to be treated as in a different category (since {{niyoga}} is allowed by the {{smá¹›tis}} and by the usage of {{Å›iá¹£á¹­as}}) and belongs to the caste of his mother. {{AparÄrka}} (on {{YÄj.}} I. 92 p. 118) does not accept this view and holds that even {{kÄnÄ«na}} and {{sahodha}} may be held to be {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} (if the begetter can be proved to have been a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}); while {{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}} on {{YÄj.}} II. 133 says that {{kÄnÄ«na}} and {{gÅ«á¸haja}} must be deemed to belong to the mother’s caste as the begetter may not be known and that {{sahodhaja}} (II. 135) is also to be so treated. These several kinds of secondary sons will be treated under inheritance.

Varna and jÄti

A few words must be said about the word {{jÄti}}. The idea of {{vará¹‡a}} was as we have seen based originally on race, culture, character and profession. It takes account mainly of the moral and intellectual worth of man and is a system of classes which appears more or less natural. The ideal of {{vará¹‡a}} even in the {{smá¹›tis}} lays far more emphasis on duties, on a high standard of effort for the community or society rather than on the rights and privileges of birth. The system of {{jÄtis}} (castes) lays all emphasis on birth and heredity and tends to create the mentality of clinging to privileges without trying to fulfil the obligations corresponding to such privileges. The word ‘{{jÄti}}’ in the sense of caste hardly ever occurs in the vedic literature. In the {{Nirukta}}77 (XII. 13) it is said ‘after {{agnicayana}} (the building of the fire altar), a man should not approach a {{rÄmÄ}} (for sexual intercourse); {{rÄmÄ}} is so called because she is approached only for pleasure and not for (accumulation of) merit; she is of a dark caste’. Here the word {{ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ajÄtyÄ}} occurs with reference to a woman of the {{Å›Å«dra}} caste. Almost these very words occur in {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} (18. 17-18)78 where for {{ká¹›á¹£á¹‡ajÄtyÄ}} the word {{ká¹›á¹£á¹‡avará¹‡Ä}} is substituted. {{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 56 ({{yad-ucyate}} etc.)79 quotes these words as a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} text, but there the word is {{ká¹›á¹£á¹‡avará¹‡Ä«}}. Therefore, the use of the word {{jÄti}} in the sense of caste can be traced back at least to the times of the {{Nirukta}}. {{PÄá¹‡ini}} (V. 4. 9)80 teaches the formation of words like ‘{{brÄhmaá¹‡ajÄtÄ«ya}}’ derived from words ending in ‘{{jÄti}}’ (in the sense of caste). The expression ‘{{jÄtidharma}}’ (rules of castes) occurs in Gautama XI. 20, {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} I. 17 and XIX. 7, Manu I. 118 and VIII, 41 and the word {{jÄti}} (caste) occurs also in {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} II. 3. 6.1, II. 1.2.3, Manu (IV. 141, X, 11, 18, 40, 97), {{YÄj.}} (I. 95, 361, II. 69, 206, III. 213), {{NÄrada}} ({{á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna}} 288) and in the {{vÄrtika}} on {{PÄá¹‡ini}} IV. 1. 137. {{Vará¹‡a}} and {{jÄti}} are sometimes clearly distinguished as in {{YÄj.}} II. 69 and 206. But very often they are confounded. In Manu X. 27,31 the word {{vará¹‡a}} is used in the sense of mixed castes ({{jÄtis}}). Conversely the word {{jÄti}} often appears to be used to indicate ‘{{vará¹‡a}}’. Vide Manu III. 15, VIII. 177, IX. 86 and 335, X. 41 and {{YÄj.}} I. 89 (in which latter {{sajÄti}} appears to mean ‘{{savará¹‡a}}’).

Even when {{anuloma}} marriages were allowed there is no unanimity among the sages and the {{smá¹›tis}} as to the status of the progeny of such unions. Three different views are found. The first view is that if a male of one {{vará¹‡a}} married a female of the {{vará¹‡a}} immediately after it, the progeny belonged to the {{vará¹‡a}} of the father; e. g. {{Baud. Dh. S.}}81 (I. 8.6 and I. 9. 3) says that sons born to a person of a {{savará¹‡a}} wife or a wife of the {{vará¹‡a}} next to his own are {{savará¹‡a}} i. e. the son of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a wife of the {{ká¹£atriya}} {{vará¹‡a}} is a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. The {{AnuÅ›Äsanaparva}} 48. 4, {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 106) and {{Kauá¹­ilya}} (III. 7) say the same. Gautama IV, 15 as interpreted by {{Haradatta}} appears to say that the off-spring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} wife is called {{savará¹‡a}}, but not the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} wife or of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male from a {{Å›Å«dra}} wife. Fick (pp. 54-57) shows that even according to the {{BhaddasÄla JÄtaka}} ’the family of the mother does not matter; the family of the father alone is important’. The second view is that the progeny of {{anuloma}} unions is in status lower than the father, but higher than the mother; e. g.
