Manu X. 6 ‘sages declare the sons begotten by {{dvijas}} on wives of {{vará¹‡as}} immediately next to theirs as similar (to the fathers, but not of the same {{vará¹‡a}} with the fathers) but tainted by the inferiority of their mothers.’ The third view (and this is the common view) is that the progeny of {{anuloma}} marriages is of the same {{vará¹‡a}} as regards its privileges and obligations as the mother’s; e. g. vide {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16. 282 and {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (prose) quoted by the {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 91 and {{AparÄrka}} (p. 118). A classical echo of this view is found in the {{ÅšakuntalÄ}} of {{KÄlidÄsa}} where king {{Duá¹£yanta}} exclaims aside to himself ‘would that this girl were born of the sage from a wife who was not {{savará¹‡a}}’.83 {{MedhÄtithi}} on Manu X, 6 says that {{PÄá¹‡á¸u}}, {{Dhá¹›tarÄá¹£á¹­ra}} and {{Vidura}} being {{ká¹£etraja}} sons took the caste of their mothers. The {{Mit.}} explains the words of {{Åšaá¹…kha}} by saying that the offspring of {{anuloma}} marriages such as {{mÅ«rdhÄvasikta}} are not really different from {{ká¹£atriyas}}, that they have the same {{saá¹skÄras}} but they do not become {{ká¹£atriyas}} etc. and are dubbed by these names to indicate their mixed origin and come to form separate sub-castes. The {{pratilomas}}, as said above, are lower in status than any of the two parents.

Varna and the enumeration of castes

The ancient {{dharmaÅ›Ästras}} mention only a few mixed castes. {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} mentions only {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}, {{paulkasa}} and {{vaiá¹‡a}}. Gautama names five {{anuloma}} castes, six {{pratiloma}}, one and eight others according to the view of some. {{BaudhÄyana}} adds to those mentioned by Gautama a few more viz. {{rathakÄra}}, {{Å›vapaka}}, {{vaiá¹‡a}} and {{kukkuá¹­a}}. {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} names even a smaller number than Gautama and {{BaudhÄyana}}. It is Manu (X) and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (XVI) that for the first time dilate upon the avocations of the mixed castes. Manu refers to 6 {{anuloma}}, 6 {{pratiloma}} and 20 doubly mixed castes and states the avocations of about 23; {{YÄj.}} names only 13 castes (other than the four {{Vará¹‡as}}). {{UÅ›anas}} names about 40 and gives their peculiar avocations. All the {{smá¹›tis}} taken together hardly mention more than about one hundred castes.

The number of primary {{anulomas}} is only six (vide Manu X. 10, {{YÄj.}} I. 91-92), but Manu names only three of them, viz. {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}, {{niá¹£Äda}} and {{ugra}}. The primary {{pratilomas}} also are six (vide Manu X. 11â€“12 and 16-17 and {{YÄj.}} I. 93â€“94) viz. {{sÅ«ta}}, {{vaidehaka}}, {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}, {{mÄgadha}}, {{ká¹£attá¹›}}, and {{Äyogava}}. Further sub-castes are said to arise from the unions of the {{anulomas}} and {{pratilomas}} with the four {{vará¹‡as}} and of the male of one {{anuloma}} and the female of another, from the union of {{pratilomas}} among themselves and from the union of a male or female of an {{anuloma}} caste and the female or male of a {{pratiloma}} caste. For example, {{YÄj.}} I. 95 defines {{rathakÄra}} as the offspring of a {{mÄhiá¹£ya}} male and a {{karaá¹‡a}} female, i. e. it is a further mixture between two {{anuloma}} castes. The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 95 says that {{upanayana}} and other {{saá¹skÄras}} are performed for the offspring of {{anuloma}} persons marrying among themselves. The {{Smá¹›tyarthasÄra}} (p. 13) says the same.84 Similarly Manu X, 15 says that {{Ävá¹›ta}} and {{ÄbhÄ«ra}} spring from the union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with an {{ugra}} girl and an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl respectively (i. e. from the union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with {{anuloma}} caste girls). Manu X. 19 says that {{Å›vapÄka}} is the offspring of a {{ká¹£attá¹›}} male (a {{pratiloma}}) with an {{ugra}} female (an {{anuloma}}). Manu X. 33 defines {{maitreyaka}} as the offspring of a {{vaidehaka}} male and an {{Äyogava}} female (i.e. from parents who are both {{pratiloma}}). A {{smá¹›ti}} verse quoted85 by {{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 95 says that there are six {{anulomas}}, 24 doubly mixed castes (due to the union of the six {{anulomas}} with the four {{vará¹‡as}}), 6 {{pratilomas}} and 24 doubly mixed castes (due to the unions of 6 {{pratilomas}} with four {{vará¹‡as}}) i. e, in all 60 and further mixtures of these among themselves give rise to innumerable sub-castes. Similarly {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16.786 says that the further mixed castes arising from the unions of mixed castes are numberless. This shows that before the time of the {{Viá¹£á¹‡udharmasÅ«tra}} (i. e. at least about 2000 years ago) numberless castes and sub-castes had been formed and the writers on {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} practically gave up in despair the task of deriving them, even though mediately, from the primary {{vará¹‡as}}. The same state of things was continued and was rather aggravated by the time of the {{nibandhakÄras}}. {{MedhÄtithi}} on Manu X. 31 speaks of 60 mixed castes along with the four {{vará¹‡as}} and adds that by the intermingling of these endless sub-castes are formed. The {{MitÄká¹£arÄ}}87 on {{YÄj.}} I. 95 observes that since the castes springing from the double intermingling of {{vará¹‡as}} are innumerable, it is impossible to describe or enumerate them. Similarly the {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} in its {{prakÄ«rá¹‡aka}} topic of {{vyavahÄra}} section, when dealing with castes, merely quotes several passages from the {{smá¹›tis}} and does not add a word of its own about the castes, their names or avocations in its own day (first half of 12th century). The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 9488 expressly says that the avocations of the {{pratilomas}} (about which {{YÄj.}} is silent) should be understood from the {{smá¹›tis}} of {{UÅ›anas}} and Manu. Medieval writers on {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} usually ignore the treatment in detail of the numerous sub-castes and content themselves with dilating upon the duties of the four {{vará¹‡as}}.

Varna and sub-castes

There was great diversity of opinion among the {{smá¹›tikÄras}} about the derivation and status of the several sub-castes. We find that the same sub-caste is known under five or six different names even so early as the present {{Manusmá¹›ti}}. Manu X. 22 gives seven names for the same caste and Manu X. 23 gives five for another. {{ViÅ›varÅ«pa}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 92 explains that these different names are due to difference of locality.89 There is another difficulty. The same name given to a caste is differently derived by different writers (vide under {{niá¹£Äda}} and {{pÄraÅ›ava}} below). For the same sub-caste different names are given in different {{smá¹›tis}} (vide under {{ká¹›ta}} and {{rÄmaka}}). It became difficult to assign any peculiar derivation for groups of people and so Manu (X. 40), {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}}90 (18.7) and {{AnuÅ›Äsana parva}} 148. 29 laid down that men’s sub-caste was to be known from their actions and occupations. This shows that according to most writers castes in the times of the {{smá¹›tis}} were predominantly occupational.

One word that frequently occurs in connection with castes and sub-castes is {{vará¹‡asaá¹…kara}} (or only {{saá¹…kara}}). In Manu X. 12 and 24 the word {{vará¹‡asaá¹…kara}} is used in the plural in the sense of mixed castes, while in Manu X. 40 (and in V. 89) the word {{saá¹…kara}} seems to be used in the sense of ‘mixture or intermingling of {{Vará¹‡as}}’. Gautama (VIII. 3) employs the word {{saá¹…kara}} and says ‘on the two (the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and the king) depends the prosperity of men, protection, the prevention of mixture (of {{vará¹‡as}}) and the (accumulation of) merit (or the observance of {{dharma}}).91 {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 102)92 says ’to be born from a union in the inverse order of {{Vará¹‡as}} amounts to {{vará¹‡asaá¹…kara}};’ while {{Bá¹›haspati}} quoted in the {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} appears to apply the word {{vará¹‡asaá¹…kara}} to both {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}} castes. {{Baud.}}93 {{Dh. S.}} (I. 9. 16) says that those who are born of {{Vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} are called {{vrÄtyas}}. The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 96 applies the word {{vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} to both {{anuloma}} and {{pratiloma}} progeny. {{MedhÄtithi}}94 on Manu V. 88 says that the word ‘{{saá¹karajÄta}}’ indicates {{pratilomas}} like {{Äyogava}} and that even though as regards {{anulomas}} there is intermingling (of {{vará¹‡as}}), yet as they have the privileges of the caste of their mothers, even Manu himself does not apply the word {{saá¹kÄ«rá¹‡ayoni}} to them as seen above (Manu X. 25). Yama95 quoted in the {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} says ‘{{Vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} arises by the violation of the restrictions (about marriage); if the proper order of {{vará¹‡as}} (i. e. male of higher {{vará¹‡a}} marrying a woman of a lower {{vará¹‡a}}) is followed, (the offspring) is entitled to be regarded as belonging to the system of {{vará¹‡as}}, but if the reverse order is followed it is sin.’ Manu (X. 24) says ‘mixed castes arise by members of one {{vará¹‡a}} having sexual intercourse with women of another {{vará¹‡a}}, by marrying women who ought not to have been married (such as a {{sagotra}} girl) and by neglect of the duties peculiar to one’s {{vará¹‡a}}’. The {{AnuÅ›Äsanaparva}} 48. 196 remarks that ‘{{vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} arises from wealth, greed, desires, uncertainty about the {{vará¹‡a}} (of a person) or ignorance about {{vará¹‡a}}’. Even in such a philosophical treatise as the {{BhagavadgÄ«tÄ}} (I. 41-43), it is said ‘when women become corrupt (or demoralised), intermingling of {{vará¹‡as}} arises; {{saá¹kara}} necessarily leads the whole family and the destroyer of the family to hell. By reason of these transgressions of the destroyers of families which bring about {{vará¹‡asaá¹kara}}, the ancient caste observances and family observances are subverted.’

Varna and vará¹‡asaá¹…kara

On account of the great emphasis laid on the prevention of {{vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} the {{smá¹›tis}} ordain that it is one of the principal duties of the king to punish people if they transgress the rules prescribed for {{vará¹‡as}} and to punish men and women if guilty of {{vará¹‡asaá¹kara}}. Gautama (XI. 9-10) says ’the king should protect the {{vará¹‡as}} and {{ÄÅ›ramas}} according to the {{Å›Ästras}} and he should make them conform to their duties when they swerve from them;’ {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} (19. 7-8) ’the king paying attention to all these viz. the observances of countries, castes and families should make the four {{vará¹‡as}} conform to their duties and should punish them when they go astray’; {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} III. 3 and {{YÄj.}} I. 361, {{MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eyapurÄá¹‡a}} 27, {{MatsyapurÄá¹‡a}} 215. 63 say the same. {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 113)97 says ‘when a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman goes astray a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} is born from a {{Å›Å«dra}} male (and the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman); therefore the king should specially protect women from {{saá¹…kara}}’. It is on account of this that so early as in the first centuries of the Christian era we see that king {{VÄsiá¹£á¹­hiputa Siri PulumÄyi}} is extolled as having prevented the mixture of the four {{vará¹‡as}} (E. I. vol. VIII pp. 60-61, ‘{{vinivatita-cÄtuvaá¹‡a-sakarasa}}’). That {{Vará¹‡asaá¹kara}} had gone too far in the opinion of the author of the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} ({{Vanaparva}} 180. 31-33) follows from the following words which are despairingly put in the mouth of {{Yudhiá¹£á¹­hira}}98 “It appears to me that it is very difficult to ascertain the caste of human beings on account of the confusion of all {{vará¹‡as}}; all sorts of men are always begetting offspring from all sorts of women; speech, sexual intercourse, being born and death-these are common to all human beings; and there is scriptural authority (for this view) in the words ‘We, who ever we are, offer the sacrifice’. Hence those who have seen the truth regard character as the principal thing desired.” {{Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya}} in his {{bhÄá¹£ya}} on {{VedÄntasÅ«tra}} I. 3. 33 remarks that, though in his day {{vará¹‡as}} and {{ÄÅ›ramas}} had become disorganised and unstable as to their {{dharmas}}, that was not the case in other ages, since otherwise the {{Å›Ästras}} laying down regulations for them would have to be deemed purposeless or futile.99

Gautama (IV. 18-19), Manu (X. 64-65) and {{YÄj.}} (I. 96) speak of a peculiar doctrine called {{JÄtyutkará¹£a}} and {{JÄtyapakará¹£a}}. These passages have given rise to some divergence of interpretation, but their general sense is clear. Gautama (IV. 18)100 says that according to the {{ÄcÄryas}} the {{anulomas}} when they marry in such a way that the bridegroom in each stage is higher or lower than the bride they rise to a higher or go down to a lower {{vará¹‡a}} in the 7th or 5th generation (respectively). This is explained by {{Haradatta}} as follows: When a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman the daughter born of this marriage is called {{savará¹‡Ä}}; if this latter is married to a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a daughter is born and this latter is again married by a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and if this continues in the same way for seven generations, then when the 7th girl in descent marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, whatever child is born of that union belongs to the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} {{vará¹‡a}} (though in the preceding generations only the fathers were {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and the mothers were all not strictly {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, but only {{savará¹‡Äs}} if at all). This is called {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} (rise in status as a caste). On the other hand, when a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} girl and a son is born who is called {{savará¹‡a}}, then that son marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} girl and has a son and this is continued for five generations, then when the fifth son (in descent) marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} girl, the child born belongs to the {{ká¹£atriya}} {{vará¹‡a}} (though in all the preceding generations the father was higher than {{ká¹£atriya}} and the mother only was a {{ká¹£atriya}}). This is {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} (fall in status as a caste). The same rule holds good as regards a {{ká¹£atriya}} marrying a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} marrying a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. The same rule applies among {{anulomas}} also e. g. if a {{savará¹‡a}} (as defined by Gautama) marries an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl and a daughter is born who again marries a {{savará¹‡a}} and this continues for seven generations, then the child of the 7th girl (in descent) from a {{savará¹‡a}} male becomes a {{savará¹‡a}} (rises in status); on the other hand if a {{savará¹‡a}} (offspring of {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and {{ká¹£atriya}} female) marries an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl and a son is born and that son marries an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl and their son marries an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl and this goes on for seven generations, then the child of the 7th male (in descent) born of an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} wife becomes an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} (i. e. there is {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} as to {{anulomas}}).

Varna and jÄtyutkará¹£a

According to Manu101 X. 64 when a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman, the daughter born is {{pÄraÅ›ava}}, and if this {{pÄraÅ›ava}} daughter marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and the daughter of this latter union marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and this continues for seven generations, then the seventh generation will be a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (i. e. there will be {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}). Conversely, if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and a son is born, he is a {{pÄraÅ›ava}} and that son marries a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and their son again marries a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and this goes on for 7 generations, the 7th generation becomes a mere {{Å›Å«dra}} (there is {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}}). It will be seen that this differs from Gautama in several respects. In the first place for both {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} and {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} seven generations are prescribed, while in Gautama they are 7 and 5 respectively (according to {{Haradatta}}). In the second place according to Gautama the 8th in descent from the first {{anuloma}} marriage secures {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}, while according to Manu, the 7th secures it. Further Manu is silent about {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} when the original parents are {{anulomas}}. Besides, the commentators of Manu shorten the period for rise or fall in {{jÄti}} as stated below. Manu X. 65 extends the same rules to the offspring of the marriage of a {{ká¹£atriya}} with a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman and of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} with a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman. {{MedhÄtithi}} and {{KullÅ«ka}} extend these express words of Manu further by explaining that if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and a daughter is born and she again marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} then in the fifth generation there will be {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}; conversely if a son is born from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} wife, and he marries a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and this goes on, then in the fifth generation the son will be a {{vaiÅ›ya}} (i. e. there will be {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}}). Similarly if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman then there is {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} or {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} in three generations.

{{YajÃ±avalkya}} (I. 96)102 speaks of two kinds of {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} or {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} viz. one due to marriage (as in Manu and Gautama) and another due to the avocation followed. It should be understood that there is rise in caste in the 7th or even in the 5th generation; if there is inversion as to the avocations, then there is corresponding similarity (of {{vará¹‡a}} in the 7th or even 5th generation). This is elaborated by the {{MitÄká¹£arÄ}} as follows: If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and a daughter is born she is a {{niá¹£ÄdÄ«}}; if this latter marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a daughter is born and she in turn marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and this goes on for six generations, then the child of the 6th girl (in descent) becomes a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} (he being 7th in descent); similarly if a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman and a girl is born, she is an {{ambaá¹£á¹­hÄ}}; if the latter marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a daughter is born and this goes on in the same way, then the fifth girl in descent has a child (from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} husband) which is the 6th in descent from the original {{anuloma}} marriage and which then becomes a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} marries a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman and a daughter is born who is called {{mÅ«rdhÄvasiktÄ}} ({{YÄj.}} I. 91) and she marries a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, then the fourth in descent marrying a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} has a child (5th in descent), then that child becomes a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}. Similarly, if a {{ká¹£atriya}} married a {{Å›Å«dra}} female and a daughter was born, she was called {{ugrÄ}}, then {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} by marrying a higher male took place in the 6th generation. If a {{ká¹£atriya}} married a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman, the daughter born was a {{mÄhiá¹£yÄ}} and {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} took place in the 5th generation. If a {{vaiÅ›ya}} married a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman, the daughter of the marriage is a {{karaá¹‡Ä«}} and if she marries a {{vaiÅ›ya}}, then in the fifth generation there would be {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}. Certain peculiar avocations and activities are prescribed for the four {{vará¹‡as}}. Each {{vará¹‡a}} may in times of difficulty follow the occupation peculiar to the caste immediately below it, but should not follow the avocations peculiar to the higher {{vará¹‡as}}103; he must however revert to his proper avocation when the difficulty is over (vide {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} II. 13-23, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} II. 15, {{YÄj.}} I. 118-120, Gautama X. 1-7 &c.). If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} begins to follow the avocations peculiar to a {{Å›Å«dra}} and has a son, who does the same and this goes on continuously for seven generations, the 7th becomes a {{Å›Å«dra}} (by caste). If a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} begins to follow the avocations peculiar to a {{vaiÅ›ya}} or a {{ká¹£atriya}} then in the 5th or 6th generation respectively there is fall in caste (i. e. the 5th or 6th becomes respectively a {{vaiÅ›ya}} or {{ká¹£atriya}}). Similarly if a {{ká¹£atriya}} follows the avocation of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} or {{Å›Å«dra}} and this goes on continuously, then the 5th or 6th (respectively) becomes a {{Å›Å«dra}} or {{vaiÅ›ya}}. Similarly if a {{vaiÅ›ya}} takes to the work peculiar to {{Å›Å«dras}}, then the fifth generation becomes {{Å›Å«dra}}.

{{Baud.}}104 {{Dh. S.}} (I. 8. 13-14) gives another illustration of {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}. He says “if a {{niá¹£Äda}} (the son of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} wife) marries a {{niá¹£ÄdÄ«}} (and this goes on continuously), then the fifth generation becomes free of the taint of a {{Å›Å«dra}} status, he can have {{upanayana}} performed for him and his son (6th from the original {{niá¹£Äda}} pair) can have a vedic sacrifice performed for him.’

These provisions would considerably lessen the rigour of the caste system based purely on birth. But one feels grave doubts whether such a method of {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} or {{jÄtyapakará¹£a}} (particularly the one based on occupation) was or could be ever enforced in actual life. It would have been impossible to remember descent in a particular way for five or seven generations. The want of unanimity among the original {{smá¹›tikÄras}} and the commentators also points in the direction that the method advocated, though it might have originally some slight basis in fact, was only a hypothesis and an ideal. Hardly any examples of {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}} in the way set out by Manu or {{YÄj.}} occur in the literature on {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} or in {{inscriptions}}. In the {{inscriptions}} we have authentic cases of intercaste marriages only, but hardly anything further.105 From the Talgunda pillar inscription of king {{KÄkusthavarman}} of the Kadamba family we learn that the Kadambas were originally of {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} lineage, that the founder of the family was a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} {{MayÅ«raÅ›arman}}, who became exasperated with the Pallavas of KÄÃ±cipura and took up the sword to conquer the earth, that his descendants applied the affix ‘{{Varman}}’ to their names (as if they were {{ká¹£atriyas}}, according to Manu II. 32) and {{KÄkusthavarman}} (4th in descent from {{MayÅ«raÅ›arman}}) married his daughters to Gupta and other kings106. This shows that an originally {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} family came to look upon itself as {{ká¹£atriya}} by virtue of pursuing the profession of arms and governing the people. In the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} we meet with stories of kings who became {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. For example, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 30 speaks of {{VÄ«tahavya}}, a king, as having become a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}; similarly {{Åšalya}} (39. 36-37) speaks of {{Ará¹£á¹­iá¹£eá¹‡a}}, {{SindhudvÄ«pa}}, {{DevÄpi}} and {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}} as having become {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} at a sacred spot on the {{SarasvatÄ«}}. In the {{PurÄá¹‡as}} also there are stories107 of kings like {{ViÅ›vÄmitra}}, {{MÄndhÄtÄ}}, {{Saá¹ká¹›ti}}, {{Kapi}}, {{VadhryaÅ›va}}, {{Purukutsa}}, {{Ará¹£á¹­iá¹£eá¹‡a}}, {{AjamÄ«á¸ha}} and others as having risen to the status of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. These are mythical sages and their rise is not stated to have been due to the principle of {{jÄtyutkará¹£a}}108. Ibbetson (Report on the census of the Punjab 1881, pp. 174-176) notes that {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} following certain professions became degraded and that the caste was changed.

That the professional castes were wealthy and well organized follows from the {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} works and epigraphic records. In this connection the words {{Å›reá¹‡i}}, {{pÅ«ga}}, {{gaá¹‡a}}, {{vrÄta}} and {{saá¹…gha}} deserve to be carefully studied. All these were called {{samÅ«ha}} (group) or {{varga}} according to {{KÄtyÄyana}}.109 These words occur in the Vedic literature, but the sense is generally ‘a group’ and there is no special meaning attached. ‘{{Åšreá¹‡i}}’ occurs in the {{á¹šg.}} I. 163. 10110 (like flamingoes the horses press forward in rows or groups); both {{vrÄta}} and {{gaá¹‡a}} occur in {{á¹šg.}} III. 26. 6, V, 53. 11 and in numerous other places. The {{Kauá¹£Ä«takÄ« Br.}} 16.7111 speaks of Rudra as {{pÅ«ga}} (as he is the head of the band of Maruts). {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} I. 1. 3. 26112 quotes a {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}} passage about a group ({{saá¹…gha}}) of {{brahmacÄrins}} going about for alms. {{PÄá¹‡ini}} teaches113 the formation of derivatives from {{pÅ«ga}}, {{gaá¹‡a}}, {{saá¹…gha}} (V. 2. 52), from {{vrÄta}} (V. 2. 21). In his time it appears the words had acquired specific meanings. The {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} explains (on {{PÄá¹‡ini}} V, 2.21) that {{vrÄtas}} are groups formed by men of various castes with no fixed means of livelihood but subsisting by the might (or strength) of their bodies (by bodily labour of various kinds). The {{KÄÅ›ikÄ}}[^185] explains {{pÅ«gas}} as associations of men of different castes with no fixed professions, who are solely bent on making money or seeking pleasure. {{Kauá¹­ilya}} in one place distinguishes between soldiers and {{Å›reá¹‡is}} (guilds) and in another place says that the guilds of {{ká¹£atriyas}} in {{Kambhoja}} and {{SurÄá¹£á¹­ra}} subsist by the profession of arms and {{vÄrtÄ}} (agriculture).[^186] {{Vas. Dh. S.}} XVI. 15 says that boundary disputes are to be settled by the evidence of the old men in the village or town or of guilds ({{Å›reá¹‡i}}) when there is conflict of documentary evidence. {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} V. 167 prescribes banishment for him who embezzles the wealth of associations ({{gaá¹‡a}}) and who transgresses the conventions made by them. Manu (VIII. 219) has a similar rule about village and local associations ({{saá¹…gha}}). The above words are variously explained by the several commentators (vide my notes to the translation of {{KÄtyÄyana}} verses116 678-682 of that reconstructed {{smá¹›ti}}). {{KÄtyÄyana}} says ‘{{Naigama}} is an association of citizens of the same city, {{vrÄta}} is a company of soldiers carrying various arms, {{pÅ«ga}} is an association of traders and the like, {{gaá¹‡a}} is a group of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, {{saá¹…gha}} is a body of {{Bauddhas}} or {{Jainas}}; and bands of {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and {{Å›vapacas}} are called {{gulma}}.’ {{YÄj.}} (I. 361) directs the king to punish {{kulas}}, castes, {{Å›reá¹‡is}}, {{gaá¹‡as}}, if they transgress their rules (of conduct or business) and the {{Mit.}} explains {{Å›reá¹‡i}} as a guild of sellers of betel leaves and the like and {{gaá¹‡a}} as of ‘{{helÄbukas}}’ (horse-dealers); while {{YÄj.}} II. 192 and {{NÄrada}}[^188] ({{samayasyÄnapÄkarma}}) require the king to prevent the breach of the conventions of {{Å›reá¹‡i}}, {{naigama}}, {{pÅ«ga}}, {{vrÄta}}, {{gaá¹‡a}} and to confirm them in their traditional occupations. {{YÄj.}} II. 30 says that {{pÅ«gas}} and {{Å›reá¹‡is}} had authority to investigate disputes and that the {{pÅ«ga}} was a higher tribunal than the {{Å›reá¹‡i}}. The {{Mit.}} on this explains that {{pÅ«ga}} is an association of people of different castes and different occupations that stay in one locality, while a {{Å›reá¹‡i}} is a group of people of different castes, that subsist by the occupation of one caste and gives ‘{{heá¸Äbukas}}’ ‘{{tÄmbÅ«likas}}’ (betel sellers), ‘{{kuvindas}}’ (weavers) and ‘{{carmakÄras}}’ (shoe-makers) as examples of {{Å›reá¹‡is}}. In the {{Hará¹£a}} stone of {{ChahamÄna VigraharÄja}} (E. I. vol. II. p. 124) there is a reference to one {{dramma}} for each horse given to ‘{{heá¸Ävikas}}’. In the Nasik Inscription No. 15 (E. I. vol. VIII p. 88) we are told that in the reign of the {{Ä€bhÄ«ra}} king {{ÄªÅ›varasena}} 1000 {{kÄrá¹£Äpaá¹‡as}} were deposited with an association of potters as a permanent donation yielding interest, 500 with a guild of oilmen and 2000 with a guild of watermen ({{udakayantra-Å›reá¹‡i}}) for medicines to be given to sick {{bhiká¹£us}}. No. 9 and No. 12 of the inscriptions at Nasik also contain reference to deposits of money with the guild of weavers. The MathurÄ {{BrÄhmÄ«}} inscription of {{Huviá¹£ka}}’s reign mentions a guild of flour-makers ({{samitakara}}, vide E. I. vol. 21 p. 55 at p. 61). The Junnar Buddhist cave Inscription (A. S. W. I. vol. IV p. 97) refers to an investment of monies with the guild ({{Å›reá¹‡i}}) of bamboo-workers and of braziers ({{kÄsakÄra}}). The Indore copperplate of {{Skandagupta}} (of the Gupta {{saá¹vat}} 146) speaks of the deposit with the guilds of the oilmen of {{Indrapura}} for permanently securing a supply of two {{palas}} of oil (C. I. I. vol. III p. 70). Similarly it is said that a guild of silk weavers from {{LÄá¹­a}} (southern {{GujarÄt}}) came to {{DaÅ›apura}} (Dasor in Malwa) and built a temple of the sun in the {{MÄlava}} year 494 i. e. 437-38 A. D. (C. I. I. vol. III p. 81 = I. A. vol. 15 p. 194). These examples show that about the first centuries of the Christian era such castes as woodworkers, oilmen, betel sellers and weavers that are at present very low in the hierarchy of castes had very efficient caste guilds, so famous for their organization, integrity and stability that people deposited with them thousands for permanent services to objects of charity.118

We shall now append a list of several castes enumerated or mentioned in {{smá¹›ti}} and other {{dharmaÅ›Ästra}} works from about 500 B. C. to 1000 A. D. in alphabetical (Sanskrit) order. They are not given in a tabular form owing to numerous contradictory statements in the {{smá¹›tis}} themselves. References are given only to a few {{smá¹›tis}}, the principal ones drawn upon being the {{DharmasÅ«tras}}, Manu, {{YÄj.}}, {{Vaikh. smÄrtasÅ«tra}} X. 11-15, {{UÅ›anas}}, the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} ({{Åšiva-mÄhÄtmya-khaá¹‡á¸a}} chap. 12). It is hoped that the list is fairly exhaustive for the {{smá¹›ti}} period. It will be noticed that many of the caste names collected here still occur under the same forms or under slightly modified forms of the names.

{{Andhra}}. Vide above note 118 quoting the {{Ait. Br.}} Manu X. 36 says it was a low subcaste sprung from {{Vaidehaka}} father and {{KÄrÄvara}} mother and that {{Andhras}} were to live outside the village and to subsist (X. 48) by killing wild beasts. In the edicts of {{AÅ›oka}} the {{Andhras}} are associated with {{Pulindas}} (vide Rock Edict No. 13). The {{Udyogaparva}} (160. 103) mentions {{Andhras}} (probably as people of {{Ä€ndhradeÅ›a}}) along with {{Draviá¸as}} and {{KÄÃ±cyas}}. In the NÄlanda plate of {{DevapÄladeva}} (E. I. vol. 17 p. 321) {{meda}}, {{Ändhraka}} and {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} are spoken of as the lowest castes. In Orissa one scheduled caste is noted as {{Ä€di-Andhra}} (vide {{Sch. C. O.}} 1936).

{{Antya}}. According to {{Vas. Dh. S.}} 16.30, Manu IV. 79, VIII. 68, {{YÄj.}} I. 148, 197, Atri 251, Likhita 92, verse {{Ä€pastamba}} (III. 1) this word is a generic appellation for all lowest castes like the {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}. Vide the chapter on ‘untouchables’. The word ‘{{bÄhya}}’ has the same sense. {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} I. 3. 9. 18 says that there is a cessation of Vedic study on the day on which {{bÄhya}}s enter a village; vide also {{NÄrada}} ({{á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna}} 155), {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16.14.

{{Antyaja}}. This word is applied to all lowest castes like the {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} in Manu IV. 61, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 36.7, {{YÄj.}} I. 273, {{Bá¹›hadyama}} (quoted in {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} III. 260). In Manu VIII. 279 the word is used in the sense of ‘{{Å›Å«dra}}’. Various enumerations of the subdivisions of {{antyajas}} are found in the {{smá¹›tis}}. Atri 199 enumerates seven {{antyajas}} viz. {{rajaka}} (washerman), {{carmakÄra}} (worker in hides), {{naá¹­a}} (dancer caste, represented in the Deccan by {{KolhÄá¹­is}}), {{buruá¸a}} (worker in bamboos), {{kaivarta}} (fisherman), {{meda}}, {{bhilla}}.119 This verse is quoted as {{Ä€pastamba}}’s by the {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} III. 265, while {{AparÄrka}} p. 1123 ascribes it to Atri. The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} III. 260 distinguishes between two groups of {{antyajas}}, viz. the one quoted above which it says is not so low as another group of seven, which are called {{antyÄvasÄyins}},120 viz. {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}, {{Å›vapaca}} (eater of dog flesh), {{ká¹£attá¹›}}, {{sÅ«ta}}, {{vaidehika}}, {{mÄgadha}} and {{Äyogava}}. In the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} ({{ÅšÄnti}} 101. 19) reference is made to {{antyaja}} soldiers and {{NÄ«lakaá¹‡á¹­ha}} explains that they were the {{kaivartas}} and {{bhillas}} of the border regions. According to the {{SarasvatÄ«vilÄsa}} (p. 74) {{PitÄmaha}} speaks of the seven castes of {{rajaka}} and others as {{praká¹›tis}}.121 Is it possible that the {{PrÄká¹›ta}} languages were originally so called because they were spoken by these castes called {{praká¹›tis}}? In the {{Saá¹…gamner}} plate of {{Bhillama}} II dated {{Å›aka}} 922 (E. I. vol. II. p. 220) a village is granted with eighteen {{praká¹›tis}} (meaning probably the eighteen guilds of washermen and others). The {{VÄ«ramitrodaya}} ({{vyavahÄra}} p. 12) explains that {{Å›reá¹‡is}} mean the eighteen low castes such as the {{rajaka}}. This shows that these low castes had risen in social status in the medieval ages by their organization and wealth. The {{Veda-VyÄsa smá¹›ti}} (I. 12-13) enumerates twelve castes by name as {{antyajas}} and adds that all those who eat cow’s flesh are also {{antyajas}}.122

{{AntÄvasÄyin}} or {{AntyÄvasÄyin}}. Manu IV.79 separately mentions ‘{{antyas}}’ and ‘{{antyÄvasÄyins}}’ and Manu X. 39 says that the {{antyÄvasÄyin}} is the offspring of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} male from a {{niá¹£Äda}} female, that he is condemned even by all ‘{{bÄhyas}}’ (untouchables) and stays in a cemetery. Gautama 20.1 and 23, 32 mentions ‘{{antyÄvasÄyin}}’ (and -{{yinÄ«}}). {{Vas. Dh. S.}} 18. 3 holds that the {{antyÄvasÄyin}} is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman. The {{BhÄradvÄja-Å›rautasÅ«tra}} (XI. 22. 12) forbids the study of the Veda in the presence of the {{antyÄvasÄyin}}. The {{AnuÅ›Äsana-parva}} (22.22) speaks of {{Medas}}, {{Pulakasas}} and {{AntÄvasÄyins}} (the printed text is corrupt). {{ÅšÄnti}} (141. 29-32) gives a graphic description of a hamlet of {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and calls them ‘{{antyÄvasÄya}}’ (in verse 41). {{NÄrada}} ({{á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna}} 182) says that an {{antyÄvasÄyin}} is not eligible as a witness. Some modern works like the {{JÄtiviveka}} (D.C. Ms. No.347 of 1887-91) say that {{Dom}} in modern times is the {{antyÄvasÄyin}} of the {{smá¹›tis}}.

{{Abhiá¹£ikta}} - See under {{MÅ«rdhÄvasikta}}. {{Ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} - (same as {{Bhojakaá¹‡á¹­ha}}). In {{Ait. Br.}} (chap. 39. 7) king {{Ambaá¹£á¹­hya}} is said to have performed an {{AÅ›vamedha}} sacrifice. In {{PÄá¹‡.}} VIII. 3. 97 the word {{Ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} is derived and on {{PÄá¹‡.}} IV.1.170 {{Ambaá¹£á¹­hya}} (king?) is cited by {{Pat.}} as an example derived from {{Ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} (a country). It is a question whether the caste of {{Ambaá¹£á¹­has}} derived its name from a country. {{Kará¹‡aparva}} (6. 11) mentions a king {{Ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}. In {{Baud. Dh. S.}} I. 9. 3, Manu X. 8, {{YÄj.}} I. 91, {{UÅ›anas}} 31, {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} v. 107) {{Ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} is an {{anuloma}} sprung from the marriage of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman, while according to Gautama 4. 14 as interpreted by {{Haradatta}} he is the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman. {{AvÄ«ra}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female.

{{Ä€bhÄ«ra}}. According to Manu X. 15 he is the child of the union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} girl. The {{MahÄbhÄrata}} ({{Mausalaparva}} 7. 46-63 and 8. 16-17) states that the {{ÄbhÄ«ras}} were {{dasyus}} and {{mlecchas}} who attacked Arjuna after the great war in the land of the five rivers and carried away {{Vá¹›á¹£á¹‡i}} women. The {{AmarakoÅ›a}} says that they are cow-herds and that the {{ÄbhÄ«ra}} wife of a {{MahÄÅ›Å«dra}} is called {{Ä€bhÄ«rÄ«}}. The {{Ä€bhÄ«ras}} became absorbed in Hindu society and we find that an {{Ä€bhÄ«ra}} {{senÄpati}} {{RudrabhÅ«ti}} in the year 103 (181-82 A. Vide E. I. vol. 8. p. 88. {{Ä€bhÄ«ras}} are called {{ahirs}} in modern times. Vide J. B. B. R. A. S. vol. 21 pp. 430-433, Enthoven’s ‘Tribes and castes of Bombay’ vol. I. p. 17 ff.

{{Ä€yogava}}. Vide {{Ä€yogÅ«}} above (p. 43) from Vedic literature. According to {{Gaut.}} IV, 15, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16. 4, Manu X. 12, {{Kauá¹­.}} III. 7, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48. 13, {{YÄj.}} I. 94 this is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung from the union of a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female; while {{Baud. Dh. His avocation (Manu X. 48) is to pare wood, while {{UÅ›anas}} (verse 13) says he is a weaver or subsists by making vessels of bronze or by cultivating paddy or by dealing in cloth. According to {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16. 8 and {{AgnipurÄá¹‡a}} (151. 15) he is to make his living by going to the stage. The {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} says (26. 68-69) that he works in stones and bricks, makes pavements and whitewashes walls i. e. he is the modern {{PÄtharvaá¹­a}} (in the Deccan).

{{Ä€vantya}}. Same as {{BhÅ«rjakaá¹‡á¹­ha}} (Manu X. 21).

{{Ä€vá¹›tika}}. According to {{Vaikh.}} X. 12 he is the child of a clandestine union between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and deals in horses.

{{Ä€hiá¹‡á¸ika}}. According to Manu X. 37 he is the offspring of a {{niá¹£Äda}} male from a {{vaidehÄ«}} female i. e. he is a double {{pratiloma}} caste. {{KullÅ«ka}} says that his avocation according to {{UÅ›anas}} is to prevent strangers from trespassing on places where offenders are kept imprisoned. Manu X. 36 shows that the same caste is called {{KÄrÄvara}} when it follows the craft of a {{carmakÄra}}.

{{Ugra}}. For Vedic reference see above (p. 45). According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9.5), Manu X. 9, {{Kauá¹­.}} III.7, {{YÄj.}} I. 92, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48, 7 he is an {{anuloma}} offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male from a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman, while according to {{UÅ›anas}} (verse 41) he is the offspring of the union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman. The {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} (I. 2. 7. 20) allows a pupil to bring wealth from a {{Å›Å«dra}} or an {{ugra}} when the teacher is in distress or difficulties. {{Gaut.}} 4. 14 (as explained by {{Haradatta}}) says that {{ugra}} is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. The {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} (I. 6. 18. 1) says that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} may accept the gift of money, corn like paddy, flesh of deer, house, field, hay for oxen from an {{ugra}}. Manu X. 49 says that the {{ugra}} should subsist by catching and killing animals that hide in holes, while {{UÅ›anas}} (verse 41) states that he is to be the staff-bearer of the king and to carry out the punishments inflicted on offenders. Vide {{Vaikh.}} 10. 13. According to the {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} and {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} (p. 255) he is called ‘{{RajpÅ«t}}’. In the {{JÄtiviveka}} (the D. C. collection of 1887-1891 No. 347) he is called {{RÄvut}}.

{{Udbandhaka}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (verse 15) he is the offspring of the union of a {{sÅ«nika}} and a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman, subsists by washing clothes and is an untouchable. {{Vaikh.}} 10. 15 says he is the offspring of a {{khanaka}} and a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman.

{{Upakruá¹£á¹­a}}. According to {{Ä€Å›v. Å›r. sÅ«tra}} (II. 1) he does not belong to the {{dvijÄtis}}, but is authorised to perform the vedic rite of {{agnyÄdheya}} and the commentary explains that he is a {{vaiÅ›ya}} following the profession of a carpenter.

{{Oá¸ra}}. Vide Manu X. 43-44. {{Oá¸ra}} is a country corresponding more or less to modern Orissa. Most of the names of people mentioned in Manu X. 44 are derived from countries. Vide note on {{Khasa}} (p. 79) and see {{SabhÄparva}} 51, 23.

{{Kaá¹­akÄra}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (45) and {{Vaikh.}} 10, 13 he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{vaiÅ›ya}} and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{Karaá¹‡a}}. According to {{Gaut.}} (IV. 17 the view of some {{Ä€cÄryas}}) and {{YÄj.}} I. 92, he is the child of the marriage of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} and a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman (i. e. he is an {{anuloma}}). Manu (X. 22) says that a {{ká¹£atriya}} who is a {{vrÄtya}} (i. e. for whom no {{upanayana}} has been performed) has from a similar woman a child variously called {{Jhalla}}, {{Malla}}, {{Nicchivi}} ({{Licchavi}}?), {{Naá¹­a}}, {{Karaá¹‡a}}, {{KhaÅ›a}}, {{Draviá¸a}}. {{Ä€diparva}} 115. 43 tells us that {{Dhá¹›tarÄá¹£á¹­ra}} had from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female a {{karaá¹‡a}} son named {{Yuyutsu}}. {{Ká¹£Ä«rasvÄmÄ«}} on Amara says that {{karaá¹‡a}} also denotes a group of officers like {{kÄyasthas}} and {{adhyaká¹£as}} (superintendents). The {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 49-51) says he is the same as {{cÄraá¹‡a}} or {{vaitÄlika}} and his business is to sing the praises of kings and {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and study the science of erotics.

{{KarmakÄra}}. {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (51. 14) mentions this caste. It is most probably the same as {{karmÄra}}. But {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (prose) quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 115 separately mentions in the same passage {{karmakÄra}} and {{karmÄra}}.

{{KarmÄra}}. For vedic references vide (p. 43) above. This caste appears in the {{gaá¹‡a kulÄlÄdi}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). Manu IV. 215 mentions it. In Bengal the {{Lohar}} is a scheduled caste (vide Sch. C. O. 1936).

{{KÄá¹syakÄra}}. (modern {{kÄá¹sÄra}} in Marathi) Mentioned by {{NÄrada}} ({{á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna}} 274) and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} X. 4 in connection with the balance ordeal.

{{Kakavaca}}. Mentioned in {{UÅ›anas}} (50) as doing the work of bringing grass for horses.

{{Kamboja}}. Vide Manu X. 43-44. The country of {{Kamboja}} was known to {{YÄska}} ({{Nirukta}} II. 2) and {{PÄá¹‡ini}} (IV. 1. 175). {{Udyogaparva}} 160. 103, {{Droá¹‡a}} 121. 13 mention {{KÄmbojas}} with {{Åšakas}}. Vide under {{Yavana}}.

{{KÄyastha}}.126 Heated controversies have raged in medieval and modern times about the origin and status of {{kÄyasthas}} and the bitterness is reflected in the decisions of the Indian courts also. In {{Bholanath v. Emperor}}127 the Calcutta High Court held that the {{kÄyasthas}} of Bengal were {{Å›Å«dras}} and went so far as to hold that a {{kÄyastha}} could marry a {{Dom}} female. But in {{Asita Mohan v. Nirode Mohani}}128 the Privy Council left open the question whether the {{kÄyasthas}} of Bengal were {{Å›Å«dras}}. On the other hand in {{Tulsi Ram v. Bihari Lal}}129 and in {{Ishwari Prasad v. Rai Hari Prasad}}[^181] the Allahabad and Patna High Courts respectively held that the {{kÄyasthas}} were {{dvijas}} and not {{Å›Å«dras}}. In {{Subrao v. Radha}} 52 Bom. 497 at p. 504-506 this conflict of decisions is referred to. The word {{kÄyastha}} does not occur in the ancient {{dharmaÅ›Ästras}} of Gautama, {{Ä€pastamba}}, {{BaudhÄyana}}, or {{Vasiá¹£á¹­ha}} nor in the {{Manusmá¹›ti}}. The {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} VII. 3 defines a public document ({{rÄjasÄká¹£ika}}) as one written in the royal court or office by a {{kÄyastha}} appointed by the king and attested by the hand of the superintendent of the office.[^182] These words suggest that the {{kÄyastha}} was an officer and that there is nothing about a caste here. {{YÄj.}} I. 322 calls upon the king to protect the subjects from the harassment of {{cÄá¹­as}} (rogues), thieves, bad characters, desperadoes and the like and particularly of {{kÄyasthas}}. The {{Mit.}} explains that {{kÄyasthas}} are accountants and scribes, are favourites of the king and very cunning. {{UÅ›anas}}[^183] (35) holds the {{kÄyasthas}} to be a caste and gives an uncomplimentary derivation of the name by saying that it is compounded of the first letters of {{kÄka}} (crow), Yama and {{sthapati}} to convey the three attributes of greed, cruelty and the spoliation (or paring), characteristic of the three. The {{Veda-VyÄsa smá¹›ti}}[^184] (I. 10-11) includes the {{kÄyastha}} among {{Å›Å«dras}} along with barbers, potters and others. {{Sumantu}} quoted in the {{Par. M.}} II part I p. 383 makes the food of a {{lekhaka}} along with that of oilmen and others unfit for a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}.[^185] {{Lekhaka}} is obviously a caste here, but whether it is the {{kÄyastha}} caste is doubtful. {{Bá¹›haspati}} as quoted in the {{Smá¹›ticandrikÄ}} ({{vyavahÄra}}) speaks of the {{gaá¹‡aka}} and {{lekhaka}} as two persons to be associated with a judge in a court of justice and says they were to be {{dvijas}}. Vide also {{AparÄrka}} p. 600. Therefore both these were only officers and not members of a particular caste. In the {{Má¹›cchakaá¹­ika}} (Act IX) a {{Å›reá¹£á¹­hin}} and {{kÄyastha}} are associated with the judge. So the {{kÄyastha}} seems to be the same as the {{lekhaka}} of {{Bá¹›haspati}} and as a mere official. In the first centuries of the Christian era the {{kÄyastha}} was merely an officer and the word was possibly derived from or is a Sanskrit approximation of some foreign word for an officer, though in some parts of the country (as shown by {{UÅ›anas}} and {{Veda-VyÄsa}}) the {{kÄyasthas}} also had come to form a caste in medieval times. Though numerous officers are mentioned in the inscriptions of the first five or six centuries of the Christian era, the {{kÄyastha}} hardly ever figures among them. One of the earliest inscriptions where the word {{kÄyastha}} occurs is the Kanaswa Inscription of {{Åšivagaá¹‡a}} dated in the {{MÄlava}} era 795 (i. e. 738-39 A. D.). Vide I. A. vol. 19 pp. 56 and 59. See also E. I. vol. X. p. 79 for {{KÄyastha KÄÃ±cana}} in Balera plates in 994-95 A. D., E. I. vol. XI. p. 149 for {{ÅšÄsanika kÄyastha}} in Naraspatam plates of 1045-46 A. D., E. I. vol. XI. p. 20,25 for {{kÄyastha SurÄditya}} who was ‘{{SarvaÅ›Ästravit}}’ in {{Govindacandra}}’s plate dated 1129-30 A. D., E. I. 19 pp. 209, 213 AmodÄ plate dated 1161 A. D. (for {{VÄstavya kÄyastha}}), E. I. vol. 19 p. 45 Inscription of Hammira (dated 1288-89 A. D.), E. I. XII. p. 46 (dated 1328-29 A. D. for {{MÄthura kÄyastha}}), E. I. 20 Appendix No. 442 p. 64 (for {{Gauá¸a kÄyastha}}). In the {{RÄjatarangiá¹‡Ä«}}[^186] (VIII. 131) {{kÄyasthas}} and {{diviras}} are mentioned separately and in VIII. 2383 it is stated that the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a Åšivaratha}}, who was a roguish {{kÄyastha}}, was strangulated in the reign of {{Jayasimha}}. So in the 12th century {{kÄyasthas}} were only officers in Cashmir and {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} held such offices. Vide {{RÄjatarangiá¹‡Ä«}} V. 180-184 for a bitter diatribe against {{kÄyasthas}}.

The {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} (pp. 279-280) speaks of three sub-castes of {{kÄyasthas}}.

{{KÄrÄvara}}. According to Manu X. 36 he is a doubly mixed caste sprung from the union of a {{niá¹£Äda}} with a {{vaidehÄ«}} and his craft is that of a {{carmakÄra}}. According to later digests like the {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} he is called ‘{{kahÄra}}’ or ‘{{bhoi}}’ and holds torches and carries umbrellas for others.

{{KÄruá¹£a}}. According to Manu X. 23 this caste springs from the union of a {{vrÄtya vaiÅ›ya}} and a similar female. This caste is also called {{SudhanvÄcÄrya}}, {{Vijanman}}, {{Maitra}} and {{SÄtvata}} (according to Manu).

{{KirÄta}}. Vide above (p. 44) for vedic references. {{Veda-VyÄsa}} speaks of him as a subdivision of {{Å›Å«dra}} (note 184). According to Manu (X. 43-44) he is a {{ká¹£atriya}} reduced to the status of a {{Å›Å«dra}}. {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} (35.17-18) says the same about {{Mekalas}}, {{Draviá¸as}}, {{LÄá¹­as}}, {{Pauá¹‡á¸ras}}, {{Yavanas}} and others. {{Kará¹‡aparva}} 73. 20 speaks of {{kirÄtas}} as men of fiery valour. {{AÅ›vamedhika}} 73. 25 shows that Arjuna met {{kirÄtas}}, {{yavanas}} and {{Mlecchas}} when proceeding to the north with the {{AÅ›vamedha}} horse. The {{AmarakoÅ›a}} says that {{kirÄta}}, {{Å›abara}} and {{pulinda}} are subdivisions of {{Mleccha}} castes.

{{Kukkuá¹­a}}. According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} I. 8. 8 he is a {{pratiloma}} caste, but I. 9. 15 says that he is the child of the union of a {{Å›Å«dra}} with a {{niá¹£Äda}} woman, while I. 8. 12 states that he is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} from a {{niá¹£Äda}} woman.[^187] Manu X. 18 agrees with {{Baud. Dh.}} I. 9. 15. According to {{Kauá¹­.}} III. 7 he is the offspring of an {{ugra}} male from a {{niá¹£Äda}} female. According to {{Ä€dityapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted in the {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} he manufactures swords and other weapons and engages in cockfights for the king.

{{Kuá¹‡á¸a}}. According to Manu III. 174 he is the offspring of a clandestine intercourse between a married {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman whose husband is living and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male.

{{Kukunda}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{mÄgadha}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{KumbhakÄra}}. This word occurs in the {{gaá¹‡a kulÄlÄdi}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). {{UÅ›anas}} (32-33) says that he is the offspring of the clandestine union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 12) agrees with {{UÅ›anas}} and adds that the offspring becomes either a {{kumbhakÄra}} or a barber who shaves parts of the body above the navel. {{Veda-VyÄsa}} (I. 10-11) and Devala (quoted in {{Par. M.}} II. 1. p. 431 and {{Gá¹›. R.}} p. 337) include the potter among {{Å›Å«dras}}.[^188] In the Central Provinces the {{KumbhÄra}} is a scheduled caste.

{{KulÄla}}. For Vedic references see above (p.43). {{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118 explains the formation of {{kaulÄlakaá¹}} (made by a potter). The {{Ä€Å›v. Gá¹›.}} IV. 3. 18 says that all earthen vessels ({{kaulÄlaá¹}}) of a deceased {{agnihotrin}} should be kept by his son. Why two names arose for the caste of potters it is difficult to explain.

{{Kulika}}. This caste is mentioned in {{Åšaá¹…kha}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1175 (where {{AparÄrka}} explains it as {{devalaka}}).

{{KuÅ›Ä«lava}}. According to {{BaudhÄyana}} as quoted in the {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} he is the offspring of an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} from a {{vaidehaka}} female. According to Amara he is the same as a {{cÄraá¹‡a}} (a bard). According to {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7) he is the offspring of a {{vaidehaka}} male from an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} female (i. e. exactly the reverse of {{BaudhÄyana}}’s view) and he gives the name {{vaiá¹‡a}} to the offspring of an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} male from a {{vaidehaka}} female.

{{Ká¹›ta}}. According to {{Gaut.}} IV. 15 he is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. {{YÄj.}} I. 93 and others call this caste {{vaidehaka}}.

{{Kaivarta}}. In the Assam valley {{kaibartta}} is a scheduled caste. Vide under {{antyaja}} (p. 70) above. {{MedhÄtithi}} on Manu X. 4 says that this is a mixed caste. Manu X. 34 tells us that the inhabitants of {{Ä€ryÄvarta}} employ the name {{kaivarta}} to denote the offspring of a {{niá¹£Äda}} from an {{Äyogava}} woman, who is also called {{mÄrgava}} and {{dÄsa}} ({{dÄÅ›a}}?) and who subsists by plying boats. {{Åšaá¹…kara}} on {{VedÄntasÅ«tra}} II. 3. 43 says that {{dÄÅ›a}} and {{kaivarta}} are the same. Fick p. 302 notes that fishermen who work with nets and baskets were called in the {{JÄtakas}} {{kevaá¹­á¹­a}}.

{{Kolika}}. Mentioned as one of the {{antyajas}} by {{Veda-VyÄsa}}. Vide note 173 under {{antyaja}}. {{Koli}} is a scheduled caste in the Central Provinces and {{Kol}} in United Provinces.

{{Ká¹£attá¹›}}. For vedic references see above. According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} I. 9.7, {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), Manu X. 12, 13 and 16, {{YÄj.}} I. 94, {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 112), this is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung from a {{Å›Å«dra}} father and {{ká¹£atriya}} mother. Manu (X. 49-50) prescribes for him the same avocation as for {{ugra}} and {{pulkasa}}. {{Vas. Dh. S.}} 18. 2 calls him {{vaiá¹‡a}}. In the {{AmarakoÅ›a}} {{ká¹£attá¹›}} is given three meanings, a charioteer, doorkeeper and the caste described above. In {{ChÄndogya Up.}} IV. 1, 5, 7, 8 the word seems to mean only door-keeper. {{PÄá¹‡.}} notices the word (VI. 4.11). The {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 63-66) says that {{ká¹£attá¹›}} is also called {{niá¹£Äda}} and that he is an expert in catching deer by means of nets, is a forester and kills wild animals, also rings a bell at night to remind people of the hour.

{{Khanaka}}. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) he is the offspring of an {{Äyogava}} male and {{ká¹£atriya}} female and lives by digging.

{{Khasa}} or {{khaÅ›a}}. According to Manu X. 22 this is another name for {{karaá¹‡a}}; while in X. 43-44 he makes the {{khaÅ›as}} a {{ká¹£atriya}} caste originally but reduced to the status of {{Å›Å«dras}} by the absence of {{saá¹skÄras}} and the absence of contact with {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. Vide {{SabhÄ.}} 52. 3, {{Udyoga}} 160. 103.

{{Guhaka}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{Å›vapaca}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female.

{{Goja}} (or {{Goda}}). According to {{UÅ›anas}} (28-29) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{Gopa}}. Vide note 184. He is the modern {{Gavli}} and a {{Å›Å«dra}} subcaste. {{YÄj.}} II. 48 says that debts contracted by {{gopa}} wives must be paid by their husbands as the latter’s profession and earnings depend upon them. The {{KÄmasÅ«tra}} (I. 5. 37) mentions a caste called {{GopÄlaka}}.

{{Golaka}}. He is the offspring of a clandestine intercourse between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} widow and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male. Vide Manu III. 174, {{Laghu-ÅšÄtÄtapa}} 105, {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} ({{Åšiva}}. 12. 12).

{{Cakrin}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (22-23) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and subsists by (selling) oil and oilcakes or salt. He seems to be the same as {{tailika}}. {{HÄrÄ«ta}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 279) mentions him. According to {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1177 he is one who presses sesame. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) he is the offspring of a secret love affair between a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female and engages in the sale of salt and oil.

{{CarmakÄra}}. Vide under {{antyaja}} (p. 70) above. He is mentioned in many {{smá¹›tis}} like {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (51.8), {{Ä€pastamba}} (in verse 9.32), {{ParÄÅ›ara}} (VI. 44). According to {{UÅ›anas}} (4), he is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} girl, while verse 21 says that those sprung from a {{vaidehaka}} and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} girl subsist by working on hides. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) agrees with this latter. Manu IV. 218 speaks of him as {{carmÄvakartin}} (cutter of hides). According to several {{smá¹›tis}} he is one of the seven {{antyajas}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of an {{Äyogava}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female. This name persists to this day as {{cÄmbhÄr}} (in Western India) or {{cÄmÄr}} in all other Indian provinces. This caste is often spoken of as {{MochÄ«}} (shoe-maker). Often the {{mochÄ«s}} are Moslems. This is an untouchable caste and the population of {{carmakÄras}} in India is very large. In the Bombay Presidency alone they are about two hundred and ninety thousand (in 1931).

{{Cakrika}}. According to Amara he is a person who rings a bell. {{Ká¹£Ä«rasvÄmÄ«}} says that he announces the king’s arrival by ringing bells and that some regard him as the same as {{vaitÄlika}}. According to {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (prose) and {{Sumantu}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (pp. 1175â€“76) {{cakrika}} and {{tailika}} are separate subcastes. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 14) he is the offspring of a love affair between a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and his avocation is the sale of salt, oil and oil cakes.

{{CÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} (or {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}). For Vedic references see above pp. 44-45. Acc. to {{Gaut.}} IV. 15-16, {{Vas. Dh. S.}} 18.1, {{Baud. Dh. S.}} I. 9.7, Manu X. 12, {{YÄj.}} I. 93, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48. 11 he is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. He is the lowest among men (Manu X. 12), beyond the pale of religious observances prescribed for the four {{vará¹‡as}} ({{sarvadharma-bahiá¹£ká¹›ta}}, as {{YÄj.}} I. 93 says) and often spoken of in the same breath with dogs and crows (e. g. {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} II. 4. 9.5, {{Gaut.}} 15. 25, {{YÄj.}} I. 103).[^189] The word occurs in the {{kulÄlÄdi gaá¹‡a}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). {{Veda-VyÄsa-smá¹›ti}} (I. 9-10) says that there are three kinds of {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}}. viz. the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman, the offspring of an unmarried woman, the offspring of a union with a {{sagotra}} girl. Yama quoted in {{Par. M.}} vol. II. part 1 p. 306 says that one born of a person who after becoming an ascetic turns back to the householder’s life, one born of a {{sagotra}} woman and one who is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman are all {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}}. {{Laghu-ÅšÄtÄtapa}} (59) has a similar verse. Manu (X. 51-56) says that {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and {{Å›vapacas}} should have their houses outside the village, vessels used by them cannot be used by others (even after putting them in fire), their wealth consists of dogs and asses, their clothes should be the garments on corpses, they should take their food in broken vessels, their ornaments were to be made of iron, they should incessantly roam, they are not to enter towns and villages at night, they have to carry the corpses of persons who have no relatives, they are to be hangmen when the king so orders, they may take the clothes, ornaments and beds of persons that are to be hanged. {{UÅ›anas}} (9-10) says that their ornaments are to be of lead or iron, they should have a leather thong round their necks or cymbal under their armpits, they should remove the dirt of the village in the first part of the day. According to {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16.11 and 14 their avocation is to be hangmen or to kill those offenders who are condemned to death and {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} have to stay outside the village and to wear the clothes on corpses. {{ÅšÄnti}} 141.29-32 gives a graphic description of their hamlet. The classical description of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} hamlet is in the {{KÄdambarÄ«}} of {{BÄá¹‡a}}. In {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} (29.17) {{MÄtaá¹…ga}} is called a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} because he was born of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman from a barber. {{Fa Hien}} (405-411 A. D.) describes how {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} had to live apart and give notice of their approach when entering a town or market place by striking a piece of wood to make themselves known so that a man may avoid them and not come in contact with them (vide ‘Record of Buddhist kingdoms’ tr. by Legge, p. 43). In modern times {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} are returned as a scheduled caste in Madras and Orissa.

{{CÄ«na}}. According to Manu X. 43-44 the {{CÄ«nas}} were a {{ká¹£atriya}} caste reduced to the status of {{Å›Å«dras}}. They are frequently named in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} e. g. {{SabhÄ}} 51. 23, {{Vanaparva}} 177. 1, {{Udyoga}} 19. 15.

{{CuÃ±cu}}. According to Manu X. 48 the avocation of {{Meda}}, {{Andhra}}, {{CuÃ±cu}} and {{Madgu}} is to kill forest (or wild) animals. {{KullÅ«ka}} says that he is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{vaidehaka}} woman.

{{Cucuka}}. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) he is the offspring of the marriage of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} with a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman and his avocation is the sale and purchase of betelnut, betelnut leaves and sugar.

{{Cailanirá¹‡ejaka}} (or only {{nirá¹‡ejaka}})-washerman. This occurs in {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 51. 15 and Manu IV. 216. It appears that {{Viá¹£á¹‡u}} who separately mentions ‘{{rajaka}}’ (in 51. 13) distinguishes between the two. {{HÄrÄ«ta}} (quoted in {{AparÄrka}} p. 279) also distinguishes between ‘{{rajaka}}’ (one who dyes clothes) from ‘{{nirá¹‡ejaka}}’ (one who washes clothes). Very often ‘{{rajaka}}’ means only ‘a washerman’ (as Amara says).

{{JÄlopajÄ«vin}} (subsisting by catching animals in a net) probably the same as a {{kaivarta}}. He is mentioned in {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 279.

{{Jhalla}}. Manu X. 22 says that it is another name for {{Karaá¹‡a}} and {{KhaÅ›a}}. In Bengal {{Jhalo MÄlo}} or {{MÄlo}} is returned as a scheduled caste.

{{Domba}} (or {{Doma}}) is the same as {{Å›vapaca}} according to {{Ká¹£Ä«rasvÄmÄ«}} on Amara. {{ParÄÅ›ara}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (p. 1197) puts {{Å›vapÄka}}, {{domba}} and {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} on the same level. In the {{RÄjatarangiá¹‡Ä«}} (V.354, VI. 182, VI. 192) {{dombas}} are spoken of as untouchables on the same level with {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and as following the profession of singers. In {{RÄjatarangiá¹‡Ä«}} V. 389-394 {{dombas}} are spoken of as {{Å›vapacas}}. This caste is returned as a scheduled caste in Madras as {{Dombo}}, as {{Dom}} in Bengal, Bihar and U. P.

{{Taká¹£an}} or {{Taká¹£aka}} (Carpenter). For vedic references see above (p. 43). He is the same as {{vardhaki}} quoted above, under {{kÄyastha}}. He is mentioned by Manu IV. 210, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 51. 8. According to the {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} on {{PÄá¹‡.}} II. 4. 10 the {{taká¹£an}} is a {{Å›Å«dra}} and on the same level with an {{ayaskÄra}} (ironsmith) and excluded from sacrificial rites. Even so early as the {{Åšat. Br.}} the {{taká¹£an}} was an unholy person ({{amedhya}}) and his touch made {{yajÃ±apÄtras}} impure (I. 1.3. 12). {{UÅ›anas}} 43 says that {{taká¹£aka}} is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female from a {{sÅ«caka}} (i. e. he is a {{pratiloma}}). He must be supposed to be originally different from the {{rathakÄra}} (vide {{Tait. S.}} quoted above p. 43) who was allowed the vedic rite of {{ÄdhÄna}} or probably in the time of the {{smá¹›tis}} the carpenter lost his former status. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 14)[^190] the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} girl from a {{cucuka}} ({{sÅ«caka}}?) is a {{taká¹£aka}} who is untouchable or a goldsmith or blacksmith or worker in bell-metal. {{Bá¹›haspati}} names the {{taká¹£aka}} as one whose food a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} could not eat.

{{TantuvÄya}} (a weaver), also called {{kuvinda}}. He is mentioned in {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (51. 13) and {{Åšaá¹…kha}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1175. He is regarded as a {{Å›Å«dra}} by the {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} on {{PÄá¹‡.}} II. 4. 10 and as excluded from sacrificial rites.

{{TÄmbÅ«lika}}-seems to have been a caste. Vide {{KÄmasÅ«tra}} I. 5. 37. {{BÄá¹‡a}} refers to his house as stored with spices and betel.

{{TÄmropajÄ«vin}} (coppersmith). According to {{UÅ›anas}} (14) he is the offspring of an {{Äyogava}} from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) says the same and calls him {{tÄmra}}. The {{JÄtiviveka}} (D. C. No. 347 of 1887-91) gives the name as {{tÄmra-kuá¹­á¹­aka}}.

{{TunnavÄya}} (a tailor). He occurs in Manu IV. 214. According to the {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1178 he is the same as {{sÅ«cÄ«}} (or {{saucika}}).

{{Tailika}} (oilman)-occurs in {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (51. 15), {{Åšaá¹…kha}} and {{Sumantu}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (pp. 1175 and 1176).

{{Darada}}. Manu X. 44 names him. Vide note on {{khaÅ›a}} above p. 79. Vide {{MahÄbhÄrata}}, {{Udyoga parva}} 4. 15.

{{DÄÅ›a}} (a fisherman). This occurs in an {{Upaniá¹£ad}} passage according to {{VedÄnta sÅ«tra}} II. 3. 43. {{Veda-VyÄsa}} (I. 12-13) includes him among {{antyajas}} (vide footnote 173). Manu X. 34 says that {{mÄrgava}}, {{dÄÅ›a}} and {{kaivarta}} are the same and that he subsists by plying boats. {{PÄá¹‡.}} (III. 4. 73) derives the word. Vide under {{kaivarta}}.

{{DivÄkÄ«rti}}-occurs in {{MÄnava-gá¹›hya}} II. 14. 11. According to Amara both {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} and {{nÄpita}} are called {{divÄkÄ«rti}}.

{{Dauá¹£manta}}. According to Gaut. 4. 14 this is an {{anuloma}} caste sprung from the union of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} the name is {{Dauá¹£yanta}}.

{{Draviá¸a}}. The same as {{karaá¹‡a}}, according to Manu X. 22. According to Manu X. 43-44, this is a {{ká¹£atriya}} caste reduced to the status of a {{Å›Å«dra}}. Vide under {{khaÅ›a}}, and {{MahÄbhÄrata}}, {{Udyoga}} 160. 103, {{Droá¹‡a}} 93, 43.

{{Dhigvaá¹‡a}}. According to Manu X. 15 he is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male from an {{Äyogava}} female and according to Manu X. 49 he subsists by working on (and sale of) hides. {{KullÅ«ka}} on Manu X. 49 quotes a text of {{UÅ›anas}} that {{Dhigvaá¹‡as}} subsist by working on hides and selling them and distinguishes them from {{kÄrÄvaras}}. This passage is not found in the printed text of {{UÅ›anas}}. In the {{JÄtiviveka}} he is styled {{MocikÄra}}.

{{DhÄ«vara}}. Same as {{kaivarta}} and {{dÄÅ›a}}. {{Gaut.}} (IV. 17) indicates that this is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and a {{ká¹£atriya}} female (according to the view of some teachers). In the {{Tait. Br.}} III. 4. 12 {{Dhaivara}}, {{DÄÅ›a}} and {{Kaivarta}} are separately mentioned, but how they were distinguished it is difficult to say. All caught fish in different ways. {{DhÄ«mar}} is a scheduled caste in the Bhandara District of C.P.

{{Dhvajin}} (seller of wines mentioned by {{Sumantu}} and {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1176. The {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (p. 1177) says he is the same as {{Å›auá¹‡á¸ika}}.

{{Naá¹­a}}. He is one of the seven {{antyajas}} (vide note 170 above). {{Naá¹­a}} is a scheduled caste in Bengal, Bihar, U. P. and Punjab. {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (p. 279) distinguishes between {{naá¹­a}} and {{Å›ailÅ«á¹£a}} and {{AparÄrka}} remarks that {{naá¹­a}} is a specific caste, while {{Å›ailÅ«á¹£a}} is one who though not a {{naá¹­a}} by caste makes a living by going on the stage. Manu X. 22 says that he is the same as {{karaá¹‡a}}, {{khaÅ›a}} (vide note on {{khaÅ›a}} p. 79). {{PÄá¹‡ini}} knew of a {{Naá¹­asÅ«tra}} composed by {{ÅšilÄlin}} and another by {{Ká¹›Å›ÄÅ›va}} (IV. 3. 110 and 111), but whether the {{naá¹­as}} he meant were so by caste or whether he meant only actors in general is not clear. Vide under {{raá¹…gÄvatÄrin}} for a quotation from {{MaitrÄ« Up.}} Probably this caste is the same as the {{KolhÄá¹­is}} or {{BahurÅ«pis}} of modern times (in the Deccan) who are dancers, acrobats and jugglers by profession. Fick’s work p. 299 speaks of {{naá¹­akakulas}} mentioned in Buddhist {{JÄtakas}} and at p. 294 he shows that in the {{Suruci JÄtaka}} the {{naá¹­a}} is described as throwing up a ball of rope and climbing it and performing other tricks of jugglery.

{{Nartaka}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (19) he is the offspring of a {{raÃ±jaka}} from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman and his profession is that of a singer. {{Bá¹›haspati}} quoted in {{Par. M.}} (II. part I p. 380) separately mentions in the same verse {{naá¹­a}} and {{nartaka}} (both being {{abhojyÄnna}} to a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}). Atri VII. 2 also mentions the two separately. See note 189.

{{NÄpita}} (barber). The {{SÄá¹…khÄyana-gá¹›hya}} I. 25 refers to him (in {{cÅ«á¸Äkarma}}). {{UÅ›anas}} (32-34) and {{Vaikh.}} (X. 12) say that he is the offspring of a clandestine affair between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and {{UÅ›anas}} derives the word by saying that he shaves the body above the navel of a person.[^191] {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) says that the offspring of an {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}} male from a {{ká¹£atriya}} female is a {{nÄpita}}, but he shaves the hair on the body below the navel. {{ParÄÅ›ara}} (XI. 21)[^192] says that the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} girl is called {{dÄsa}} if {{saá¹skÄras}} are performed on him, but if they are not performed he becomes a {{nÄpita}}. {{PÄá¹‡.}} (VI. 2. 62) specially provides for the accentuation of words meaning artisan ({{Å›ilpin}}, like {{nÄpita}} or {{kulÄla}}) when compounded with the word {{grÄma}}. The {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} ({{Åšiva-chap.}} XII. 15) says that the offspring of the clandestine union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female is called {{kumbhakÄra}} or {{Å«rdhva-nÄpita}}, while verse 32 says that the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} female from a {{niá¹£Äda}} is called ‘{{adho-nÄpita}}’.

{{Nicchivi}}. Manu X. 22 says that this is another name for {{karaá¹‡a}} or {{khaÅ›a}}. Probably this is a misreading for {{Licchavi}} or {{Licchivi}}. {{Kauá¹­.}} ({{ArthaÅ›Ästra}} XI. 1) speaks of the oligarchies or corporations of {{Licchivika}}, {{Vá¹›jika}}, {{Mallaka}}[^193] etc. {{VaiÅ›ÄlÄ«}} was the seat of this oligarchy in the 6th century B. C. (Rapson’s ‘Ancient India’ p. 169). Vide Indian Antiquary vol. 32 p. 233 for their Tibetan affinities and I. A. vol. 9 p. 178 and vol. 14 p. 98 for inscriptions of this tribe and Indian Historical Quarterly, 1933, vol. 18 p. 439 ff. {{Fa Hien}} (in ‘Records of Buddhist Kingdoms’ translated by Legge, 1886, p. 71) says that the {{Licchavis}} of {{VaiÅ›ÄlÄ«}} were attached to Buddha and also speaks of {{Ä€nanda}} (pupil of Buddha) and the {{Licchavis}}.

{{Niá¹£Äda}}. For vedic references see above (pp. 43 and 46). The {{Nirukta}}[^194] III. 8 while explaining the words ‘{{paÃ±cajanÄ mama hotraá¹ juá¹£adhvam}}’ in {{á¹šg.}} X. 53. 4 remarks that according to {{Aupamanyava}} the ‘five people’ are the four {{vará¹‡as}} with {{niá¹£Äda}} as the fifth. This shows that {{Aupamanyava}} regarded them as distinct from the four {{vará¹‡as}} (including the {{Å›Å«dras}}). The {{Nirukta}} further says that according to the {{Nairuktas}} the {{niá¹£Äda}} is so called because ‘sin (or evil) sits down in him’; from this it follows that in the times of {{YÄska}} the {{niá¹£Ädas}} had come to be looked down upon with scorn as evil people and were probably some aborigines like the modern Bhils. According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9.3 and II. 2.33), {{Vas.}} 18.8, Manu X. 8, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48.5, {{YÄj.}} I. 91 the {{Niá¹£Äda}} is an {{anuloma}} caste, offspring of the marriage of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman. {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (II. 2.34), {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), Manu (X. 8) and {{YÄj.}} (I. 91) say that {{pÄraÅ›ava}} is another name for {{niá¹£Äda}}. Manu (IX. 178) derives {{pÄraÅ›ava}} from the root {{pá¹›}} (to go across) and the word {{Å›ava}} (a corpse) meaning ’though living he is like one dead’. In a corrupt passage of {{Vas. Dh. S.}}[^195] (18. 9-10) the same derivation is accepted. {{Gaut.}} (IV. 14) distinguishes between {{niá¹£Äda}} and {{pÄraÅ›ava}}, the former (acc. to the com. {{Haradatta}}) being the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman and the latter being the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. According to {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} v. 108) and {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48.12, {{niá¹£Äda}} is the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female and he subsists by fishing (according to the latter). {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 108) says that {{pÄraÅ›ava}} is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. {{UÅ›anas}} (36-38) says that the offspring of the marriage of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman is called {{pÄraÅ›ava}} and gains his livelihood as the worshipper of the goddess {{BhadrakÄlÄ«}} or by learning {{Åšaiva Ägamas}} or by playing on musical instruments, while {{niá¹£Äda}} is the illegitimate offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman who subsists by killing wild beasts (such as elephants) and by selling flesh. The {{Vaikh.}}[^196] (X. 13) has practically the same remarks to make about {{pÄraÅ›ava}} and {{niá¹£Äda}}. {{ÅšÄntiparva}} (59. 96-97) speaks of {{niá¹£Ädas}} as men with reddish eyes and with black hair. In the {{RÄmÄyaá¹‡a}} {{Guha}}, the king of {{niá¹£Ädas}}, helps {{RÄma}} to cross the river Ganges ({{AyodhyÄ}} 50. 33). Here he is spoken of as belonging to the caste of {{niá¹£Ädas}} ({{niá¹£ÄdajÄtyaá¸¥}}). We have historical evidence for the name {{pÄraÅ›ava}} in the {{Hará¹£acarita}} (I, towards end), where {{BÄá¹‡a}} tells us that he had with him two {{pÄraÅ›ava}} brothers {{Candrasena}} and {{MÄtá¹›á¹£eá¹‡a}} in his travels.[^197] In the Tipperah copperplate of a chieftain called {{LokanÄtha}} (about 650 A. D.) we are told that the king’s maternal grandfather {{KeÅ›ava}} was a {{pÄraÅ›ava}} (vide E. I. vol. 15 at p. 307). The {{Mit.}} on {{YÄj.}} I. 91 cautions that this {{niá¹£Äda}} (the {{anuloma}} offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{Å›Å«dra}} woman) is different from another caste called {{niá¹£Äda}}, which is a {{pratiloma}} one and lives by catching fish. {{MedhÄtithi}} on Manu X. 8 does the same. {{Niá¹£Äda}} occurs in the {{gaá¹‡a kulÄlÄdi}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). The {{VÄyupurÄá¹‡a}} (vol. II. chap. 1. 120-121) narrates that the {{niá¹£Ädas}}, dark and dwarfish, were produced from the left hand of king Vena and they dwelt on the {{Vindhya}}. In the {{BhÄgavata-purÄá¹‡a}} we have the same story[^198] (IV. 14. 42 ff.) and we are told that {{niá¹£Ädas}} were dark like crows, of short stature, snubnosed, with red eyes and hair and that they dwelt in mountains and forests.

{{Pahlava}}–occurs in Manu (X. 43-44) as a {{ká¹£atriya}} caste reduced to the status of {{Å›Å«dra}}. Vide note on {{khaÅ›a}}. The {{MahÄbhÄrata}} several times mentions the {{Pahlavas}}, {{PÄradas}} and other non-Aryan people (vide {{SabhÄ}} 32. 16-17, {{Udyoga}} 4. 15, {{BhÄ«á¹£ma}} 20. 13).

{{PÄá¹‡á¸usopÄka}}. According to Manu X. 37 he is the offspring of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} male from a {{vaidehaka}} woman, who maintains himself by dealing in bamboos. He is the same as {{buruá¸a}}.

{{PÄrada}}. Vide Manu X. 43-44 and note on {{khaÅ›a}} above. {{PÄradas}} are frequently mentioned in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}}, generally as {{Mlecchas}} and non-Aryan tribes (vide {{SabhÄ}} 32. 16, 51. 12, 52. 3; {{Droá¹‡a}} 93. 42 and 121. 13). Vide under {{Yavana}}.

{{PÄraÅ›ava}}. Vide {{niá¹£Äda}} above. In the {{Ä€diparva}} 109, 25 {{Vidura}} is called {{pÄraÅ›ava}} and he is said (in {{Ä€di.}} 114.12) to have married the {{pÄraÅ›avÄ«}} daughter of king {{Devaka}}. The {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 43) says that he is called {{Å›Å«dra}} and is superior to a {{Å›Å«dra}} (in status) and that he was to live by the avocations prescribed for {{Å›Å«dras}}.

{{Piá¹…gala}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from an {{Äyogava}} female.

{{Puá¹‡á¸ra}} or {{Pauá¹‡á¸raka}}. Frequently mentioned in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} among non-Aryan tribes e. g. {{Droá¹‡a}} 93. 44, {{Ä€Å›vamedhika}} 29. 15-16. {{MedhÄtithi}} on Manu X. 44 remarks that the {{Pauá¹‡á¸rakas}} are spoken of as {{ká¹£atriyas}} in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}}, so a man may be misled into thinking that even in the days of {{MedhÄtithi}} they were still {{ká¹£atriyas}} (but they were not so).

{{Pulinda}}. For vedic references see above (p. 47). They were a wild mountain tribe like the {{KirÄtas}} or {{Åšabaras}}. In the {{Vanaparva}} 140. 25 {{Pulindas}}, {{KirÄtas}} and {{Taá¹…gaá¹‡as}} are spoken of as dwelling on the {{HimÄlaya}}. {{Udyoga}} 160. 103, {{Kará¹‡a}} 73. 19-20, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 33. 21-22 mention {{pulindas}}. {{UÅ›anas}} (16) says that he is the illegitimate offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male from a {{ká¹£atriya}} female and he lives by rearing cattle and by killing wild beasts. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 14) says the same and adds that he subsists on the produce of the forest and by killing wild animals. The {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} is of the same opinion as {{Vaikh.}}

{{Pulkasa}} (or {{paulkasa}}). The word is also written as {{pukkasa}}. {{Åšaá¹…karÄcÄrya}} on {{Bá¹›hadÄraá¹‡yaka Up.}} IV. 3. 22 says that {{pulkasa}} and {{paulkasa}} are the same. According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9. 14) and Manu X. 18 he is the offspring of a {{niá¹£Äda}} male from a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. According to {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7) he is the offspring of a {{niá¹£Äda}} male from an {{ugra}} female, while {{Vaikh.}} X. 14, {{UÅ›anas}} (17) and the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} say that he is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} male from a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman and that he makes his livelihood by manufacturing and selling[^199] liquors or natural intoxicating sap. The same is the view of some teachers according to Gautama IV. 17. According to {{Vas. Dh. S.}} (18. 5) and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (16.5) he is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male from a {{ká¹£atriya}} female and the latter adds that he subsists by hunting. The {{AgnipurÄá¹‡a}} (151. 15) says that {{pukkasas}} are hunters. Yama and {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted in the {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} make him out as the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} male from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female. Manu X. 49 prescribes catching and killing animals that live in holes as the occupation of {{ká¹£attá¹›}}, {{ugra}} and {{pukkasa}}. {{Ká¹£Ä«rasvÄmÄ«}} (com. of Amara) says that {{pukkasa}} is the same as {{má¹›tapa}} (which see below). {{Ä€p. Dh. S.}} (II. 1. 2. 6) says that when a {{BrÄhmaá¹‡a}}, {{Ká¹£atriya}} or {{VaiÅ›ya}} is guilty of the theft of gold, of the murder of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, they undergo for some time in hell torments and then are respectively born as {{CÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}, {{Paulkasa}} or {{Vaiá¹‡a}}.

{{Puá¹£kara}}. Mentioned in {{Veda-VyÄsa-smá¹›ti}} (I. 12) as one of the {{antyajas}}. Vide note 173 above.

{{Puá¹£padha}}. Manu (X. 21) says that this is another name of {{Ävantya}} (p. 73).

{{Buruá¸a}} (worker in bamboo). He is one of the seven {{antyajas}} (vide note 170 above). The word is also written as {{varuá¸a}} (vide below). {{Varuá¸a}} occurs in the {{KulÄlÄdigana}} ({{PÄá¹‡ini}} IV. 3. 118). {{Buruá¸a}} is a scheduled caste in Orissa.

{{Bhaá¹­a}}. He is one of the {{antyajas}} according to {{Veda-VyÄsa}} (I. 12). Vide note 173 above and on {{raá¹…gÄvatÄrin}} below.

{{Bhilla}}. He is one of the {{antyajas}} according to {{Aá¹…giras}}, Atri 199, Yama 33. Vide note 170 above.

{{Bhiá¹£ak}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (26) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{ká¹£atriya}} girl and he maintains himself by studying the {{Ä€yurveda}} in its eight parts, or astronomy, astrology and mathematics (verse 27). {{BhÅ«pa}}. According to Yama quoted in {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} he is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} female.

{{BhÅ«rjakaá¹‡á¹­aka}}. According to Manu X. 21 he is the offspring of a {{vrÄtya brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a similar woman. In several countries he is called {{Ävantya}} or {{vÄtadhÄna}}, {{puá¹£padha}} or {{Å›aikha}}.

{{Bhrjjakaá¹‡á¹­ha}} (same as {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}). According to the view of some teachers mentioned in Gaut. IV. 17, he is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female.

{{Bhoja}}. According to the {{SÅ«ta-saá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male.

{{Madgu}}. Mentioned in Manu X. 48 as subsisting by killing wild beasts. {{KullÅ«ka}} on that verse explains that according to {{BaudhÄyana}} he is the offspring of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} from a {{bandin}} female. {{Vaikh.}} X. 12 says that he is the offspring of the marriage of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male with a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and subsists as a merchant ({{Å›reá¹£á¹­hin}}) and does not take to the profession of a warrior.

{{Maá¹‡ikÄra}}.
