{{Matsyabandhaka}} (a fisherman). According to {{UÅ›anas}} (44) he is the offspring of a {{taká¹£aka}} (carpenter) from a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman.

{{Malla}}. Vide Manu (X. 22) who says that it is another name of {{Jhalla}} etc.

{{MÄgadha}}. According to Gaut. IV. 15, {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 48. 12, {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), Manu X. 11, 17 and {{YÄj.}} I. 93 he is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung from the union of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and a {{ká¹£atriya}} female; while according to the view of some teachers mentioned in Gaut. IV. 16, {{UÅ›anas}} (7) and {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) he is the offspring of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female; but {{Baud. Dh. S.}} I. 9.7 says he is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} female and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 16.5 makes him the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} female. Manu (X. 47) prescribes trade by land routes for {{MÄgadhas}}; {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} X. 48 says that they maintain themselves on speech (i. e. by praise) and are also called {{bandin}}. {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 60-62) also calls him {{bandin}} and says he is slightly superior to {{Å›Å«dra}} and that he is clever in ornate prose, in six languages, in {{kalÄs}} and he is a devotee of {{KÄlikÄ}}. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) states that even {{Å›Å«dras}} cannot take food cooked by him, that he is an untouchable and maintains himself by praise, by singing or by being a messenger. {{UÅ›anas}} (7-8) says that he becomes the {{bandin}} of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and {{ká¹£atriyas}}, he maintains himself by lauding (the deeds of others) or as a messenger or servant of a {{vaiÅ›ya}}. {{PÄá¹‡ini}} IV. 1. 70 derives the word {{MÄgadha}} from the country {{Magadha}}, but apparently not in the sense of a caste.

{{MÄá¹‡avika}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of the clandestine union of a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{MÄtaá¹…ga}}. The same as {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}. The {{KÄdambarÄ«}} of {{BÄá¹‡a}} (para 9) and Amara employ them as synonymous. Yama (12) uses the word {{MÄtaá¹…ga}} where we ordinarily find {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} in other {{smá¹›tis}}. In the Bombay Presidency there is an untouchable caste called {{MÄá¹…g}} which numbers about three hundred thousand. There are {{MÄá¹…gs}} as a scheduled caste in Orissa.

{{MÄrgava}}. Same as {{kaivarta}} (fisherman). Vide Manu X. 34.

{{MÄlÄkÄra}} or {{MÄlika}}. Vide {{Veda-VyÄsa}} (I. 10-11) quoted above in note 184.

{{MÄhiá¹£ya}}. According to the view of some teachers mentioned in Gaut. IV. 17 and {{YÄj.}} I. 92 he is an {{anuloma}} caste sprung from the marriage of a {{ká¹£atriya}} with a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman. According to the {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 45â€“46) he is entitled to {{upanayana}} and his avocations are astrology, augury, prognostications due to {{svara}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the same as {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}.

{{MÅ«rdhÄvasikta}}. This is an {{anuloma}} caste according to the view of some teachers in Gaut. IV. 17 and {{YÄj.}} I. 91 sprung from the marriage of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 12) the offspring of the marriage of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} with a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman is the foremost among {{anulomas}} and is called {{savará¹‡a}}, while the offspring of the clandestine union of these two is called {{abhiá¹£ikta}}. If crowned he becomes a king, otherwise he subsists by studying {{Ä€yurveda}} (Medicine) or the science of ghosts or astronomy, astrology and mathematics. In the {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (chap. 26. 36-39) more details are added. [[92]]

{{Má¹›tapa}}. Vide {{pulkasa}} above. In the {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}}[^200] on {{PÄá¹‡.}} II. 4. 10 this caste is mentioned as separate from {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}, but both are included by the {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} under {{Å›Å«dras}} that are {{niravasita}} (i. e. those who are so low in the caste system that if a vessel is used by them for eating their food from it cannot be used by any one of the other castes even after the vessel is purified by fire &c.).

{{Meda}}. He is one of the seven {{antyajas}} (vide note 170). This is mentioned in {{NÄrada}} ({{vÄkpÄruá¹£ya}}, verse 11). {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 22. 22 speaks of {{medas}}, {{pulakasas}} and {{antÄvasÄyins}}, and the com. {{NÄ«lakaá¹‡á¹­ha}}[^201] explains that {{Medas}} are those who eat the flesh of dead cattle. Manu X. 36 says that he is the offspring of a {{vaidehaka}} male and a {{niá¹£Äda}} woman, that he dwells outside the village and Manu X. 48 says that his business along with that of {{Andhra}}, {{CuÃ±cu}} and {{Madgu}} is to kill wild beasts. Vide under {{Andhra}} above and see E. I. vol. XX. p. 130 where {{meda}} and {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} are named as lowest castes in a grant of {{ParamÄrdideva}} in {{saá¹vat}} 1236. According to {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}}, {{Ändhras}} and {{medas}} have to sweep dirty places, doorsteps and privies.

{{Maitra}}. Manu X. 23 says he is the same as {{KÄruá¹£a}}.

{{Maitreyaka}}. According to Manu X. 33 he is the offspring of a {{vaidehaka}} male and {{Äyogava}} female and his means of livelihood are belauding kings and nobles continually and ringing a bell in the early morning. The {{JÄtiviveka}} says that he is called {{DhokanakÄra}}.

{{Mleccha}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of the clandestine union of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman and a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male.

{{Yavana}}. According to the view of some mentioned in Gaut. IV. 17 he is a {{pratiloma}} caste sprung from a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman. According to Manu X. 43-44 {{Yavanas}} were originally {{ká¹£atriyas}} reduced to the status of {{Å›Å«dras}} (vide under {{khaÅ›a}} above p. 79). In the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} {{Yavanas}} are almost always associated with {{Åšakas}} and several other non-Aryan tribes; vide {{SabhÄ.}} 32. 16-17, {{Vanaparva}} 254. 18 (as inhabiting the west), {{Udyoga}} 19.21, {{BhÄ«á¹£ma}} 20. 13, {{Droá¹‡a}} 93. 42 and 121. 13, {{Kará¹‡a}} 73. 19, {{ÅšÄnti}} 65. 13. From {{StrÄ«parva}} 22. 11 it appears that {{Jayadratha}} (king of the countries of {{Sindhu}} and {{SauvÄ«ra}}) had {{Kamboja}} and {{Yavana}} women in his harem. {{PÄá¹‡ini}} (IV. 1. 49) derives the word {{YavanÄnÄ«}} from {{Yavana}} and a {{vÄrtika}} on that {{sÅ«tra}} adds that {{YavanÄnÄ«}} means the {{lipi}} (the written alphabet) of the {{Yavanas}}. {{PataÃ±jali}} in his {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} (on {{PÄá¹‡.}} II. 4. 10) indicates that he was prepared to regard {{Yavanas}} and {{Åšakas}} as {{Å›Å«dras}} but as outside {{Ä€ryÄvarta}}. {{AÅ›oka}} in Rock Edicts V and XIII refers to the {{Yonas}} and {{Kambojas}} as nations on the borders of his empire. The {{Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a}} IV. 3. 21 describes that the {{Yavanas}} shaved the head and also the beard, the {{Åšakas}} tonsured their heads only, the {{PÄradas}} allowed the hair on the head to grow long and the {{Pahlavas}} grew beards and that all became {{Mlecchas}} because they gave up their original {{dharmas}} and because {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} also left them.

{{Raá¹…gÄvatÄrin}} (or -{{tÄraka}}). According to Manu IV. 215 he is distinct from {{Å›ailÅ«á¹£a}} and {{gÄyana}}. {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1175), {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (17. 36) and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 51. 14 mention him. According to {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1178) he is a {{naá¹­a}} who goes on the stage for livelihood and who introduces various changes in his appearance and dress. {{Raá¹…gÄvatÄrin}} is mentioned in the {{MaitrÄ« Up.}} along with {{naá¹­a}} and {{bhaá¹­a}}.[^202]

{{Rajaka}} (washerman). {{Dhobi}} is a scheduled caste in U. P., Bihar and C. P. and in Bengal (called {{DhobÄ}}). He is one of the {{antyajas}} according to several writers (vide note 170 above). According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) and the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{pulkasa}} (or {{vaideha}}) from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman, while according to {{UÅ›anas}} (18) he is the offspring of a {{pulkasa}} male from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} girl. The {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}} on {{PÄá¹‡.}} (II. 4. 10) includes him under {{Å›Å«dra}}. {{YÄj.}} II. 48 mentions him as liable to pay his wife’s debts, because his livelihood depends on her. The {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} mentioned another caste of {{rajaka}} (which is the offspring of an {{ugra}} male and {{vaidehaka}} female).

{{RaÃ±jaka}} (dyer). Manu IV. 216 mentions him. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (19) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{ká¹£atriya}} female.

{{RathakÄra}}. Vide above pp. 43, 45 for Vedic references. According to {{Baud. gá¹›.}} II. 5. 6 and {{BhÄradvÄja gá¹›.}} (I) his {{upanayana}} was to be performed in the rainy season. It appears he lost his status gradually. In the {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9. 6) he is the offspring of the marriage of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male with a {{Å›Å«dra}} female. According to {{YÄj.}} I. 95 he is an {{anuloma}} doubly mixed as he is the offspring of a {{mÄhiá¹£ya}} male from a {{karaá¹‡a}} female and according to a prose passage of {{Åšaá¹…kha}} quoted by the {{Mit.}} the {{rathakÄra}} is entitled to have {{upanayana}} performed, to offer sacrifices and gifts and makes his living by learning the art of taming horses, of making chariots and building houses. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (5-6) and {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman and he is to behave like a {{Å›Å«dra}}, he is not a twice-born ({{dvija}}) and cannot repeat Vedic mantras and makes his living by tending and driving horses and carts.

{{RÄmaka}}. According to {{Vas. Dh. S.}} 18. 4 he is a {{pratiloma}} sprung from the union of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female. This would be {{ká¹›ta}}, according to {{Gaut.}} (IV. 15), {{vaidehaka}} according to {{BaudhÄyana}}.

{{Vandin}} (a bard, written as {{bandin}} also). According to {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted in {{Ká¹›tyakalpataru}} he is a {{pratiloma}} sprung from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male and {{ká¹£atriya}} female. According to the {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1177 a {{vandin}} is one who sings the praises of men.

{{VÄtadhÄna}}. According to Manu X. 21 he is the same as {{Ävantya}}.

{{Vena}} (or {{Vaiá¹‡a}}). According to Manu X. 19 and {{Baud. Dh. {{Velava}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a {{Å›Å«dra}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman by stealth.

{{Vaidehaka}}. According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9. 8), {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), Manu X. 11, 13, 17, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. {{VyÄdha}} (huntsman)-mentioned by {{Sumantu}} in {{AparÄrka}} p. 1176 and {{HÄrÄ«ta}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 279), {{Ä€pastamba}} (Verse) 9. 32, {{YÄj.}} II. 48.

{{Åšaka}}. Manu (X. 43-44) mentions {{Åšakas}} along with {{Yavanas}} and others as originally {{ká¹£atriyas}} reduced to the state of {{Å›Å«dras}}. Vide note on {{Yavana}} (p. 92) above. Vide note 200.

{{Åšabara}}. An aboriginal jungle tribe like the {{Bhilla}}. In the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} the {{Å›abaras}} are frequently mentioned (e. g. {{AnuÅ›Äsana}} 35. 17, {{ÅšÄnti}} 65. 13).

{{ÅšÄlika}}. According to the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the same as {{MÄgadha}}.

{{ÅšÅ«lika}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (42) he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female and his avocation is to impale those offenders who are sentenced to be impaled. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) and {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} he is the offspring of a clandestine union between a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{Åšaikha}}. According to Manu X. 21 he is the same as {{Ävantya}}.

{{ÅšailÅ«á¹£a}}. {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 51. 13, Manu IV. 214, {{HÄrÄ«ta}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 279 distinguish him from {{raá¹…gÄvatÄrÄ«}} and the {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1178) defines him as one who finds out employment for {{naá¹­as}}.[^204] {{Ä€pastamba}} 9. 32 (verse) speaks of him in the same breath with {{rajaka}} and {{vyÄdha}}. {{YÄj.}} II. 48 does the same and the {{Mit.}} thereon paraphrases the word by {{naá¹­a}}.

{{Åšauá¹‡á¸ika}} (wine-seller). {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 51. 15, Manu IV. 216, {{YÄj.}} II. 48, {{Åšaá¹…kha}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1175) and {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1177) mention him.

{{Åšvapaca}} or {{ÅšvapÄka}}. He is one of the {{antyajas}} enumerated by {{Veda-VyÄsa}} (I. 12-13). Vide note 173 above. He occurs in the {{gaá¹‡a kulÄlÄdi}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). According to {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9, 12), {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), he is the offspring of an {{ugra}} male from a female of the {{ká¹£attá¹›}} sub-caste, while according to Manu X. 19 he is the offspring of a {{ká¹£attá¹›}} male from an {{ugra}} female. {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) and {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} say that he is the offspring of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} female, while {{UÅ›anas}} (11) holds that he is the offspring of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} male from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} woman. According to Manu X. 51-56 {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} and {{Å›vapacas}} follow the same avocation and are governed by the same rules (vide p. 81 under {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}}). {{UÅ›anas}} (12) says that they eat the flesh of dogs and that dogs are their wealth, while {{Vaikh.}} says that they wear the same marks that {{cÄá¹‡á¸Älas}} have to wear (under the king’s order, as Manu says in X. 55), that they remove the filth of towns etc., stay near cemeteries, have to dispose of corpses of men that leave no relatives, have to act as hangmen for offenders sentenced to death and to take their clothes etc., have to take food in broken pots and to eat dogs’ flesh and to deal in hides and armour (or in armour made of hides). The {{BhagavadgÄ«tÄ}} (5. 18) puts him on the same level with dogs. In the {{MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a}} (8. 81-83, 86, 96) a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} is called {{Å›vapÄka}} (i. e. no distinction is made between the two). In the {{JÄtiviveka}} he is identified with the {{MahÄr}} and with the {{MÄá¹…g}} of the Deccan.

{{SÄtvata}}. According to Manu X. 23 he is the same as {{kÄruá¹£a}} above.

{{SudhanvÄcÄrya}}. According to Manu X. 23 he is the same as {{kÄruá¹£a}} above.

{{Suvará¹‡a}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (vv. 24-25) he is the offspring of the legal union with Vedic mantras of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} male and a {{ká¹£atriya}} female. It appears likely that the text is corrupt and we should read {{savará¹‡a}} for {{suvará¹‡a}}. He should perform rites prescribed by the Atharvaveda, he should by the king’s order ride a horse, elephant or chariot, may act as the commander of an army or may practise as a physician.

{{Suvará¹‡akÄra}} or {{Sauvará¹‡ika}} or {{HemakÄra}} (goldsmith). A {{hiraá¹‡yakÄra}} is mentioned in the {{VÄj. S.}} XXX. 17 and in {{Tait. Br.}} III. 4. 14. According to {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} X. 4 and {{NÄrada}} ({{á¹›á¹‡ÄdÄna}} 274) a goldsmith or a dealer in bronze or a {{bania}} was to examine the balance in the balance ordeal. Manu IV. 215, {{YÄj.}} I. 163, {{Åšaá¹…kha}} and {{Sumantu}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (pp. 1175, 1176) treat him on a level with {{karmakÄra}} (blacksmith) and {{niá¹£Äda}}. Manu IX. 292 condemns him as the worst of all rogues ({{sarvakaá¹‡á¹­akapÄpiá¹£á¹­ha}}). For the view of {{Vaikh.}} and {{Bá¹›haspati}} vide under {{taká¹£an}} above (note 189). In the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} it is said that after {{ParaÅ›urÄma}}’s alleged extermination of the {{ká¹£atriyas}}, some of them that escaped resorted to the castes of ironsmiths and goldsmiths.[^205]

{{SÅ«caka}}. According to {{UÅ›anas}} (v. 43) he is an {{anuloma}} born of the marriage of a {{vaiÅ›ya}} male from a {{Å›Å«dra}} female.

{{SÅ«cika}} or {{saucika}} or {{sÅ«cÄ«}}-one who works with a needle, a tailor. According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 15) and {{UÅ›anas}} (v. 22) he is a {{pratiloma}}, offspring of a {{vaidehaka}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman and engages in the work of sewing with a needle. {{Saucika}}, according to the lexicon of Amara, is the same as {{tunnavÄya}} (for which see above p. 83) and the {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} quoted by {{AparÄrka}} (p. 1178) also equates {{sÅ«cÄ«}} with {{tunnavÄya}}.

{{SÅ«ta}}. For vedic references see above p. 43. According to Gaut. (IV. 15), {{Baud. Dh. S.}} (I. 9. 9), {{Vas.}} (18.6), {{Kauá¹­.}} (III. 7), Manu (X. 11), {{NÄrada}} ({{strÄ«puá¹sa}} 110), {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (16.6), {{YÄj.}} (I. 93) and {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}}, he is a {{pratiloma}} sprung from a {{ká¹£atriya}} male and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. {{Kauá¹­.}} is careful to add that the {{sÅ«ta}} who figures in the {{purÄá¹‡as}} as the reciter is quite different from this. A {{vÄrtika}} on {{PÄá¹‡.}} (VI. 3. 70) teaches the formation of words like {{sÅ«taputrÄ«}}.[^206] According to Manu (X. 47) and {{Viá¹£á¹‡u}} the avocation of {{sÅ«tas}} is driving a chariot (i. e. breaking and yoking horses). According to {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) he makes his livelihood by reminding the king of his duties and by cooking food for him, {{UÅ›anas}} (v. 3) also says that his business is to remind (a king) of his duties. According to the {{Kará¹‡aparva}} (32. 48) {{sÅ«tas}} are the attendants of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} and {{ká¹£atriyas}} and the latter need not carry out what the {{sÅ«ta}} says. According to the {{VÄyupurÄá¹‡a}} (vol. I. 1. 33-38 and vol. II. 1. 139) the {{sÅ«ta}} was to preserve the pedigrees of kings and great men and traditions about learning or books, he is not authorised to study the Veda, the middling way of maintenance for him is to depend upon kings and to look after chariots, elephants and horses; an inferior way of maintenance is the practice of medicine. The {{Vaikh.}} (X. 13) and the {{SÅ«tasaá¹hitÄ}} expressly say that the difference between the {{sÅ«ta}} and the {{rathakÄra}} consists in this that the former is the offspring of a marriage, while the latter is the offspring of a clandestine union of a {{ká¹£atriya}} male with a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. The {{SahyÄdrikhaá¹‡á¸a}} (26. 53-54) says that he is inferior to {{ká¹£atriyas}}, but is entitled to do all the work of a {{ká¹£atriya}} and that he also performs the work of a charioteer, of taming elephants and riding horses.

{{SÅ«nika}} or {{Saunika}} (a butcher). According to {{UÅ›anas}} (v. 14) he is the offspring of an {{Äyogava}} from a {{ká¹£atriya}} woman. {{HÄrÄ«ta}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 279) speaks of him in the same breath with {{rajaka}} and {{carmakÄra}}. The {{BrahmapurÄá¹‡a}} (quoted by {{AparÄrka}} p. 1177) says that he is ‘{{paÅ›umÄraka}}’. {{Sumantu}}[^207] quoted by {{Par. M.}} makes his food unfit for {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}. He is the same as ‘{{khÄá¹­ika}}’ according to the {{JÄtiviveka}}.

{{Sairindhra}}. According to Manu X. 32 he is the offspring of a {{dasyu}} (as defined in Manu X. 45) from an {{Äyogava}} woman and he makes his living by combing the hair (of men and women), he is not to be treated as a {{dÄsa}} (i. e. he has not to eat {{ucchiá¹£á¹­a}} food), but is to perform menial work (such as shampooing the body) or is to subsist by catching deer etc. The word is included in the {{gaá¹‡a kulÄlÄdi}} ({{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 3. 118). From the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} we see what duties {{DraupadÄ«}} disguised as {{sairandhrÄ«}} had to do for the queen of {{VirÄá¹­a}} ({{VirÄá¹­aparva}} 9. 18-19), viz. combing and arranging the hair, pounding unguents, making garlands. Similarly {{DamayantÄ«}} became a {{sairandhrÄ«}} to the mother of the Cedi king ({{Vanaparva}} 65. 68-70), but she refused to eat {{ucchiá¹£á¹­a}} food, would not wash the feet of anybody and would not allow any man to approach her. According to {{Ä€dipurÄá¹‡a}} quoted in {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} he lives by hunting deer and guarding royal harems and women after delivery.

{{SopÄka}}. According to Manu (X. 38) he is the offspring of a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} male and a {{pukkasa}} female, subsists by the profession of being hangman to those whom the king condemns to death.

{{Saudhanvana}}. Vide note on ‘{{rathakÄra}}’ above (45). Vide {{KÄmasÅ«tra}} I. 5. 37.

It will have been noticed that some of the castes mentioned in the {{smá¹›tis}} such as {{ambaá¹£á¹­ha}}, {{mÄgadha}}, {{malla}} and {{vaidehaka}} are connected with countries ({{Amba}}, {{Magadha}}, {{Videha}} etc.), that some others are based upon race such as {{Ä€bhÄ«ra}}, {{KirÄta}} and {{Åšaka}}. Manu (X. 43-45) and the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} ({{AnuÅ›Äsana parva}} 33. 21-23 and 35. 17-18) were prepared to admit that several foreign races like the {{Åšakas}}, {{Yavanas}}, {{KÄmbojas}}, {{Draviá¸as}}, {{Daradas}}, {{Åšabaras}}, {{KirÄtas}} etc. were originally {{ká¹£atriyas}}, but had been reduced to the status of {{Å›Å«dras}} by losing contact with {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} or by not liking the idea of being subject to the {{brÄhmaá¹‡ical}} system. The {{Viá¹£á¹‡upurÄá¹‡a}} (IV. 4. 47-48) says the same. Numerous names of castes arise from the professions they follow, e. g. {{ayaskÄra}}, {{kumbhakÄra}}, {{carmakÄra}}, {{taká¹£an}}, {{tailika}}, {{naá¹­a}}, {{rathakÄra}}, {{veá¹‡a}} etc. Even in ancient times {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} followed so many different occupations almost as in modern times that the list of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} who cannot be invited at {{Å›rÄddhas}} because they follow occupations other than those prescribed for them is rather very formidable (vide Manu III. 151 ff. and the remarks on {{paá¹…ktipÄvana}} later on).

It appears that comparatively very early many among the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} had given up the occupations peculiar to them and were entitled to be called {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} simply because of their birth. The {{MahÄbhÄá¹£ya}}[^208] of {{PataÃ±jali}} quotes a verse ‘{{tapas}}, vedic study, and birth (from {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} parents) these are the causes why a person is called a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}; he who is devoid of {{tapas}} and vedic study is a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} only by birth (and not a real {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}})’. Similarly in another place {{PataÃ±jali}} quotes a verse ‘know this to be the auspicious sign of the best of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, viz. whose lore, actions and birth are all three holy’ (vol. II. p. 220 on {{PÄá¹‡.}} IV. 1. 44). Though in the {{MahÄbhÄrata}} it is often said that a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is so by birth alone and that he deserves respect from all, still we meet several times with passages[^209] wherein there is a revolt against the caste system dependent on birth alone and where it is severely condemned and great emphasis is laid on the moral worth of a man. In the {{Vanaparva}} (181. 42-43) we are told[^210] ‘Truthfulness, restraint, {{tapas}}, generosity, non-injury to sentient beings, constant adherence to {{dharma}}-these always lead men to the fruition (of their goal) and not caste nor family’. {{Udyogaparva}} (43. 20 ff.) explains at great length what is meant by self-restraint. In the {{ÅšÄntiparva}}[^211] (189. 4 and 8) ‘Truthfulness, generosity, freedom from hatred and wickedness, humility, kindness, and {{tapas}}–he is known as a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} where these are seen. If these signs are seen in a {{Å›Å«dra}} and they do not exist in a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}, then the {{Å›Å«dra}} would not be a {{Å›Å«dra}} and the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} would not be a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}’. In another place we are told[^212] ’there is no difference between the {{vará¹‡as}}; the whole world is {{brÄhma}} (the creation of {{BrahmÄ}}), since it was created by {{BrahmÄ}} in former ages and was evolved into {{vará¹‡as}} by actions (or occupations).’ {{Udyogaparva}}[^213] (43. 49) remarks ‘Do not regard a person as {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} by his talk; he who does not depart from truth is a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}}’; {{Vanaparva}} (216. 14-15) ’that {{Å›Å«dra}} who is always struggling for self-restraint, truthfulness and {{dharma}} is a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} in my opinion, for a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} is so by his character.’ Vide {{Vanaparva}} (313. 108-111) and {{YÄj.}} I. 200 and {{Vá¹›ddha Gautama}} p. 632. It has been seen above (p. 6) that Gautama laid the greatest emphasis on the eight qualities of the soul. But in spite of those sentiments the caste system dependent on birth has continued in all its strength and rigour for ages and writers like the {{Par. M.}} II. 1, p. 228 are emphatic in saying that between {{jÄti}} and character one must look principally to the eminence of the caste.

In certain medieval works called {{JÄtiviveka}} and in other works like the {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}} (first half of 17th century A. D.) several more castes are mentioned, some of which are set out below.

{{Ä€ghÅ«sika}} or {{Andhasika}}–from a {{vaidehika}} male and a {{Å›Å«dra}} female; sells cooked food; also called {{Randhavanu}}. {{Avartaka}}–from a {{bhá¹›jjakaá¹‡á¹­ha}} and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. {{Ä€hiá¹‡á¸ika}}–from a {{niá¹£Äda}} and a {{vaideha}} female; called {{GÄruá¸Ä«}} in Marathi. {{Aurabhra}}–called {{Dhaá¹…gar}} in Marathi (tends rams and ewes for their wool). {{Kaá¹­adhÅ«naka}}–from {{Ävartaka}} and a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} woman. {{Kuntalaka}}–the same as {{nÄpita}}. {{Kuruvinda}}–from a {{kumbhakÄra}} and a {{kukkuá¹­a}} female. Same as modern {{sÄlÄ«}}, according to {{ÅšÅ«drakamalÄkara}}. {{Gholika}}–from a {{VyÄdha}} male and {{gÄruá¸Ä«}} female. He is called {{UndiramÄru}} (rat-killer) in the vernacular. {{Durbhara}}â€”from an {{Äyogava}} and a {{dhigvaá¹‡a}} woman; called {{Dohor}} (or {{Dhor}}) in modern times. {{Paustika}}–from a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a}} and a {{niá¹£Äda}} female; called in modern times {{KahÄra}} or {{BhoÄ«}} (palanquin-bearer). {{Plava}}–from a {{cÄá¹‡á¸Äla}} and an {{Ändhra}} woman; called ‘{{bÄdÄ«}}’ in modern times. {{Bandhula}}–from {{maitreya}} and {{jÄá¹…ghika}} female; called {{JhÄrekarÄ«}} now (who takes out gold particles from the dust at the doors of goldsmiths). {{BhasmÄá¹…kura}}–from a {{Åšaiva}} fallen ascetic and a {{Å›Å«dra}} prostitute; called {{Gurava}} by the {{JÄtiviveka}}. {{Manyu}}–from a {{vaiÅ›ya}} and a {{ká¹£atriya}} female; called {{TÄvaá¸ia}} (thief catcher). {{Romika}}–from {{malla}} and an {{avartaka}} woman; called {{Loá¹‡Är}} in modern times (manufacturer of salt). [[102]] {{ÅšÄlakya}} or {{ÅšÄkalya}}–from a {{mÄlÄkÄra}} and {{kÄyastha}} woman; called {{Maniar}}. {{Åšuddha-MÄrjaka}}–called {{Maá¹‡á¸alÄ«}} (who gains livelihood by singing and playing on musical instruments). {{Sindolaka}} or {{spandÄlika}}, from a {{Å›Å«dra}} male and a {{mÄgadha}} female; called {{Raá¹…gÄrÄ«}} (dyer).

Varna and modern castes

In modern times each of the principal {{vará¹‡as}} has numerous {{Bub-castes|sub-castes}}, based upon difference of country, occupation, sect and other causes. For example, {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} are first divided into ten classes, five of them being {{Gauá¸as}}131 and five {{DrÄvidas}}. Among the {{DrÄviá¸a brÄhmaá¹‡as}}, the {{MahÄrÄá¹£á¹­ra}} {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} are again subdivided into numerous sub-castes such as the {{CitpÄvana}} (or {{Koá¹…kanastha}}), {{KarhÄá¸e}}, {{DeÅ›astha}}, {{Devarukhe}} {{sto|etc.}} It is said that in {{Gujerat|Gujarat}} there are 84 subcastes of {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}},132 {{as|and}} most of which do not interdine nor intermarry. There is often a further distinction among the members of the same subcaste, viz, those who follow the priestly occupation and those who do not. The spirit of exclusiveness and ideas of superiority that arose in {{vedio|Vedic}} times gradually led on to further and further {{divisons|divisions}} and subdivisions of people owing to geographical situation and other {{causes}}. It appears that probably even in ancient times the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} of the north looked down upon the {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} in eastern countries like {{Magadha}}. Vide notes 31-32 above and Fick (pp. 213-214). The {{MatsyapurÄá¹‡a}} (16. 16) says that {{brÄhmaá¹‡as}} who dwell in {{Mleccha}} countries, in {{TriÅ›aá¹…ku}}, {{Barbara}}, {{Oá¸ra}} (Orissa), {{Ä€ndhra}} ({{Teliá¹…gÄna}}), {{á¹¬akka}}, {{Draviá¸a}} and {{Koá¹…kaá¹‡a}} are not to be invited at {{Å›rÄddhas}}.133 [[103]]

Among {{ká¹£atriyas}} there are several subdivisions, such as those claiming descent from the Sun or the Moon and those that call themselves to be {{Agnikulas}} (vide Sherring, vol. I. p. 120 ff, and Tod’s Rajasthan vol. I chapter VII for lists of royal tribes). The {{ParamÄras}} have 35 branches, {{Guhilots}} 24 branches, {{Chabmanas|ChahamÄnas}} 26 branches, {{Solankis|Solaá¹…kis}} 16 branches and so on.

Even among comparatively late works the total number of subcastes enumerated does not go beyond even two hundred. For example, Wilson in ‘Indian castes’ vol. I pp. 65-70 mentions only 134 castes with their Sanskrit and modern Marathi names as gathered from the {{JÄtiviveka}}, {{MÄdhava-kalpalatÄ}}, {{Paragu. rÄma-pratÄpa|ParaÅ›urÄma-pratÄpa}} and other works. It is only in the census reports prepared at great expense by the British Government after elaborate efforts and organization that the great complexity of the caste system in modern India is laid bare to the eye. But this work cannot, by reason of its limited scope, go into these varied and complex details of the hundreds of castes that exist in the several provinces of India. [[104]]

  2. E. g. Sherring in his ‘Hindu Tribes and castes’ vol. III p. 274 {{qaya|says}} ‘It has been said with some truth that caste promotes {{oleanliness|cleanliness}} and order and is in a certain sense a bond of union among all classes of the Hindu community. Yet {{gurely|surely}} these ends might have been attained in a simpler manner and by a {{loss|less}} antagonistic process. The invention of a project so wonderfully elaborate and intricateâ€” a project of bringing into absolute subjection two hundred millions of the human species by robbing them of their independence, … the invention of a project like this, so prodigious and far-reaching was not needed to accomplish such useful and beneficent ends. That another and very different object was in view from the very first is abundantly manifest. This object was neither more nor less than to exalt the BrÄhmaá¹‡a, to feed his pride and to minister to his self-will.’ Equally sweeping condemnation can be and has been indulged in as regards feudalism and modern capitalism. Fick (p. 331) entirely scouts the idea that the theory of castes was invented by the priests. There are several fallacies lurking in the above quoted passage of Sherring. In the first place, there is nothing to show that the caste system was {{inyonted|invented}} by any body of persons who could impose their will on a continent. The system simply grew up in the lapse of ages. The population of Hindus when Sherring wrote may have been near two hundred millions, but it could not have been more than a small fraction of that colossal number during the thousands of years that the system has flourished. Besides writers like Sherring pass over the great achievements of Indians under the caste system in Literature, religion and philosophy, in handicrafts and in the fine arts and unduly exaggerate the defects of the system that have become glaring only in the {{maobina-made|machine-made}} civilization of the 19th and 20th centuries. These critics ignore the great adaptability of the system, whereby it preserved Indian society from social anarchy during ages of foreign invasions and internecine wars. While severely condemning the brÄhmaá¹‡as the critics altogether forget that the vast and varied Sanskrit Literature owes its production and preservation mostly to the sacrifice of the brÄhmaá¹‡as for ages. Under the caste system, no man was allowed to be useless to the commonwealth and his conduct was a question of honour with his group. When all work was turned out with the hands, the caste system tended to preserve and augment the skill of artisans. Moreover what social organization is to be substituted and how is not made clear by these critics. Most of those critics have the western social system based on wealth and the industrial revolution in view; but that system also is as evil as or perhaps worse than the modern caste system. ↩︎ ↩︎

IV. 18.}} In the Tai. S. II. 5. 11. 2 we read {{à¤¯à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¶à¥à¤šà¤¾à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¶à¥à¤š à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¶à¥à¤¨à¤®à¥‡à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¥à¤°à¥‚à¤¯à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤¨à¥‡ à¤¤à¤¦à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¯à¤¦à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤µà¤¿à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤¨à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤¦à¤ªà¤¾à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¥‹ à¤¨à¤¾à¤ªà¤¾à¤µà¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤ƒ à¥¤|à¤¯à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¶à¥à¤šà¤¾à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¶à¥à¤š à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¶à¥à¤¨à¤®à¥‡à¤¯à¤¾à¤¤à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¥à¤°à¥‚à¤¯à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤¨à¥‡ à¤¤à¤¦à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¯à¤¦à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤ªà¤¾à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤¨à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¤¦à¤ªà¤¾à¤µà¤•à¥à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¬à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤®à¤£à¥‹ à¤¨à¤¾à¤ªà¤¾à¤µà¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤ƒ à¥¤}} This literally means ‘if a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a non-brÄhmaá¹‡a come (to a man) asking him a question, then he should speak to the brÄhmaá¹‡a first; that he speaks to the brÄhmaá¹‡a first is really tantamount to speaking first to one-self; when he speaks away from the brÄhmaá¹‡a (i. e. he speaks to the non-brÄhmaá¹‡a first and then to the brÄhmaá¹‡a) that is really speaking away to oneself; therefore a brÄhmaá¹‡a should not be spoken away (i. e. postponed to non-brÄhmaá¹‡as).’ Manu VIII. 24 says that a king should take up the causes of litigants in the order of the vará¹‡as (i. e. if there are two plaintiffs coming with complaints at the same time the brÄhmaá¹‡a’s complaint should be first attended to). Bá¹›haspati says the same thing. Therefore the Tai. S. should be interpreted in the same way. Another meaning is possible viz. if a brÄhmaá¹‡a and a non-brÄhmaá¹‡a come to a person and ask him who is superior, the person should declare that the brÄhmaá¹‡a is the superior of the two (on account of his birth as brÄhmaá¹‡a probably or of his being a learned man). This sense of ‘adhi’ as meaning ‘superior’ is found in Manu I. 99 ‘since brÄhmaá¹‡a when born becomes (or is born) superior (to all) on this wide earth.’ Prof. Keith in his translation of Tai. S. (Harvard Oriental Series, vol. 18 p. 203) says in a footnote â€œit is not absolutely certain that adhi-brÅ« means ‘decide in favour of’ rather than ‘speak in favour of’.” Vide also Vedic Index (II. p. 83). Dr. Ghurye (in ‘Caste and race in India’ p. 43) says ‘in a legal dispute between a brÄhmaá¹‡a or non-brÄhmaá¹‡a an arbitrator or witness must speak in favour of the former.’ It appears that Dr. Ghurye simply follows the rather guardedly expressed view of the famous Professor, but makes the sense more emphatic than Prof. Keith puts it and does not think for himself whether any other meaning is possible or more appropriate. Any stick is good for beating the brÄhmaá¹‡a with. The brÄhmaá¹‡as were never ashamed in the smá¹›ti texts of declaring the privileges they claimed. But they never claimed to be treated in a court of law as above truth and justice. If they had taken the Tai. S. passage in the sense in which Dr. Ghurye takes it they would never have scrupled to say so in smá¹›ti works and would have quoted the Tai. S. in support. Hence the meaning is different. There is no question here of an Arbitration or judicial decision. In á¹šg. I. 100. 19 there is a similar expression ‘May Indra speak in our favour on all days’ ({{à¤‡à¤¨à¥à¤¦à¥à¤°à¥‹ à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤§à¤¿ à¤¶à¥à¤°à¥à¤§à¤¿|à¤‡à¤¨à¥à¤¦à¥à¤°à¥‹ à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¹à¥à¤¯à¤§à¤¿à¤µà¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¾ à¤¨à¥‹ à¤…à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥}}). Vide á¹šg. X. 63. 11 and VÄj. S. 16.5 for the verb ‘vac’ with ‘adhi’ in the sense of ‘speak in favour of’ or ‘bless’. ↩︎ ↩︎

V. 10. 7.}} There were strict rules about giving one’s ucchiá¹£á¹­a to another. Ä€p. Dh. S. (I. 11. 31. 25-26) did not allow a brÄhmaá¹‡a to give his ucchiá¹£á¹­a to one not a brÄhmaá¹‡a, unless certain rather obnoxious things were done to it. Manu IV. 80 forbids the giving of ucchiá¹£á¹­a to a Å›Å«dra (who is not a dependant) while Manu X. 125 allows it to be given to a Å›Å«dra who is a dependant. ↩︎ ↩︎
